<a id="paper-2605.22580"></a>
### [Practical Countermeasure Against Attacks Exploiting Detection Efficiency Mismatch in Quantum Key Distribution](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22580v1)

**Authors:** Ben J. Taylor, Peter R. Smith, James F. Dynes, Robert I. Woodward, Marco Lucamarini, R. Mark Stevenson, Andrew J. Shields  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22580v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22580_figures/2605.22580_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Conceptual schematic of a time-shift attack. HOS: high-speed optical switch; VDL: variable optical delay line. Eve shifts optical pulses generated by Alice in time by +δt1 or −δt2, chosen to maximise mismatch on Bob’s two non-identical detectors, the efficiencies of which vary uniquely in time.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22580_figures/2605.22580_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Key components for experimental characterisation of a fibre-based, GHz-clocked, phase-encoding QKD system’s vulnerability to a time-shift attack. We sweep values of a relative delay, with resolution δt = 4.5 ps, and add this to the global reference clock’s signal, which is then supplied to all active optical components in Bob, whilst recording counts from the APD outputs. LD: laser diode; ϕA, ϕB: phase modulators; Att.: attenuation; λ: narrow-wavelength filter, here at 1550.12 ± 0.04 nm.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22580_figures/2605.22580_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Normalized detection efficiency of APD 0 and APD 1 under time-shift relative to default, with steps of 4.5 ps across a range of 1 ns (full clock cycle), in both cases of (a) two-state and (b) four-state demodulation at Bob. We show bounds at which the QBER exceeded 11% on average, such that the protocol would have aborted outside the white central region. Each data point comprises 10Mb of total counts data.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22580_figures/2605.22580_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Bit-0 normalised detection bias across a 495 ps window, in increments of 4.5 ps. Whilst ideal APDs would exhibit no detection bias, the bias with two-states varies significantly, to a maximum of ≈30%. This data subset corresponds to the central region in Fig. 3, where average QBER was below 11%.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22580_figures/2605.22580_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Asymptotic secret key rates (SKR) in bits per single photon sent against channel loss, accounting for detection efficiency mismatch. The four-state countermeasure recovers 97.1% of the ideal case of no mismatch, whilst the rate is reduced to 38.3% of ideal in the two-states case.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates a practical way to protect high-speed quantum key distribution from attacks that exploit differences in detector efficiency. By using a four-state modulation technique, the researchers were able to almost entirely recover the secret key rate even when the system was under a simulated time-shift attack. This provides a robust, design-level defense for future scalable quantum communication networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information researchers as it addresses the practical implementation of security protocols against hardware-level side-channel attacks in high-speed photonic networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Quantum Key Distribution (QKD) systems are vulnerable to side-channel attacks, specifically the time-shift attack, which exploits detection efficiency mismatches between the receiver's detectors.

**Main result.** The implementation of a four-state countermeasure on a GHz-clocked prototype recovers 97.1% of the ideal secret key rate, effectively mitigating the impact of detection efficiency mismatch.

**Method.** The researchers implemented a 'four-state Bob' setup that expands phase modulation from two to four phases to symmetrize detection events, and simulated attacks by introducing controlled time-delays.

**Model / system.** A GHz-clocked, fiber-based, phase-encoding, decoy-state BB84 QKD prototype using a gain-switched laser diode, asymmetric Mach-Zehnder interferometers, and InGaAs avalanche photodiodes.

**Key observables.** Quantum Bit Error Rate (QBER), Secret Key Rate (SKR), and detection efficiency mismatch between detectors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 1 GHz clock rate, 4.5 ps time-shift resolution, and an 11% QBER threshold.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes detectors have non-identical responses across degrees of freedom (time, frequency, polarization) and that the countermeasure does not introduce new side-channels like Trojan-horse attacks.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the time-shift attack; Figure 2 details the experimental setup for characterizing vulnerability; Figure 3 illustrates the normalized detection efficiency and the effectiveness of the four-state method.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the vulnerability of QKD to time-shift attacks, describes the experimental implementation of the four-state countermeasure on a high-speed system, quantifies the security and performance recovery using numerical bounds, and discusses the broader implications for scalable QKD.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate a practical countermeasure against a well-known class of attacks on quantum key distribution (QKD) systems that exploit detection efficiency mismatch, where the receiver's detectors do not exhibit identical responses to incoming photons across all degrees of freedom. This class of quantum hacking strategies is broad and significantly includes the time-shift attack, which targets an arrival-time-dependent side channel at the receiver. The four-state countermeasure, previously only proven to be secure in theory, is implemented here on a GHz-clocked prototype QKD system and evaluated for its security and performance. We show that its presence enables almost complete recovery of the system's ideal secret key rate. Our results provide strong justification for adopting this countermeasure as a standard component in future scalable and practical QKD systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21662"></a>
### [Fidelity-Aware Frequency Allocation and Transpilation Co-Design for Tunable Coupler Quantum Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21662v1)

**Authors:** Dylan VanAllen, Evan McKinney, Israa G. Yusuf, Girgis Falstin, Gaurav Agarwal, Jason Pollack, Michael Hatridge, Alex K. Jones  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21662v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21662_figures/2605.21662_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. High-level workflow for the co-design of quantum computer architectures [1].</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21662_figures/2605.21662_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. (a) SNAIL-qubit coupling graph (b) 2Q conversion connectivity graph, with unique colored edges for activated exchanges. (c) Compatibility graph, with nodes as interac- tions and edges as interferences.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21662_figures/2605.21662_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Spectral positioning of a SNAIL and 4 qubit bare modes and their interacting resonant frequencies.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21662_figures/2605.21662_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. In this diagram, the central SNAIL is driven with the target interaction denoted by a green edge. Spectator terms diminish based on how many orders of hybridization they are removed from the driven SNAIL. (Blue) Both qubits coupled to the driven SNAIL (in the same module); (Orange) One qubit directly coupled to the driven SNAIL; (Purple) Neither qubit directly coupled to the driven SNAIL.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21662_figures/2605.21662_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Infidelity versus detuning for spectator amplitude with Rabi oscillations compared with spectator amplitude using Rabi magnitude bound.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a co-design strategy that simultaneously optimizes hardware frequency allocation and software circuit transpilation to mitigate errors in tunable-coupler quantum computers. By accounting for hardware-specific noise during the routing process, the proposed FINESSE algorithm significantly improves gate fidelity compared to standard compilers. This approach effectively manages the tension between high connectivity and the detrimental effects of frequency crowding.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics as it provides a rigorous framework for modeling how parasitic, non-local, and dissipative interactions (spectator errors and decoherence) impact the scalability of driven, multi-mode superconducting circuits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental tradeoff between connectivity and gate fidelity in superconducting quantum architectures, specifically how frequency crowding in tunable-coupler systems leads to parasitic interactions and degraded performance.

**Main result.** The authors developed FINESSE, a noise-aware transpilation framework that achieves up to an 8.9% reduction in log-infidelity on SNAIL architectures and a 12.3% reduction on IBM Brisbane, demonstrating that joint optimization of frequency allocation and circuit mapping is superior to standard methods.

**Method.** The study uses an error-budgeting framework to model coherent and incoherent errors, a constrained optimization approach for frequency assignment, and a novel transpilation algorithm (FINESSE) that uses fidelity-weighted distances for SWAP selection.

**Model / system.** The research focuses on superconducting quantum architectures using SNAIL-based tunable couplers and transmon qubits, specifically modeling the 'Corral' module and evaluating performance on the 127-qubit IBM Brisbane heavy-hex processor.

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity, circuit depth, log-infidelity cost, and connectivity/edge count.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pump strength (eta), qubit and SNAIL frequencies, detuning (delta), gate duration, and the fidelity-connectivity tradeoff limit (4 qubits per SNAIL).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a weak-coupling approximation where spectator-induced errors are additive and uses a conservative amplitude bound for spectator interaction to avoid reliance on precise Rabi synchronization.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the co-design workflow; Figure 2 details the SNAIL-qubit coupling and compatibility graphs; Figure 3 illustrates spectral positioning; Figure 13 compares log-infidelity across algorithms; Figure 15 shows fidelity degradation with increasing edge count; Figure 16 compares performance on IBM Brisbane.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a co-design logic: first establishing the hardware-level problem of frequency crowding and error modeling, then presenting a frequency allocation optimization, followed by the development of the FINESSE transpilation framework, and concluding with performance benchmarks on both SNAIL and IBM architectures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Frequency crowding is a fundamental limitation in superconducting quantum architectures, particularly in tunable-coupler systems. We present a framework that explicitly models both coherent spectator-induced errors and incoherent lifetime effects through an error budgeting approach. Using this model, we analyze how frequency crowding impacts gate fidelity as module size and connectivity scale, and formulate a constrained optimization problem to assign qubit and coupler frequencies under realistic separation and hardware constraints. We demonstrate scalable frequency allocation strategies that minimize spectator-induced errors. We further show that increasing qubit count and coupling density within a module leads to a fidelity-connectivity tradeoff. To explore the benefits at the system scale, we have developed a noise-aware transpilation approach called FINESSE, which minimizes error by selecting high-fidelity paths that satisfy connectivity via SWAP insertion while jointly optimizing downstream gate execution. We demonstrate this physics-informed architecture-transpilation co-design approach for a SNAIL-based third-order coupler that natively realizes the $\sqrt{iSWAP}$ basis with frequency aware gate fidelities. On SNAIL architectures, FINESSE achieves an average 8.9% reduction in log-infidelity cost and 6.8% reduction in circuit depth vs. SABRE. We also compare results on IBM Brisbane's architecture.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22583"></a>
### [Optimal work extraction in measurement-based quantum Otto engines: Non-adiabaticity and generalized measurements can be beneficial](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22583v1)

**Authors:** Arunabha Das, Sayan Mondal, Debarupa Saha, Ujjwal Sen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22583v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22583_figures/2605.22583_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Schematic diagram of the measurement-based quan- tum Otto engine. The measurement-based quantum Otto engine consists of four strokes. In Stroke-I, the working medium thermal- izes with a cold bath at temperature Tc. Stroke-II and Stroke-IV are unitary driving strokes, generated through adiabatic or non-adiabatic driving of the Hamiltonian. Since these strokes are entropy conserv- ing, they correspond to the work strokes of the cycle. Stroke-III is the measurement stroke, which replaces the hot-bath thermalization pro- cess present in the conventional Otto engine, where the system would otherwise interact with a bath at temperature Th &gt; Tc. The measure- ment can be either PVM or...</sub>

</details>

<sub>Figure 2. Extractable work: comparison between the PVM-based and conventional two-bath Otto engines. The dashed turquoise curve denotes the maximum extractable work from the PVM-based Otto engine, WΠ M, obtained after optimization over all measurement settings. For comparison, the work extracted from a conventional two-bath Otto engine is also shown for two hot-bath inverse temperatures: βh = 0.2 (dot-dashed blue) and βh = 0 (solid orange), represented by [WC]βh=0.2 and [WC]βh=0, respectively. Panels (a) and (b) correspond to two different...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22583_figures/2605.22583_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Extractable work: comparison between the PVM-based and POVM-based Otto engines. The non-adiabatic work output, W, is plotted as a function of the non-adiabaticity parameter P, where P = 1 corresponds to the adiabatic limit. The turquoise dashed curve denotes the maximum extractable work from the POVM-based Otto engine, WP M, obtained after optimization over all measurement settings. At the adiabatic limit, i.e., P = 1, this curve touches with the analytically derived value, i.e., W P M, represented by the yellow-colored dotted horizontal line. The lower bound on the net work extractable from the optimal POVM-based Otto engine, after accounting for the auxiliary reset cost, is...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22583_figures/2605.22583_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Comparison between the advantage of the POVM-based engine over the PVM-based engine and the corresponding aux- iliary reset cost. The enhancement in work extraction offered by the POVM-based engine over the PVM-based engine is quantified by the difference between their respective optimal work outputs, ∆W PΠ [Eq. **Summary.** This paper explores how replacing a thermal reservoir with quantum measurements can optimize a quantum Otto engine. It finds that generalized measurements (POVMs) and non-adiabatic processes can increase work extraction and efficiency, even when the energy cost of resetting the measurement apparatus is considered.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides fundamental insights into the thermodynamics of measurement-driven processes and demonstrates how controlling non-adiabaticity and measurement type can be used to optimize quantum heat engines.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the optimal work extraction and efficiency of measurement-based quantum Otto engines, specifically comparing PVM and POVM implementations under adiabatic and non-adiabatic regimes.

**Main result.** POVM-based engines can outperform PVM-based engines even after accounting for the thermodynamic cost of resetting the auxiliary system, and non-adiabaticity can enhance both work output and efficiency in certain compression regimes.

**Method.** Analytical optimization of measurement parameters and unitary operators, combined with numerical optimization using the dual annealing algorithm and Naimark's dilation theorem.

**Model / system.** A single-qubit working substance undergoing a four-stroke Otto cycle, where the hot-bath thermalization is replaced by quantum measurements (PVM or POVM) using an auxiliary system.

**Key observables.** Extractable work, efficiency, heat exchange, and net work output after accounting for auxiliary reset costs.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Compression ratio (frequency ratio), non-adiabaticity parameter (transition probability), cold bath temperature, and measurement basis angles.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the non-inverted population regime and focuses on two-outcome POVMs implemented via a single auxiliary qubit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the schematic of the measurement-based Otto cycle; Figure 2 plots extractable work versus non-adiabaticity; Figure 4 illustrates the trade-off between POVM work gain and auxiliary reset cost as a function of temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the measurement-based engine model, analyzes the adiabatic limit for PVM and POVM, extends the analysis to the non-adiabatic regime, evaluates the thermodynamic cost of POVM implementation, and concludes with operational guidelines.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Measurement-based quantum heat engines have attracted significant interest as alternatives to conventional thermal engines, as they replace the hot thermal reservoir with quantum measurements, thereby offering greater controllability and simpler implementation. Motivated by these advantages, we investigate a measurement-driven quantum Otto engine with a qubit working substance and study the optimal work extractable from such engines, including whether their performance can surpass that of conventional quantum Otto cycles. We analyze the engine in both the infinite-time (adiabatic) and finite-time (non-adiabatic) regimes, considering two distinct implementations obtained through optimization over all projection-valued measurements (PVMs) and over all two-outcome positive operator-valued measurements (POVMs). We show that measurement-based engines can outperform conventional quantum Otto engines within specific parameter regimes and that POVM-based engines can yield higher optimal work extraction than PVM-based ones. Furthermore, by incorporating the thermodynamic cost associated with resetting the auxiliary system required for POVM implementation, we demonstrate that the resulting net work output can still exceed that of PVM-based engines under suitable conditions on the spectral gaps and cold bath temperature. We also identify regimes in which non-adiabatic implementations can yield higher work output and efficiency than their adiabatic counterparts. Our study provides operational guidelines for designing improved measurement-driven quantum Otto engines.

</details>

### [Realizing tunable non-Hermitian skin effects in dynamical quantum systems via the relative phase between multiple time-periodic driving](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21959v1)

**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21959v1>

**Summary.** This paper shows that the relative phase between different periodic drives can be used to turn the non-Hermitian skin effect on or off. It also demonstrates that this phase can control the direction in which particles accumulate at the boundaries in 2D systems. This discovery offers a new way to engineer skin density profiles in platforms like optical lattices or circuit lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel mechanism for Floquet engineering of non-Hermitian topology, offering a way to dynamically tune the spatial distribution of modes in open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Controlling the emergence and the localization direction of the non-Hermitian skin effect (NHSE) in dynamical quantum systems.

**Main result.** The relative phase between multiple time-periodic driving components can act as a switch to activate or deactivate NHSE and can alter the spatial direction of skin mode localization in higher dimensions.

**Method.** Floquet theory and Magnus expansion are used to analyze the long-time averaged effective Hamiltonian and the winding number topological invariant.

**Model / system.** Non-Hermitian quantum chains and 2D lattices (such as the NH-QWZ model) subject to multiple time-periodic driving (Floquet systems) with non-reciprocal tunneling.

**Key observables.** Winding number (C), Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR), and skin density profile.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Relative phase (phi) between driving components, driving frequency (omega), and tunneling amplitudes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** High-frequency limit (omega >> Hamiltonian scales) for the Magnus expansion; focus on PT-symmetric systems.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 demonstrates the switching of NHSE on and off by tuning the relative phase and shows the corresponding change in the winding number.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of NHSE control, presents a 1D model demonstrating phase-induced switching, extends the analysis to 2D systems for directional control, and discusses potential experimental realizations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate how the relative phase between the multiple time periodic driving can decide the emergence and the favorable localization direction of non-Hermitian skin modes. For the static non-Hermitian quantum chain with parity time symmetry, non-Hermitian skin effects (NHSEs) can be prohibited. As the dynamical driving is turned on, NHSEs get artificially reactivated, where the relative phase can serve as the controlling switch by breaking the temporal symmetry constraints. Meanwhile, a change of relative phase can also alter the spatial structures of the long-time averaged effective Hamiltonian, which will consequently lead to the variation of skin localization direction for systems of higher dimensions. Our formalisms can be generally realized in diverse optical and mechanical platforms, and will pave the way for realizing tunable skin density profiles.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22603"></a>
### [Sudden death of entanglement, rebirth of magic](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22603v1)

**Authors:** Chenfeng Cao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22603v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22603_figures/2605.22603_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Trajectory classes and resource phase diagram. (a) Schematic stabilizer-polytope trajectories under local amplitude damping, all ending at the stabilizer vertex |0n⟩. Red denotes magic death and rebirth, equivalently entry into and exit from S, realized by the GHZ-X family solved in the main text and by an exact non- GHZ-X affine-plane slice in the SM. Blue denotes finite death with- out rebirth, realized by anti-W edges D2 3 and D3 4 . Gray denotes endpoint-only trajectories, with affine examples given by nonstabi- lizer real generalized-W inputs and curved examples such as D2 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22603_figures/2605.22603_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Magic–entanglement complementarity and Bell-state splitting. (a) Family-A trajectory in Regime II (α = 0.40). Magic (blue) dies at γ(2) − and is reborn at γ(2) + , after entanglement (red) vanishes at γ(2) e . Gray shading marks the stabilizer window. (b) Trajectory-level complementarity for the same parameters as in panel (a): RA(γ) −1 (blue line) coincides with the damping- reflected concurrence 1−γ</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22603_figures/2605.22603_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Single-qubit extraction and distillation windows. De- coded trajectories ˜ρ(γ) at α = 0.20 for n = 2, 4, 6 in the Bloch XZ plane. The rotated square is the stabilizer octahedron O. Each trajec- tory enters O at γ(n) − and exits at γ(n) + (labels shown for n = 4). Pink regions mark Bravyi–Kitaev sufficient distillation windows [14]: |H⟩-type x + |z| ≳1.015 (light), and after Clifford twirling |T⟩- type x + |z| &gt; 3/ √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22603_figures/2605.22603_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Super-exponential decay of the stabilizer window. Exact window width ∆n = γ(n) + −γ(n) − (solid lines with markers) against the analytical upper bound (C1) (dotted) for three initial amplitudes spanning the re-entrant regime. At all three amplitudes ∆n decays faster than any exponential in n, and the bound saturates the exact width within ∼10% for n ≳6.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that local amplitude damping can cause the 'sudden death' and subsequent 'rebirth' of magic in quantum states, even after entanglement has been lost. It reveals a fundamental symmetry between entanglement death and magic rebirth and provides a classification of stabilizer states based on their ability to generate magic under noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insights into the resource theory of magic and the non-intuitive behavior of quantum resources in open quantum systems, specifically how dissipation can act as a resource for generating non-stabilizer states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the dynamics of entanglement and magic (non-stabilizerness) under local Markovian amplitude damping, specifically focusing on the phenomenon of magic rebirth after entanglement death.

**Main result.** The paper establishes that while entanglement is irreversibly lost under amplitude damping, magic can be 'reborn' after an initial period of disappearance, following a complementarity relation gamma_e + gamma_+ = 1. It also classifies stabilizer states into magic-generators and magic-insulators based on their Hamming-weight support.

**Method.** Analytical approach using Kraus operators, Stinespring dilation, and the stabilizer polytope framework, combined with mathematical tools like partial transposition and combinatorial enumeration.

**Model / system.** n-qubit systems, specifically the GHZ-type family and Bell states, subject to local Markovian amplitude damping channels.

**Key observables.** Magic robustness (R), concurrence (C), entanglement negativity, and stabilizer polytope membership.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Damping strength (gamma), number of qubits (n), initial state amplitude ratio (alpha/beta), and phase-twist angle (phi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The noise model is restricted to local Markovian amplitude damping (non-unital) and certain phase-covariant channels.

**Figures summary.** Schematic of trajectories in the stabilizer polytope; plots of magic-entanglement complementarity; visualization of the super-exponential decay of the stabilizer window width; and threshold deformation as a function of phase.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of magic vs. entanglement dynamics, analyzes the GHZ-X manifold under amplitude damping, establishes the complementarity relation, classifies stabilizer states into generators and insulators, and explores the asymptotic behavior and phase-twist effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Local Markovian noise cannot bring entanglement back, but it can bring magic back. Unlike separability, stabilizer membership is not preserved by local channels, allowing dissipation to push states out of the stabilizer polytope as well as in. Under local amplitude damping, the $n$-qubit GHZ family $α|0^n\rangle+β|1^n\rangle$ ($0<α<β$) loses its magic at a lower damping strength $γ_-$ and regains it at a higher one $γ_+$, while entanglement is irreversibly lost at $γ_e$. This magic-entanglement complementarity, $γ_e+γ_+=1$ for every $n$, reflects a system-environment duality of amplitude damping and persists for a broader class of dissipative channels. For small $α$, the reborn magic resides in a fully separable state with all proper marginals stabilizer, yet parity-syndrome extraction concentrates it onto a single qubit for magic-state distillation. Local dissipation further divides pure stabilizer states into magic-generators and magic-insulators: at two qubits, the Bell state $|Φ^+\rangle$ generates magic immediately, while its Bell-state partner $|Ψ^+\rangle$ remains stabilizer. Together, magic and entanglement reveal a symmetry invisible to either alone.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22097"></a>
### [Q-PhotoNAS: Hybrid Quantum Neural Architecture Search Framework on Photonic Devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22097v1)

**Authors:** Farah Elnakhal, Alberto Marchisio, Nouhaila Innan, Gabriel Falcao, Muhammad Shafique  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22097v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22097_figures/2605.22097_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Motivation for automated architecture search in hybrid photonic QML. Both Digits (top) and MNIST (bottom) require four successive hand-designed attempts before reaching competitive accuracy. Each attempt is architecturally distinct from the last and requires its own training run.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22097_figures/2605.22097_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Q-PhotoNAS framework overview. Classical preprocessing (PCA + Conv2d) fuses inputs into z ∈Rd, which passes through a pre-quantum MLP and learnable phase encoder (θi = actϕ(xisi + bi) · π) before entering the photonic quantum layer (Merlin ML.QuantumLayer.simple [39]). A classical classifier head maps the Fock-basis output q to class probabilities. A genetic algorithm (pop=20, 30 generations) searches 19 hyperparameters across ∼3.7 × 1010 configurations, optimizing proxy validation accuracy. Hardware execution time is estimated via a first-principles model with Monte Carlo uncertainty quantification on the Quandela Ascella QPU.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22097_figures/2605.22097_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: Sample PhotonicModel architecture: full forward pass from raw image x ∈RH×W to class prediction. Two parallel branches: (1) Conv Frontend with L blocks (Conv2d+ReLU+BN, base channels C), adaptive average pooling, linear projection to d; (2) StandardScaler+PCA compresses flattened image to d then BN1d. Concatenate and fuse via Linear(2d →d)+SiLU to z ∈Rd. Pre-Q MLP (depth p, width w, activation act) maps z higher. Phase Enc.: maps each xi to phase θi ∈(0, π) via trainable scale si, bias bi, activation actϕ ∈{σ, tanh, clamp}. Merlin QL is ML.QuantumLayer.simple [39] photonic circuit with M modes, n photons: encoding applies θ phases, beam splitter mixes modes, PS (ϕ) applies trainable...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22097_figures/2605.22097_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 7: GA-based architecture search methodology. From dataset and search space through population evolution to the best architecture and analysis.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22097_figures/2605.22097_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 8: Group-based crossover example. Each group is chosen independently, giving 64 possible combinations per crossover.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces Q-PhotoNAS, an automated framework for searching the optimal architecture of hybrid photonic quantum-classical neural networks. By using a genetic algorithm to jointly optimize classical and quantum components, the authors achieve state-of-the-art accuracy on image classification tasks. The study also quantifies the hardware-specific latency and the unique feature-extraction capabilities of the photonic layer.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents an automated approach to optimizing variational quantum circuits on specific photonic hardware, specifically addressing the synergy between classical preprocessing and quantum phase encoding.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Designing effective hybrid photonic quantum-classical architectures is challenging due to a massive, interdependent design space involving classical preprocessing, quantum phase encoding, and photonic circuit structure.

**Main result.** The Q-PhotoNAS framework achieves high validation accuracies (99.44% on Digits, 99.78% on MNIST) and demonstrates that the photonic layer extracts non-redundant, complementary features to the classical pathway.

**Method.** A Neural Architecture Search (NAS) framework using a Genetic Algorithm (GA) to evolve 19 hyperparameters across six gene groups, combined with a learnable quantum phase encoding mechanism.

**Model / system.** A hybrid quantum-classical neural network targeting the Quandela Ascella photonic QPU, utilizing a 12-mode silicon nitride chip with thermo-optic phase shifters, directional couplers, and superconducting nanowire single-photon detectors.

**Key observables.** Photon-number probability vectors in the Fock basis, validation accuracy, inter-class cosine similarity, and hardware-estimated inference latency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 19 hyperparameters including network depth/width, phase encoding scale and bias, learning rate, and 12-mode photonic circuit configuration.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The evaluation is primarily simulation-based using the Merlin backend; hardware latency estimates assume specific thermal-optic reconfiguration costs and photon loss rates.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the motivation for NAS via architecture complexity; Figure 2/3/5 illustrate the hybrid model pipeline and flow; Figure 4/7/8/9 detail the Q-PhotoNAS GA framework, including crossover and mutation; Figure 10 shows the hardware latency model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the design challenge, describes the Q-PhotoNAS GA-based framework and learnable encoding, details the experimental setup/simulation, presents performance results on MNIST/Digits, performs quantum contribution analysis, and concludes with hardware latency estimations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Photonic quantum computing is a promising platform for scalable quantum machine learning, but designing effective hybrid architectures remains challenging under hardware and optimization constraints. Existing approaches rely on manually tuned architectures that fail to account for the collaboration between classical preprocessing, phase encoding, and photonic circuit structure, limiting both accuracy and hardware compatibility. In this paper, we propose a neural architecture search framework for hybrid photonic quantum-classical models that combines genetic algorithm-based search with learnable quantum phase encoding to systematically explore the joint design space of classical and quantum components. Our framework encodes 19 hyperparameters across six gene groups and evolves a population of hybrid architectures using group-based crossover, per-gene mutation, and elitism, evaluating each candidate on a short training budget before full retraining of the best found design. We evaluate our framework on two image classification benchmarks, Digits and MNIST, achieving final validation accuracies of 99.44% and 98.78%, respectively, with first-principles execution time estimates on the Quandela Ascella photonic QPU projecting single-image inference at 67 ms (Digits) and 149 ms (MNIST). Our quantum contribution analysis further shows that the photonic layer extracts non-redundant features orthogonal to the classical pathway, providing a measurable accuracy advantage over classical-only baselines. Our results demonstrate that automated architecture search is both practical and impactful for hybrid photonic systems, opening the way for systematic design space exploration of quantum AI on photonic devices.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [QuCtrl-BELL: A Compiler-Driven Sub-Microsecond Feedback Control Stack for Scalable Trapped-Ion Quantum Experiments](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22433v1)

**Authors:** Junpeng She, Ruoyu Yan, Zhizhen Qin, Zhanyu Li, Zhongtao Shen, Zichao Zhou, Binxiang Qi, Luming Duan

**Summary.** The paper presents QuCtrl-BELL, a new compiler-driven software stack designed for scalable trapped-ion quantum control. By separating control logic from hardware parameters, it enables sub-700 ns feedback loops and efficient parameter sweeps. This architecture bridges the gap between user-friendly Python programming and the strict timing requirements of large-scale quantum experiments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for AMO physicists as it provides the necessary classical control infrastructure for implementing complex, real-time feedback protocols essential for scalable trapped-ion gates and quantum error correction.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of scaling trapped-ion quantum computing requires a control system that provides both high-level software programmability and sub-microsecond, deterministic hardware feedback latency.

**Main result.** The QuCtrl-BELL stack achieves sub-700 ns end-to-end feedback latency by decoupling control flow from hardware state data, significantly reducing data transfer and memory pressure.

**Method.** A compiler-driven approach using a Python-embedded DSL that employs a six-stage transpilation pipeline (including CFG, SSA, and register allocation) to separate instruction streams from hardware step-tables.

**Model / system.** A distributed control platform for trapped-ion quantum computing based on a 9-slot PXIe chassis, featuring RISC-V processors, DDS/AWG boards, and a Trigger & Clock Manager (TCM) for star-topology synchronization.

**Key observables.** Feedback latency (measured at 690 ns), execution queue timing resolution (4 ns), and step-table compactness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sub-microsecond feedback threshold (<700 ns), 4 ns clock resolution, and 100 ns hardware broadcast delay.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The 700 ns synchronization overhead per readout event may limit throughput for high-frequency multi-round error correction; camera-based detection is not yet integrated into the compiler.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a standard trapped-ion experiment sequence; Figure 2 depicts the PXIe hardware architecture; Figure 3 and 4 detail the software architecture and compilation pipeline; Figure 5 illustrates the synchronization protocol; Figure 6 provides an oscilloscope trace confirming the 690 ns feedback latency.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the control tradeoff problem, describes the QuCtrl-BELL hardware and software architecture, details the six-stage compilation pipeline, presents performance evaluations regarding latency and memory efficiency, and concludes with future work.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

As trapped-ion quantum computing scales to larger qubit registers and more complex control protocols, classical control systems face a fundamental tradeoff: sub-microsecond board-level feedback requires tight hardware coupling, whereas maintainability and extensibility require clean, modular software abstractions. This paper presents QuCtrl-BELL (Bell), a compiler-driven software stack for trapped-ion quantum control. The design resolves this tradeoff by decoupling control flow -- including loops, branches, and synchronization -- from hardware state data. A Python-embedded domain-specific language (DSL) is lowered through a six-stage transpilation pipeline covering control flow graph (CFG) construction, static single-assignment (SSA) conversion, liveness analysis, and graph-coloring register allocation. The compiler generates deterministic distributed board-level programs and compact step-table data. A cross-board synchronization protocol supports feedback loops with latency below 700~ns without host intervention. Bell is deployed and evaluated on the QuCtrl-BELL platform (RISC-V + PXIe), demonstrating that a compiler-based infrastructure can provide programmability, deterministic timing, and modularity for scalable trapped-ion quantum control.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21664"></a>
### [A journey through Flatland: What does the antiflatness of a spectrum teach us?](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21664v1)

**Authors:** Barbara Jasser, Daniele Iannotti, Alioscia Hamma  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21664v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21664_figures/2605.21664_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: On the left different profiles of the eigenvalues {λi}r i=1 of a given reduced density matrix, wiht λk ≥λj with k &lt; j, showing (from top to bottom) the constant uniform, exponentially decaying and maximum antiflat spectrum respectively. On the right, a non-exhaustive list of information that can be extracted from the second-order moment of the ES.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21664_figures/2605.21664_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: R´enyi entropy curves Sα(ρA) for representative states in dA = 4 with spectra: Flat (0.25, 0.25, 0.25, 0.25); State A (0.4, 0.3, 0.2, 0.1); State B (0.7, 0.2, 0.08, 0.02); and State C (0.36, 0.34, 0.29, 0.01). FlatA states yield constant R´enyi curves, corresponding to vanishing R´enyi spread. The crossing between States A and C provides a visual indication that pointwise comparison of R´enyi entropies is insufficient; their incomparability under ≺AF is confirmed by the spread inequalities, since ∆1,2(A) &gt; ∆1,2(C) whereas ∆0.5,1(C) &gt; ∆0.5,1(A). This illustrates the partial nature of the antiflat ordering and shows that spec- tral steepness over a restricted range of α is not, by...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21664_figures/2605.21664_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Left. Probability density function of FA(ρ) for 2 qubits random pure states induced by the Haar measure, with the violet line representing the analytical result and the histogram depicting the numerical computation reasalized using Nsamples = 2 × 106. Right. Probability density function of FA(ρ) for 2 qubits random pure states induced by the Bures probability distribution, with the violet line representing the analytical result and the histogram depicting the numerical computation reasalized using Nsamples = 2 × 106.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a new framework to study the 'antiflatness' of quantum entanglement spectra, focusing on the fluctuations of eigenvalues rather than just their average. It establishes a new partial ordering for quantum states and proves that maximal spectral deviation is achieved by a specific class of 'jump' spectra. This provides a deeper tool for characterizing quantum correlations and resource theories.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and open quantum systems as it provides a new way to quantify the 'richness' of entanglement beyond simple entropy, specifically looking at how the internal structure of the entanglement spectrum evolves.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize the structural fluctuations within the entanglement spectrum of a quantum state by moving beyond standard average measures like purity or von Neumann entropy.

**Main result.** The authors introduce 'antiflatness' as a measure of spectral inhomogeneity and develop a new partial ordering called 'antiflat majorization' based on the Rényi entropy spread. They also identify a continuous Pareto frontier of extremal states that achieve maximal antiflatness.

**Method.** The study employs information geometry (escort distributions and Bregman divergences), mathematical proofs involving Lagrange multipliers and Zorn's Lemma, and statistical analysis of Haar, Bures-Hall, and t-doped Clifford random state ensembles.

**Model / system.** The framework focuses on bipartite quantum systems with reduced density operators, considering various random state ensembles and contexts such as topological order and many-body localization.

**Key observables.** Entanglement spectrum, Rényi entropy spread, Capacity of Entanglement, Linear Rényi spread, and Logarithmic antiflatness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rényi parameter (alpha), purity (Tr[rho^2]), dimension of the Hilbert space (dA), and the weight of the largest eigenvalue (r_max).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes bipartite structures and focuses on the properties of the reduced density matrix; it notes that standard majorization is structurally blind to the spectral fluctuations being studied.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates different eigenvalue profiles (flat, decaying, and maximal antiflat) and summarizes information extractable from the second-order moment of the spectrum; Figure 2 shows Rényi entropy curves for different states.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining antiflatness and the new partial ordering, moves to establishing the mathematical properties of the ordering and flatness-preserving operations, explores the geometric structure of the accessible antiflatness volume, and concludes with the identification of maximal antiflatness states and their statistical properties.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We explore the concept of antiflatness to characterize the structural fluctuations within the entanglement spectrum of a quantum state (i.e., the spectrum of its reduced density operator). As a measure of the interplay between entanglement and magic, two fundamental quantum resources, antiflatness provides second-order information about quantum correlations that standard average measures fail to capture. Recognizing that standard majorization theory fundamentally orders states by purity and is structurally blind to spectral fluctuations, we introduce a novel partial ordering known as antiflat majorization, based on the Rényi entropy spread. We define Flatness-Preserving Operations (FPOs), establishing new necessary conditions for state convertibility. Furthermore, we unify different measures of antiflatness-such as Capacity of Entanglement, Linear Rényi spread, and Logarithmic antiflatness-using the frameworks of escort distributions and Bregman divergences. We prove that the Capacity of Entanglement can be expressed as a second derivative of the Kullback-Leibler divergence along the escort trajectory, connecting it with the Quantum Fisher Information. Finally, we demonstrate that absolute maximal antiflatness is not achieved by a single universal state, but rather by a continuous Pareto frontier of extremal states with jump spectra, and we analyze the typicality of these spectral fluctuations using Haar, Bures-Hall and t-doped Clifford random state ensembles.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22386"></a>
### [Factorization rule for multitime correlations in non-Markovian open quantum systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22386v1)

**Authors:** Thomas K. Bracht, Moritz Cygorek  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22386v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22386_figures/2605.22386_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) The dynamical map for density matrix evolution becomes stationary after the memory time t ≥τc, which en- ables exact long-time extrapolation of non-Markovian dynam- ics. (b) Exact factorization of a two-time correlation function when t2 −t1 ≥τc, i.e., t1 and t2 differ by at least the memory time τc. Propagation over 2τ 2 c contains all information needed to reconstruct two-time correlations with arbitrary time ar- guments.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22386_figures/2605.22386_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. (a) Emission spectrum of a weakly cw-driven QD with non-Markovian coupling to phonons. Standard QRT predicts the phonon sideband on the wrong side of the zero-phonon line. Using factorization the complete information is con- tained in a temporal volume of τ 2 c . (b) Exemplary emission spectrum for resonant pulsed excitation.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22386_figures/2605.22386_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) Time-integrated second-order coherence for a res- onant pulse with σ = 5 ps and pulse area of π, resulting in g(2)[0] = 0.032. For other parameters, see appendix. (b) Ra- tio of photon counts stemming from a single pulse and subse- quent pulses at different pulse areas.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new exact factorization rule for calculating multitime correlation functions in non-Markovian systems. By breaking down complex n-time correlations into simpler lower-order parts, the method achieves massive computational speed-ups without sacrificing accuracy. This is particularly useful for studying the optical properties of quantum dots coupled to phonon environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful computational shortcut for simulating complex non-Markovian dynamics, allowing for the efficient calculation of high-order correlations and spectra that were previously too expensive to compute.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Calculating multitime correlation functions in non-Markovian open quantum systems is computationally expensive because the breakdown of the Quantum Regression Theorem (QRT) requires long-time, brute-force simulations.

**Main result.** The authors derive an exact factorization rule that decomposes n-th order correlations into products of lower-order correlations, providing a speed-up of two to three orders of magnitude while maintaining exact accuracy.

**Method.** The method uses an eigenvector decomposition of the stationary dynamical map within a Markovian embedding framework to decouple time steps.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on non-Markovian open quantum systems with time-independent Hamiltonians and finite memory times, specifically using semiconductor quantum dots coupled to a super-Ohmic phonon bath as a primary example.

**Key observables.** Multitime correlation functions, emission spectra S(omega), second-order coherence G(2)(t, tau), and integrated second-order coherence g(2)[0].

**Important parameters / regimes.** Memory time (tau_c), phonon dynamics (picosecond scale), radiative decay (nanosecond scale), and temperature (4 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Hamiltonian is time-independent, the initial system-environment state is an uncorrelated product, and the environment has a finite memory time.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the stationary dynamical map and the visual concept of factorization; Figure 2 compares the accuracy of the factorization method against brute-force and QRT for emission spectra; Figure 3 shows time-integrated second-order coherence.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-Markovian correlations, derives the mathematical factorization rule using Liouvillian eigenbasis decomposition, applies the method to quantum dots coupled to phonons, and validates the efficiency and accuracy against brute-force simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Experiments performed on quantum systems often measure multitime correlation functions. When quantum systems are weakly coupled to their environment, the time evolution of such correlation functions can be reduced to that of the reduced density matrix by the quantum regression theorem (QRT). While no QRT is available for general non-Markovian open quantum systems, we show that for time-independent Hamiltonians and finite memory times $τ_c$, an exact factorization rule exists that relates higher-order multitime correlations to products of lower-order correlations. Consequently, all information needed to reconstruct $n$-time correlations is contained in a temporal volume of $\mathcal{O}(τ_c^n)$. On the example of quantum dots coupled to phonons, we demonstrate that this factorization makes numerical calculations of multitime correlations extremely efficient and even enables semianalytical solutions in systems where the standard QRT breaks down.

</details>

### [Joint Unitarity and a Single Definite Outcome in a Quantum Measurement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22551v1)

**Authors:** Muxi Liu

<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates whether a single quantum measurement run with a definite outcome can be described by a joint unitary evolution. It establishes that if such a unitary process exists, the final state of the environment must retain a trace of the initial system state. This provides a way to experimentally test the compatibility of unitary dynamics with the emergence of definite outcomes.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, experimentally testable criterion to distinguish between the standard von Neumann/conditioning interpretation and models where individual measurement runs are fundamentally unitary.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Resolving the discrepancy between the von Neumann measurement model, which predicts entangled states, and the empirical reality of single, definite measurement outcomes.

**Main result.** The author derives a lower bound on the dependence of the post-measurement environment state on the initial system state, providing an experimentally testable signature of unitary dynamics in individual measurement runs.

**Method.** The study uses mathematical tools such as the trace norm, diamond norm, and the gentle measurement lemma to analyze information transfer and derive bounds on state distinguishability.

**Model / system.** A tripartite system consisting of a measured system (S), a measurement apparatus (A), and an environment (E), where different measurement outcomes are driven by different underlying unitary maps.

**Key observables.** The trace distance between the reduced final states of the environment conditioned on the same measurement outcome.

**Important parameters / regimes.** delta (state deviation), eta (environment fluctuation), and gamma (unitary mechanism fluctuation).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The multiplicity assumption (different outcomes correspond to different unitaries) and the universality requirement (the mechanism for an outcome is independent of the input state).

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the problem of outcomes, proposes an alternative unitary model with outcome-dependent maps, derives a mathematical lower bound for environment state dependence, and discusses experimental observability and noise limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigated the possibility that a single measurement run with a definite outcome is a joint unitary evolution of all the participating systems, and measurement runs with different definite outcomes correspond to different unitary maps. With reasonable assumptions, we derived a lower bound of the dependence of the environment after measurement on the state of the system before measurement, conditioned on the same measurement outcome. An experimental test of this dependence relation can either serve as evidence that the unitary dynamics and the definite outcome in the orthodox sense cannot be true together or suggest a deviation from the von Neumann measurement model plus a "conditioning" interpretational step.

</details>

### [Symmetry Breaking as Quantum Gate: Entropy and Weak Mixing Angle](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22070v1)

**Authors:** Qing-Hong Cao, Yandong Liu, Haotian Qi, Hao Zhang, Haoran Zhao

<sub>Fig. 4 is that RMI exhibits a qualitatively different de- pendence on kinematics compared with SRE. For SRE constructed in the spin computational basis, Refs. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the variation in Rényi mutual information and stabilizer Rényi entropy in electroweak scattering follows the same dependence on the weak mixing angle. This shared behavior is explained by treating the Yukawa mass insertion as a quantum gate in chirality space. The results suggest that entropic measures can serve as a diagnostic tool for the chiral structure of particle interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel bridge between high-energy particle physics and quantum information theory by interpreting fundamental symmetry breaking as a specific quantum gate operation acting on chirality space.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the relationship between electroweak symmetry breaking (EWSB) and quantum information theoretic measures, specifically looking for a correspondence between Rényi mutual information and stabilizer Rényi entropy.

**Main result.** The authors establish that RMI and SRE exhibit an identical dependence on the weak mixing angle after angular averaging, a correspondence traced to the Yukawa mass insertion acting as a -iY quantum gate in chirality space.

**Method.** The study uses helicity amplitude formalism, quantum state tomography to reconstruct density matrices, and angular averaging over the celestial sphere to evaluate entropic probes.

**Model / system.** The system consists of 2 to 2 elastic scattering processes (e.g., e- mu- to e- mu-) within the Standard Model framework, specifically focusing on neutral-current channels involving Z boson exchange across the EWSB transition.

**Key observables.** Rényi mutual information (RMI) in the helicity basis and stabilizer Rényi entropy (SRE) in the fixed beam basis.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weak mixing angle (sin^2 theta_W), scattering angle (theta), and fermion coupling strengths (kappa_L, kappa_R); high-energy limit (sqrt(s) = 10 TeV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis uses the high-energy limit to isolate energy-independent contributions and assumes tree-level 2 to 2 elastic scattering.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a conceptual framework linking QFT and QI; Figure 2 depicts the Yukawa interaction as a quantum gate; Figure 3 illustrates the scattering process before and after EWSB; Figures 4 and 5 show the dependence of entropic measures on scattering angles and the weak mixing angle.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a unified framework for QFT and QI, describes the scattering model and the quantum gate interpretation of mass insertion, presents the calculation of entropic probes, analyzes the correspondence between RMI and SRE, and discusses the diagnostic role of these measures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We establish a correspondence between two independent entropic probes -- the variation of Rényi mutual information (RMI) across the electroweak symmetry breaking (EWSB) transition and the stabilizer Rényi entropy (SRE) -- in tree-level $2\to 2$ elastic scatterings. After angular averaging, the RMI (helicity basis) and the SRE (fixed beam basis) exhibit identical dependence on $\sin^2θ_W$ within each neutral-current channel. We trace this correspondence to a common physical origin that it's the Yukawa mass insertion acts as a $-\mathrm{i}Y$ quantum gate in chirality space. Minimizing entropies across all processes yields $\sin^2θ_W$ values matching purely axial vector-like couplings in $Z$ boson exchanged channel.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21632"></a>
### [Diagrammatic Monte Carlo for Fermionic Rényi Entanglement Entropy](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21632v1)

**Authors:** Boyuan Shi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21632v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21632_figures/2605.21632_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Order-by-order interaction corrections. Left: 3 × 3 benchmark with ED derivatives, shown as red stars, for the first three coefficients. Right: 40 × 40 production run for a periodic origin-square subregion with radius r = 8. The plotted values are coefficients of ln Z2[A]/N, not cumulative sums; the omitted zeroth-order terms are the free-system values −0.27507894 (left) and −0.04089329 (right).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new Diagrammatic Monte Carlo method to directly compute Renyi entanglement entropy in interacting fermionic systems. By using a graded-swap representation and connected-determinant sampling, the method avoids the traditional sign problem and scales to much larger lattice sizes than exact diagonalization. This provides a powerful tool for studying entanglement in complex, strongly correlated regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a scalable numerical framework to calculate the Renyi entanglement entropy for interacting lattice fermions, specifically overcoming the sign problem and computational costs in 2D and finite-temperature regimes.

**Main result.** The authors successfully implemented a Diagrammatic Monte Carlo (DiagMC) framework that provides accurate order-by-order interaction corrections for large systems, validated against exact diagonalization for small clusters and applied to a 40x40 lattice.

**Method.** A direct diagrammatic Monte Carlo framework using a fermionic graded-swap representation, connected-determinant sampling (CDet), and a many-configuration Markov-chain Monte Carlo sampler.

**Model / system.** The repulsive Hubbard model on a square lattice with periodic boundary conditions, focusing on the entanglement of a square subregion A.

**Key observables.** Second Renyi entanglement entropy (S2) and its interaction-dependent coefficients.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength (U), chemical potential (mu), inverse temperature (beta), and subregion size/geometry.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary computational bottleneck is memory usage rather than the conventional auxiliary-field sign problem; the method is oriented toward finite-size/finite-region calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares order-by-order CDet Monte Carlo coefficients against exact diagonalization for a 3x3 cluster and presents production results for a 40x40 lattice.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a logical progression from the theoretical formulation of the graded-swap path integral to the derivation of the determinant expansion, followed by the description of the MCMC sampler, validation via benchmark, and finally large-scale production results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a direct diagrammatic Monte Carlo framework for the Renyi entanglement entropy of interacting lattice fermions. The method starts from the fermionic graded-swap representation of Z_n[A]=Tr_Aρ_A^n, which converts the entropy problem into a replicated path integral with mixed temporal boundary conditions on the entangling region. In this representation the replica momenta are half-shifted, q_m=(2m+1)π/n, and the interaction expansion has a determinant form suitable for connected-determinant summation. We combine this expansion with a many-configuration Markov-chain Monte Carlo sampler to obtain order-by-order corrections for very large systems to very high orders. As a benchmark, we compare the order-by-order coefficients of a 3*3 Hubbard cluster with exact diagonalization. We then report a production calculation for a large periodic lattice with a square subregions. The dominant system-size limitation is therefore memory rather than a conventional auxiliary-field sign problem. The results provide a step toward diagrammatic calculations of fermionic entanglement observables in regimes where direct quantum Monte Carlo sampling is costly or sign-problem limited.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22424"></a>
### [Long-range nonstabilizerness of topologically encoded states from mutual information](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22424v1)

**Authors:** David Aram Korbany, Tyler D. Ellison, David T. Stephen, Lorenzo Piroli  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22424v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22424_figures/2605.22424_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Pictorial representation of the string-net lattice. The figure also shows the supports of the vertex, plaquette, and string operators.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22424_figures/2605.22424_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Dehn twist on the torus implemented by a shear transformation (green arrows), followed by a local geometric deformation to restore the lattice. The coordinate grid rep- resents the connectivity of the lattice. Note that the shear transformation is a permutation where in the n-th column the vertices are shifted by n steps.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22424_figures/2605.22424_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 6. Pictorial derivation of Eq. (56). We only show the “ket” layer. The horizontal double lines (colored) indicate a trace over ˜C on the left, and over C in the middle pannel.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper investigates how mutual information between disjoint regions on a torus can detect long-range nonstabilizerness in topological phases. It provides a complete classification for the Toric Code and shows that while the method is powerful, it has limitations in non-abelian systems. The results also impose fundamental constraints on which logical gates can be implemented fault-tolerantly.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to many-body dynamics and quantum information as it provides a new information-theoretic tool to detect non-stabilizer resources in topological phases, which is crucial for understanding the limits of fault-tolerant quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize and detect long-range nonstabilizerness (LRN) in topologically encoded states in 2D, specifically determining if mutual information can serve as a reliable diagnostic tool.

**Main result.** The authors prove that for the Z2 toric code, non-integer mutual information between non-overlapping regions containing non-contractible loops provides a full classification of LRN, whereas for non-abelian Fibonacci models, the method detects LRN for all but a finite subset of states.

**Method.** The study uses information-theoretic analysis of mutual information, modular transformations (S and T matrices) of the torus, and entropic uncertainty relations (Maassen-Uffink bound) to relate anyon properties to LRN.

**Model / system.** The research focuses on 2D topologically ordered systems defined on a torus, specifically the Z2 Toric Code and non-abelian string-net models like the doubled Fibonacci topological order.

**Key observables.** Mutual information (I), von Neumann entropy, and anyon labels/flux.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Shallow-depth local quantum circuits (depth O(polylog(L))), thermodynamic limit (N -> infinity), and modular group transformations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is a regular 2D lattice; regions are sufficiently distant and large enough to encompass Wilson loop operators; the study focuses on minimal-entropy states.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Toric Code operators and non-contractible cycles; Figure 2 illustrates spatial regions A and B; Figure 3 shows Wilson loop operator mappings; Figure 4 depicts the string-net lattice; Figure 5 shows a Dehn twist via lattice shear; Figure 6 shows a pictorial derivation of trace equations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces LRN and the problem of 2D detection, establishes a theorem for LRN detection using mutual information, applies this to the Z2 Toric Code for full classification, extends the analysis to non-abelian string-net models, and concludes with implications for fault-tolerant logical gates.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study long-range nonstabilizerness (LRN), namely the obstruction to remove nonstabilizerness with shallow-depth local quantum circuits. In one-dimensional settings, the mutual information between disconnected spatial regions has proven to be a powerful tool to diagnose LRN. In this work, we focus on encoded states of two-dimensional topologically-ordered systems, and explore the ability of the mutual information to serve as a diagnostic of LRN. Focusing on the concrete setting of lattice models defined on a torus, we show that information about LRN can be gained from the analysis of the mutual information between non-overlapping regions containing non-contractible loops, and of the change of such mutual information under modular real-space transformations. We exemplify this idea in the toric code and the non-abelian string-net model with doubled Fibonacci topological order. In the former case, we show that the mutual information provides a full classification, certifying LRN for all encoded non-stabilizer states. In the latter case, instead, our approach does not lead to a full classification, as it detects LRN for all states except from a finite subset with special transformation properties under the modular group. Finally, we discuss how our results on LRN constrain the logical gates that can be implemented fault-tolerantly on the torus.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22150"></a>
### [Unified entropy entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22150v1)

**Authors:** Wenxue Ren, Binghao Li, Ruiqun Niu, Yu Guo, Shuanping Du  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22150v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22150_figures/2605.22150_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: The comparison of E2,2(ρw), E2,1/2(ρw) and E1/2,1/2(ρw).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a generalized framework for entanglement measures based on 'unified entropy,' which encompasses Tsallis and Renyi entropies. It explores the properties of both (q,s) and (r,t) type entanglement, proving their monotonicity and monogamy while identifying specific measures that satisfy tight complete monogamy. The study provides a unified approach to understanding multipartite entanglement distribution.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a generalized mathematical framework for quantifying entanglement, which is fundamental for understanding entanglement distribution and monogamy in multipartite quantum networks and many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop and characterize a unified theory of multipartite entanglement measures using the 'unified entropy' framework, specifically investigating the properties of (q,s) and (r,t) type entropy entanglement.

**Main result.** The authors prove that (q,s) entropy entanglement is an entanglement monotone and is monogamous, and they identify one specific global multipartite entanglement measure (GlMEM) that is both completely and tightly completely monogamous.

**Method.** The study employs mathematical frameworks involving the unified entropy definition, partition theory (coarsening relations), LOCC analysis, and the use of Schmidt decomposition and operator convexity.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on n-partite quantum systems consisting of finite-dimensional Hilbert spaces, specifically analyzing pure and mixed states, including the two-qubit Werner state as a benchmark.

**Key observables.** Unified entropy entanglement measures (E_{q,s}, E_{r,t}), Global Multipartite Entanglement Measures (GlMEMs), and Genuine Entanglement Measures (GEMs).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Entropy parameters (q, s, r, t) defining different regimes, such as 0 < r < 1 and q > 1, and the dimension of the subsystems.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis of mixed states assumes the existence of an optimal decomposition via the convex-roof extension.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the unified entropy framework, defines bipartite and multipartite entanglement measures, establishes monotonicity and monogamy properties for different parameter regimes, compares different types of global measures, and provides analytical proofs for hierarchy violations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The unified entropy as a promotion of the von Neumann entropy exhibits distinct diversity which contains the Tsallis entropy, the Rényi entropy, the von Neumann entropy as special cases. The unified-($r,t$) entropy entanglement with $0<r<1$ and $0< t\leq 1$ was shown to be an entanglement monotone in literature. In this paper, we explore unified-($q,s$) entropy entanglement with $q>1$ and $qs\geq1$ and show that it is also an entanglement monotone and that both of them are monogamous. Going further, we present two kinds of global multipartite entanglement measures (GlMEMs) based on the unified entropy and each kind has two subclasses which are classified by the parameters $(q,s)$ and $(r,t)$. Consequently, from the view of the complete multipartite entanglement measure theory, we show that one of them is a complete multipartite entanglement monotone and is not only completely monogamous but also tightly completely monogamous, but the other three are even not complete. We also explore the genuine entanglement measures induced by the unified entropy and the relations with the bipartite entanglement and the global entanglement are discussed, respectively.

</details>
