<a id="paper-2605.22293"></a>
### [Modular Variables and the Limits of Phase Detectability in Open Quantum Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22293v1)

**Authors:** S. V. Mousavi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22293v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22293_figures/2605.22293_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Density plots of the probability density for the Schr¨odinger dynamics (left) and the CL dynamics (right) at γ = 0.1 and T = 10. A representative set of Bohmian trajectories is superimposed. These trajectories follow the motion of the left wave packet, while the right wave packet remains empty. The middle blue trajectory corresponds to the wave-packet center. The remaining parameters are fixed as ¯h = m = kB = σ0 = 1, k = 0.1, L = 50, and g = −3.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22293_figures/2605.22293_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Local expectation value of the modular variable along Bohmian trajectories for a relative phase α = π/4 and three different Bohmian initial positions: X0 = −L/2 −2σ0 (brown), X0 = −L/2 (magenta), and X0 = −L/2 + 2σ0 (cyan). The left panel shows the Schr¨odinger dynamics [Eq. (34)], and the right panel shows the CL dynamics. The remaining parameters are ¯h = m = kB = σ0 = 1, k = 0.1, L = 50, g = −3, γ = 0.001, and T = 2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22293_figures/2605.22293_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Local expectation value of modular variable along the Bohmian trajectory with initial position X0 = −L/2, shown for different values of the relative phase α: α = 0 (top left), α = π/4 (top right), α = π/2 (bottom left), and α = π (bottom right). In each panel, the black curve corresponds to the Schr¨odinger dynamics [Eq. (34)], while the red and green curves correspond to the CL framework with temperatures T = 2 and T = 5, respectively, and a relaxation rate γ = 0.001. The remaining parameters are ¯h = m = kB = σ0 = 1, k = 0.1, L = 50, and g = −3.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22293_figures/2605.22293_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Expectation value of the modular variable of the first particle [Eq. (59)] for relative phases α = 0 (left panel) and α = π/2 (right panel). Colored curves correspond to the CL framework with relaxation rate γ = 0.005 and temperatures T = 2 (red), T = 5 (green), and T = 15 (blue). Other parameters are ¯h = m = kB = σ0 = 1, k = 0.1, g = −3 and L = 50.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that modular variables can act as sensitive probes for nonlocal phase information in quantum superpositions, even when wave packets are spatially separated and subject to environmental decoherence. While thermal noise reduces the signal amplitude, the phase information remains accessible, unlike with standard local observables. The study highlights the utility of modular operators in detecting quantum features in the presence of dissipation and gravity.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel way to access nonlocal phase information in open quantum systems where standard decoherence-related metrics like entanglement or coherence fail, offering a potential probe for gravity-induced effects in the presence of noise.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether relative phase information in spatially non-overlapping quantum superpositions can be detected using modular variables in the presence of environmental noise and gravity.

**Main result.** Modular variables remain sensitive to the relative phase even when wave packets do not overlap, whereas standard local quantities and information-theoretic measures like entanglement fail to capture this phase. Thermal noise damps the signal amplitude, but the phase-sensitive structure remains robust.

**Method.** The study uses the Bohmian mechanics framework to compute local expectation values along particle trajectories and employs the Caldeira-Leggett master equation to model open-system dynamics.

**Model / system.** Gaussian wave-packet superpositions (single and two-particle systems) subject to a uniform gravitational field and coupled to a common environment modeled by the Caldeira-Leggett equation in the high-temperature limit.

**Key observables.** Modular operators (modular momentum and translation operators), probability density, probability current, and information-theoretic measures (coherence, entropy, and entanglement).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spatial separation (L), gravitational acceleration (g), relaxation rate (gamma), temperature (T), and quantum statistics (Bose-Einstein, Fermi-Dirac, and Maxwell-Boltzmann).

**Assumptions / limitations.** High-temperature limit, Born-Markov approximation, Ohmic spectral density, and the regime of negligible spatial overlap between wave packets.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares density plots of unitary vs. dissipative dynamics; Figure 2 shows the time evolution of local modular expectation values; Figure 3 demonstrates phase dependence for different relative phases; Figure 4 shows the impact of temperature and relaxation on two-particle modular signals.

**Paper structure.** The paper derives the equation of motion for the displacement operator, analyzes Gaussian wave-packet superpositions under unitary and dissipative dynamics, extends the analysis to multi-particle systems with different quantum statistics, and concludes by comparing modular variables to standard local observables.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Modular variables serve as a striking example of quantum nonlocality, particularly in superpositions of wave packets that are spatially well separated, where the relative phase between components cannot be accessed through conventional local measurements. In this work, we explore the time evolution of Hermitian modular operators for Gaussian wave-packet superpositions under the influence of a uniform gravitational field. We consider both unitary dynamics governed by the Schrödinger equation and open-system dynamics described by the Caldeira-Leggett master equation in the high-temperature limit. Adopting the Bohmian interpretation of quantum mechanics, we compute local expectation values of these modular operators along individual particle trajectories. Our analysis shows that gravitational acceleration induces a time-varying modular signal, the expectation value of the modular observable, that remains sensitive to the relative phase between the separated wave packets. In contrast, standard local quantities such as the probability density and probability current, while modified by gravity, become insensitive to the relative phase in the regime of negligible spatial overlap. For a pair of particles coupled to a shared environment, we find that environment-induced correlations can modify the local modular expectation value observed for one particle, yielding a clear signature of environmental influence. However, the transfer of phase sensitivity via environment-generated entanglement to the modular signal of the distant particle remains negligible within the regime considered. We further demonstrate that conventional measures of coherence and entanglement do not capture the relative phase information in this non-overlapping regime.

</details>

### [Photon Anomalous Blockade in Waveguide Cavity QED with Atomic Mirrors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21889v1)

**Authors:** Yang Xue, Yue Chang, Tao Shi, Yu-xi Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21889v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

Here, the states |ep, D⟩= |ep⟩⊗|D⟩ and |gp, D⟩= |gp⟩⊗|D⟩include the branch of the dark state |D⟩, the states |ep, B⟩= |ep⟩⊗|B⟩and |gp, B⟩= |gp⟩⊗|B⟩in- clude the branch of the bright state |B⟩. (b) Energy spectrum of the system for different decay rates γ of the medium atom. The lower energy splitting (solid line) represents the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21889_figures/2605.21889_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) and (b) Correlation functions g(2)(0) as a function of the detuning ∆/√2Γγ for different driving strengths ϵ. The decay rate of the medium atom is γ = 0.01Γ in (a) and γ = Γ in (b). (c) Correlation functions g(2)(0) obtained by the ana- lytical results (blue solid, green dashed and red dotted curves) and direct numerical calculations by solving master equation (blue points, green squares, and red triangles) for different medium-atom decay rates γ. (d) Correlation functions g(2)(τ) as a function of time τ for distances d = |xp −x1| = λ0/4 and d = λ0/10, with separation between two mirror atoms fixed at |x2 −x1| = λ0/2 and detuning ∆= Re(λ−) ≈√2Γγ.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper identifies a new type of photon blockade in waveguide cavity QED that works even in high-loss, weak-coupling environments. The effect is driven by the Quantum Zeno effect rather than traditional strong coupling. This discovery offers a potential pathway for creating robust single-photon devices using existing waveguide QED platforms.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel mechanism (Quantum Zeno effect) for photon blockade in dissipative regimes, which is highly relevant for developing robust single-photon sources in open quantum systems and waveguide-based quantum networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the emergence of a photon anomalous blockade in a waveguide cavity QED system, specifically in the 'bad-cavity' regime where conventional blockade mechanisms are absent.

**Main result.** The authors discover that photon blockade can occur in a high-dissipation, weak-coupling regime due to the Quantum Zeno effect, and that this effect is robust against the position of the medium atom and can be enhanced by using N-atom mirrors.

**Method.** The researchers utilize both master equation and scattering theories, employing a non-Hermitian effective Hamiltonian and analyzing the system within single- and two-excitation subspaces.

**Model / system.** A waveguide cavity QED system consisting of a medium atom coupled to two quantized mirror atoms (or N-atom mirrors) within a waveguide, modeled using a Lindblad master equation and a non-Hermitian Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Second-order correlation functions g(2)(0) and g(2)(tau), photon statistics, and the energy spectrum (eigenvalues).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Decay rates of mirror atoms (Gamma) and medium atom (gamma), detuning (Delta), driving strength (epsilon), and the number of atoms in the mirror (N).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a weakly driven probe field and applies the Markovian approximation.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the schematic of the waveguide cavity system and a comparison to a Fabry-Perot cavity; Fig 2 illustrates the transition diagram and the energy spectrum of the system.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the waveguide cavity QED setup, develops the theoretical framework using master equations and effective Hamiltonians, analyzes the single- and two-excitation subspaces to identify the blockade mechanism, explores scaling with N-atom mirrors, and discusses experimental feasibility.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Waveguide cavity quantum electrodynamics (QED) with atomic mirrors is a growing research area of quantum optics and can be applied to quantum information processing. We here study the photon statistics of output fields from a waveguide cavity QED system, in which the waveguide is coupled to quantized mirror atoms and one driven medium atom. Our results show that the photon blockade can occur even for a bad atom cavity with large dissipation and small coupling between the medium atom and the cavity, in contrast to the small dissipation and the strong coupling of the medium atom to the cavity field for the conventional photon blockade or the quantum interference for the unconventional photon blockade in the cavity QED system. Utilizing both the master equation and scattering theories, we derive the condition under which the photon blockade occurs in weakly driven systems. We find that such photon anomalous blockade is due to the quantum Zeno effect and is robust against variations of the medium atom's position within the cavity. Our study paves a way to exploit the photon blockade and single-photon devices via the waveguide cavity QED.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22797"></a>
### [One-photon communication in atomic media](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22797v1)

**Authors:** Zixiang Hong, John C. Schotland  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22797v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22797_figures/2605.22797_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Coupling-dependence of normalized fidelity.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper quantifies how single-photon quantum information is lost when traveling through an atomic medium. It discovers that for certain types of noise, the loss of fidelity follows a universal pattern that remains bounded even at very high coupling strengths. This is significant for understanding the fundamental limits of quantum communication through matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental performance bound for quantum communication through atomic media and identifies a universal behavior for fidelity degradation that is robust against medium disorder.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the interaction between a single photon and an atomic medium causes information loss and decoherence during transmission.

**Main result.** The authors establish a universal principle where normalized fidelity for erasure and dephasing channels decreases monotonically with coupling strength but is bounded from below by 1/2, a result that holds for both uniform and disordered media.

**Method.** The study uses quantum field theory to model the interaction and analyzes the degradation through the lens of specific quantum channels (erasure, dephasing, and depolarization) and Holevo information maximization.

**Model / system.** A quantized scalar electromagnetic field interacting with a collection of two-level atoms via a Hamiltonian including field, atomic, and interaction terms under the rotating wave approximation.

**Key observables.** Quantum channel fidelity, normalized fidelity, and channel capacity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Atom-photon coupling strength (g), atomic density (n0), atomic resonance frequency (Omega), and photon wavevector (k0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a scalar field (ignoring polarization), uses the rotating wave approximation, and assumes the quantum channels are independent of the coupling strength's effect on phase.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the dependence of fidelity on the coupling strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of photon transmission loss, defines the Hamiltonian and medium configurations, analyzes specific quantum channels, derives a universal fidelity formula, and discusses the asymptotic limits and implications for information capacity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the problem of single-photon transmission through an atomic medium, using quantum channel fidelity to describe the resulting information loss. We find that the normalized fidelity decreases monotonically with coupling strength, establishing a performance bound for quantum communication through such media. Our results hold for several channel types and for deterministic and random media.

</details>

<sub>Fig. 3. Interaction of vectorial focal fields with metallic mesostructures. (a) Transmission elec- tron microscopy image of a mesocrystal cluster, consisting of several self-assembled gold nanoparti- cles. Darker areas indicate stacking of nanoparticles. (b) Energy-filtered electron image (EFTEM) after interaction with linearly polarized light (black arrow). The dashed line indicates the outline of the cluster. (c) EFTEM image after interaction of the mesocrystal with an azimuthal focal field. (d)</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to directly couple vectorial laser fields to electron beams using a thin membrane to break symmetry and reveal longitudinal field components. This enables both the coherent modulation of electron energy for attosecond science and high-resolution, sub-diffraction imaging of nanophotonic near-fields in metallic structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and ultrafast science as it provides a method for the collinear generation of attosecond electron pulses and free-electron qubits using structured light.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving direct, collinear, and tilt-free coupling between vectorial laser fields and electron beams to enable ultrafast electron modulation and near-field imaging.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated that longitudinal electric fields from radially polarized light can coherently modulate electron energy and provide sub-diffraction-limit imaging, while azimuthal polarization can probe 3D nanophotonic near-fields in metallic mesocrystals.

**Method.** The study uses an ultrafast transmission electron microscope integrated with a laser system and liquid-crystal polarizers, combined with Richards-Wolf vectorial diffraction simulations and energy-filtered TEM (EFTEM).

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of a 200 keV electron beam interacting with focused vectorial laser fields (linear, azimuthal, and radial) at a 50-nm SiN membrane, applied to both free electrons and gold nanoparticle mesocrystals.

**Key observables.** Electron energy spectra (energy gain/loss and sidebands), EFTEM images (real-space energy-filtered intensity), and longitudinal electric/magnetic field distributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 200 keV electron energy, 1030-nm laser wavelength, 250-fs pulse duration, 50-nm SiN membrane thickness, and sub-diffraction-limit spot sizes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The interaction is modeled using a coherent interaction model where electron energy modulation is proportional to the squared amplitude of the longitudinal electric field.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental setup and polarization-dependent field structures; Fig 2 compares simulated and measured electron energy spectra and EFTEM images; Fig 3 demonstrates the application to gold nanoparticle mesocrystals using various polarization states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of electron-light coupling, describes the experimental setup and polarization shaping, presents the fundamental demonstration of electron modulation with radial polarization, and extends the technique to imaging complex metallic mesostructures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Controlled interaction of laser light with electron beams is fundamental for ultrafast electron microscopy and electron-based quantum optics, yet their direct coupling is forbidden in free space. Here we use longitudinally polarized light at a thin membrane and show that the emerging focal fields can modulate the electron beam in a direct, coherent and linear way, without the need for nanostructured materials or slanted interaction geometries. Also, we use vectorial polarizations to excite and probe three-dimensional nanophotonic near-fields in metallic mesocrystals by coherent electron energy gain and loss. We find that longitudinal electric fields excite axial near-fields in a direct way while longitudinal magnetic fields excite oscillating ring currents via azimuthal electric fields. These possibilities enable tilt-free, collinear generation of attosecond electron pulses or free-electron qubits and provide novel imaging modes in ultrafast electron microscopy and metamaterial tomography.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a rigorous theory for quantizing light in complex, lossy nanostructures like waveguides and surfaces. It provides a way to calculate how cavities and quantum emitters interact with each other and with propagating photons. This is crucial for the accurate simulation of integrated quantum optical devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous mathematical foundation for simulating open quantum system dynamics in realistic, lossy nanophotonic architectures, which is essential for designing integrated quantum photonic devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a rigorous quantization framework for quasinormal modes (QNMs) in complex, lossy, and structured lightguiding environments like waveguides and surfaces.

**Main result.** A generalized quantization scheme and derivation of boundary conditions for QNMs that accounts for structured backgrounds, including a system-bath Hamiltonian with rigorously defined coupling elements.

**Method.** The authors use the electromagnetic Green's function, Green's second identity, and a regularization scheme to transform diverging QNM fields into regularized, computable forms.

**Model / system.** Multiple interacting QNM cavities (e.g., metal dimers or photonic crystal cavities) coupled to quantum emitters and a non-bosonic bath of propagating photons in waveguides, surfaces, or 3D media.

**Key observables.** Complex eigenfrequencies, coupling elements, system-bath correlation functions, and QNM overlap matrices (intracavity and intercavity).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Complex eigenfrequencies (decay rates), separation parameter (distance between cavities), transfer factor (power transfer between cavities), and group velocity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The medium is assumed to be absorptive, isotropic, inhomogeneous, and non-magnetic; the far-field divergence is addressed via a few-mode regularization approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows examples of coupled cavities in different environments (dimers, waveguides, surfaces); Figure 4 illustrates a cavity near a planar interface; Figure 5 demonstrates the decoupling of multiple waveguides.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by deriving general boundary conditions for QNMs, extends these to specific geometries (1D, 2D, 3D, and waveguides), presents a quantization scheme for the system-bath Hamiltonian, and concludes with the calculation of overlap integrals and intercavity coupling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Open optical or plasmonic resonators are placed on and connected through surfaces or via waveguides, forming complex lightguiding nanostructures, e.g. for integrated photonic quantum devices. We derive general boundary conditions for quasinormal modes that account for the structure's specific geometry. We then present a general quantization scheme for multiple, interacting quasinormal-mode cavities coupled to quantum emitters and to a non-bosonic bath of propagating photons on waveguides or a surface. We derive a system-bath Hamiltonian with rigorously defined coupling elements that can be computed using Maxwell solvers, including light-matter coupling between the electromagnetic field and quantum emitters. We define system-bath correlation functions for an effective, bath-mediated, and time-delayed interaction between the quasinormal modes and quantum emitters, which is a main ingredient commonly used to simulate open quantum system dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21925"></a>
### [Nonclassical Cutoff Fluctuations in Squeezed-Light-Driven High-Harmonic Generation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21925v1)

**Authors:** Tsendsuren Khurelbaatar, R. T. Sang, Igor Litvinyuk

<sub>FIG. 3. HHG cutoff extension under squeezed-field driving. (a) Ensemble-averaged HHG spectra for CS (black), AS (blue), and PS (red) drivers at r = 2.5. <sub>FIG. 4. Quadrature-resolved HHG cutoff statistics. **Summary.** This paper investigates how squeezed light can be used to control and certify quantum fluctuations in high-harmonic generation. It identifies a specific observable—the variance of the HHG cutoff—that acts as a self-calibrating witness for nonclassicality, even in uncertain nanoscale environments. The study reveals that amplitude squeezing can push these fluctuations below the standard quantum limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** It bridges quantum optics and strong-field physics by showing how quadrature-specific noise redistribution can be mapped onto macroscopic spectral observables, offering a way to detect nonclassicality without complex homodyne detection.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining how quantum fluctuations from squeezed-light drivers manifest in macroscopic high-harmonic generation (HHG) observables and identifying a robust 'Heisenberg witness' to certify nonclassicality in nanoscale geometries.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that amplitude squeezing suppresses the shot-to-shot variance of the HHG cutoff below the standard quantum limit, providing a self-calibrating witness that is independent of the absolute vacuum-field amplitude.

**Method.** A Wigner phase-space approach is used to map the quantum-optical driver onto a stochastic ensemble of time-dependent Schrodinger equation (TDSE) realizations, supplemented by analytical cumulant expansions and ADK theory.

**Model / system.** A 1D model atom (Argon) driven by a single-mode Gaussian squeezed coherent state in a nanoscale interaction regime where vacuum-field amplitude is significantly elevated.

**Key observables.** HHG cutoff position, HHG cutoff variance, and the normalized cutoff variance ratio relative to the Standard Quantum Limit (SQL).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameter (r), squeezing angle (theta), vacuum-to-driver amplitude ratio (~10^-2), and the Keldysh parameter (gamma < 0.5).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to a single-mode Gaussian framework and assumes an undepleted driving field and coherent phase locking.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Wigner distributions and time-domain field realizations for coherent, amplitude-squeezed, and phase-squeezed states; Figure 4 illustrates the dependence of the mean cutoff and variance on the squeezing angle and parameter.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of quantum-to-classical mapping in HHG, describes the Wigner-based stochastic TDSE method, presents analytical derivations for cutoff fluctuations, and concludes with the identification of a robust Heisenberg witness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Strong-field high-harmonic generation (HHG) is conventionally described semiclassically, with the driving laser treated as a classical field. This approximation becomes insufficient in nanoscale interaction geometries, where extreme spatial confinement raises the vacuum-field amplitude to the ~10^-2 level relative to the driving-field amplitude. When the quantum fluctuations of the driving field are redistributed between conjugate quadratures by squeezing, they can be directly imprinted onto macroscopic HHG observables. To model this interaction, we employ a Wigner phase-space approach that maps the quantum-optical driver onto a stochastic ensemble of time-dependent Schrodinger equation realizations. Although each realization remains classically simulable, the sub-vacuum quadrature covariance structure of squeezed states cannot be reproduced by any field admitting a non-negative Glauber-Sudarshan P-representation. Within this single-mode Gaussian framework, we show that amplitude squeezing suppresses the shot-to-shot variance of the HHG cutoff below the standard quantum limit (SQL). To leading order in the vacuum-to-driver ratio, the normalized cutoff variance decays exponentially with the squeezing parameter, independent of the absolute vacuum-field amplitude and therefore robust against uncertainties in the effective nanoscale mode volume. A subleading phase-noise contribution from the anti-squeezed quadrature produces a variance minimum near r_opt ~ 1.6, providing a concrete experimental target. These results establish the HHG cutoff variance ratio as a nonlinear, self-calibrating Heisenberg witness in which sub-SQL scaling directly reflects the redistribution of quantum uncertainty in the driving field.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21942"></a>
### [Photon blockade via three-body interactions: toward high-purity and bright single-photon sources](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21942v1)

**Authors:** Sheng Zhao, Peng-Bo Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21942v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

For (a) and (b), we take Ω= 0.1κ and γ = 0.1κ. (c, d) g(2)(0) and N versus Ωfor different (c) J (at γ = 0.1κ) and (d) qubit dissipation rates γ. </details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a photon blockade mechanism based on three-body interactions between a bosonic mode and two qubits. This approach overcomes the traditional trade-off between photon purity and brightness, providing a robust method for generating high-quality single photons. The mechanism is applicable across various platforms, including superconducting circuits, magnonics, and optomechanics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel way to bypass fundamental limits in quantum optics and circuit QED, offering a path toward high-performance single-photon sources that are robust to experimental imperfections like thermal noise and strong driving.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing photon blockade mechanisms, such as conventional and unconventional photon blockade, suffer from a fundamental trade-off where increasing brightness leads to decreased single-photon purity.

**Main result.** The authors propose a new photon blockade mechanism using three-body interactions that breaks the purity-brightness trade-off, enabling simultaneous high purity and high brightness while remaining robust against thermal noise.

**Method.** The study uses the Lindblad master equation to model system dynamics and employs the QuTiP package for numerical steady-state simulations, supplemented by analytical solutions in the two-photon subspace.

**Model / system.** A hybrid quantum system consisting of a single bosonic mode (photonic, magnonic, or phononic) coupled to two qubits via a three-body interaction term $J(a^\dagger \sigma^+_1 \sigma^-_2 + a \sigma^-_1 \sigma^+_2)$.

**Key observables.** Second-order correlation function $g^{(2)}(0)$ (purity) and average photon number $N$ (brightness).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength $J$ relative to decay rate $\kappa$, driving Rabi frequency $\Omega$, detuning $\Delta$, and thermal occupation $n_{th}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mechanism is presented within the context of a microwave photonic platform, though the authors assert its universality to other bosonic modes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows color maps of $g^{(2)}(0)$ and plots of $\log_{10} g^{(2)}(0)$ and $\log_{10} N$ as functions of detuning and coupling strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of the purity-brightness trade-off, presents the three-body interaction model, provides analytical and numerical results demonstrating the breaking of the trade-off, discusses robustness against noise and driving strength, and concludes with applications to microwave platforms.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Photon blockade is vital for single-photon generation, but current schemes with conventional and unconventional photon blockade face critical limitations like the purity-brightness trade-off, hindering the generation of high-performance single-photons. To overcome these limitations, we introduce a fundamentally new photon blockade mechanism by utilizing three-body interactions between a single photonic mode and two qubits. This kind of interaction intrinsically cuts off the excitation path to the two-photon state, resulting in a perfect photon blockade effect. The mechanism operates across a broad parameter range, free from the constraints of strong coupling or weak driving. Remarkably, it breaks the purity-brightness trade-off, enabling the simultaneous achievement of extreme purity and high brightness, both significantly outperforming previous mechanisms. Furthermore, this approach demonstrates robustness against thermal noise and avoids unwanted oscillations in the time-delayed correlation function. This work provides a path for generating high-purity, high-brightness, and robust single-photon sources, a key resource for quantum technologies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22383"></a>
### [Aggregation-Fragmentation Processes with Broken Detailed Balance](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22383v1)

**Authors:** P. L. Krapivsky  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22383v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22383_figures/2605.22383_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. The exponent ρ as a function of the splitting param- eter r is implicitly determined by (47). The minimal value of the exponent given by (48) is reached in the symmetric case of splitting into equal halves: r = 1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22383_figures/2605.22383_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. The mass density (top to bottom) for β = −1, −2, −3.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive analysis of aggregation-fragmentation models where detailed balance is broken. It characterizes the transition from stable nonequilibrium steady states to an explosive shattering regime where mass is lost to zero-mass particles.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates aggregation-fragmentation processes where the detailed balance condition is broken, specifically focusing on the properties of nonequilibrium steady states and the emergence of shattering transitions.

**Main result.** The paper identifies that for fragmentation rates scaling as mass^beta, a steady state exists for beta >= 0, while beta < 0 leads to an instantaneous shattering transition into 'dust'. It also provides exact solutions for the beta=1 case and asymptotic behaviors for the mass distribution tails.

**Method.** The author employs integro-differential equations, Laplace transforms, moment analysis, and asymptotic analysis to solve the master equations and determine the scaling properties of the cluster density.

**Model / system.** A continuous-mass aggregation-fragmentation model in a mean-field, well-mixed system where clusters merge at a mass-independent rate and fragment at rates scaling with mass as x^beta.

**Key observables.** Cluster mass distribution c(x, t), total cluster density N(t), and the n-th moments of the mass distribution M_n(t).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The fragmentation scaling exponent beta, the fragmentation rate ratio lambda, and the splitting parameter r for deterministic models.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated using a mean-field approach assuming a well-mixed system; aggregation rates are assumed to be mass-independent.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 illustrates the evolution of mass density for different negative values of beta, showing the decay of mass due to shattering.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the investigation of random splitting models, moves to deterministic splitting models, analyzes the scaling regimes for beta >= 0, and concludes with the study of the shattering transition and mass loss for beta < 0.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study aggregation-fragmentation processes in which pairs of clusters can aggregate, and each cluster can break into two fragments. If the rates of aggregation and fragmentation do not depend on the masses, detailed balance does not hold, but nonequilibrium steady states can still be deduced from an exact solution for the Laplace transform. For models in which aggregation rates remain constant but fragmentation rates scale as $(\text{mass})^β$, detailed balance holds only when $β=1$. Away from this solvable case, we employ asymptotic techniques and show that when $β\geq 0$, the steady states share similarities with those from the mass-independent ($β=0$) model. An instantaneous shattering transition with continuous mass loss occurs when $β<0$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Quantum Batteries in two-dimensional material-based Josephson Junctions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22582v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes a solid-state quantum battery using graphene-based Josephson junctions coupled to an LC resonator. It demonstrates that the presence of longitudinal coupling terms can be leveraged to enhance energy storage efficiency under specific resonance conditions. The study also compares different methods for charging the battery, such as modulating coupling strength versus tuning the superconducting phase.

**Why it may be interesting.** It explores how unconventional, non-Dicke interaction terms and non-identical two-level systems influence the performance of a quantum battery, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum thermodynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the charging dynamics and energy storage efficiency of a solid-state quantum battery based on two-dimensional material-based Josephson junctions.

**Main result.** The study finds that longitudinal interaction terms, absent in the standard Dicke model, can enhance energy storage during two-photon resonance but act as a disturbance during single-photon resonance.

**Method.** Numerical diagonalization of the total Hamiltonian and perturbative analysis of the interaction picture to evaluate energy evolution and resonance conditions.

**Model / system.** A superconducting ring with a graphene-based Josephson junction (GJJ) inductively coupled to an LC resonator, where Andreev bound states act as non-identical two-level systems.

**Key observables.** Stored energy E_B(t), energy storage capacity C_B(phi), and charging power.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength g, superconducting phase difference phi, transmission probability tau, and resonator frequency omega_r.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Short junction limit (L << xi), projection into the low-energy subspace of Andreev bound states, and use of a sudden quench protocol for charging.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the device schematic; Figure 2 illustrates the graphene junction and Andreev level splitting; Figure 9 compares different charging protocols.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the device architecture, defines the Hamiltonian and interaction terms, analyzes the impact of longitudinal coupling on single- and two-photon resonances, compares different charging protocols, and evaluates the role of energy distribution across channels.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the solid-state implementation of a Dicke-like quantum battery consisting of a two-dimensional material-based Josephson junction inductively coupled to a resonator, using graphene as a representative example. In this configuration, Andreev bound states naturally act as non-interacting, energetically non-degenerate two-level systems, and the setup allows for both single-photon and two-photon resonant processes. The coupling between the LC-circuit flux and the supercurrent through the junction gives rise to peculiar longitudinal interaction terms that have no counterpart in the conventional Dicke model. These additional couplings can enhance energy storage for a proper range of parameters. The proposed architecture also enables an alternative, but equivalent, charging protocol that relies on tuning the superconducting phase difference across the junction.

</details>

### [Sensitivity Evaluation of SU(1,1) Interferometers with Arbitrary Input Probe State and Homodyne Detections](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22029v1)

**Authors:** Sonu Jana, Dhruv Baheti, Paul Grossiord, Fabien Bretenaker, Nadia Belabas, Syamsundar De

Both modes, to- gether with the pump, then act as inputs to the second PA (characterized by g2 and G2). Internal and external losses are modeled by fictitious beam splitters placed in- side and outside the interferometer...</sub>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a unified theoretical framework to predict the phase sensitivity of SU(1,1) interferometers for any arbitrary input state. It identifies how different gain and detection configurations can be optimized to mitigate the degrading effects of internal and external optical losses. The findings provide a roadmap for achieving optimal quantum-enhanced sensing in practical, noisy environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it provides a rigorous tool for designing loss-resilient quantum-enhanced sensors and understanding the interplay between amplification, squeezing, and decoherence.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a generalized theoretical framework to evaluate the phase sensitivity of SU(1,1) interferometers using arbitrary two-mode input states and various homodyne detection schemes under the influence of internal and external losses.

**Main result.** The study provides a comprehensive analytical formulation showing that dark-fringe operation is more robust to external losses and identifies that single-port detection with balanced gains is highly robust to internal losses. It also proves that for certain inputs, the full SU(1,1) interferometer is equivalent to the truncated version under joint homodyne detection.

**Method.** The authors use a Heisenberg representation and Bogoliubov-type transformations to propagate field operators through the interferometer, deriving analytical expressions for quadrature variances and signal derivatives.

**Model / system.** The system consists of SU(1,1) interferometers (both full two-amplifier and truncated single-amplifier configurations) utilizing optical parametric amplifiers (PAs) and subject to models of internal and external optical losses.

**Key observables.** Field quadrature operators (X and Y), single-port homodyne detection outputs, and joint homodyne detection outputs (sum and difference of photocurrents).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Parametric amplifier gains (G, g), phase shift (phi), internal loss coefficients (L), external loss coefficients (l), and input state statistics (e.g., coherent state amplitude alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Lossy modes are assumed to be initialized in the vacuum state; the analysis focuses specifically on homodyne detection rather than photon-counting.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the schematics of full, truncated, and lossy SU(1,1) interferometers and different detection schemes. Figure 2 compares phase sensitivity across different phase biases. Figure 5 provides a color-coded map of optimal configurations based on loss levels. Figure 6 illustrates sensitivity variation as a function of external losses for different detection ports.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a general derivation of interferometer transformations, followed by the evolution of field quadratures and statistical moments. It then evaluates sensitivity at bright and dark fringes, compares different gain regimes and detection strategies, and concludes with a specific application to coherent state inputs and a summary of optimal configurations under loss.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We provide a general theoretical derivation of the phase sensitivity achieved by SU(1,1) interferometers under homodyne detection. The general expressions obtained accommodate arbitrary input states and include internal and external losses. In this systematic review, both full SU(1,1) interferometers with two parametric amplifiers and the truncated interferometers with only one parametric amplifier are examined. We investigate scenarios involving both single-output ports and joint homodyne detection, and consider parametric amplifiers with equal gains or with a boosted gain second amplifier. Our analytical formulation provides physical insight and understanding of the improvements in the sensitivity, which are shown to originate from noise reduction and/or signal amplification, depending on the configurations and practical implementations. Surprisingly, the configuration with single-output mode detection and parametric amplifiers with equal gains exhibits the highest robustness to very high internal losses. We finally apply this framework to a ubiquitous $|α,0\rangle$ input two-mode coherent probe state. This approach permits the comparison of different strategies and the optimization of the interferometer performance in the presence of losses. In particular, we determine which amplification and detection configurations provide the best performance, depending on the level of losses. This exemplifies how this general analytical approach provides a powerful tool to design quantum-enhanced interferometers and achieve optimal sensitivity with selected probe states and homodyne detection.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22165"></a>
### [Computable lower bound of the parameterized entanglement monotone](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22165v1)

**Authors:** Ning Yang, Yu Guo, Shuanping Du  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22165v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22165_figures/2605.22165_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. The comparison of (a) Cα and its lower bounds Lα,0.49,3, ¯Lα,0.49,3, and that of (b) Cq and its lower bounds Lq, 3 4−q ,3, ¯Lq, 3 4−q ,3 for |Φ⟩given in Eq. (27) with θ = 0.2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22165_figures/2605.22165_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. The comparison of the diﬀerent lower bounds for |Ψ⟩given in Eq. (28) with θ = 0.2: (a) Cα, Lα,0.49,2, and ¯Lα,0.49,2; (b) Cq, Lq, 3 4−q ,2, and ¯Lq, 3 4−q ,2; (c) Cq, Lq,2.4721,2,</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22165_figures/2605.22165_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. The comparison of Cα and its lower bounds Lα,0.49,3, ¯Lα,0.49,3 for the 3 ⊗3 isotropic state ρF with (a) α = 0.6 and (b) α = 0.88, respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22165_figures/2605.22165_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. The comparison between C1.5(ρF ) and its lower bounds for the cases d = 2, 3, 4, respectively.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops computable lower bounds for a class of parameterized entanglement monotones, specifically q-concurrence and alpha-concurrence. By using a generalized (N, M)-POVM framework, the authors provide bounds that are tighter than standard PPT and realignment criteria. The work also includes analytical expressions for these measures in the case of isotropic states.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is relevant for quantum information theorists as it provides more efficient, measurement-based tools for quantifying entanglement in bipartite systems without requiring full state tomography or complex optimizations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of calculating faithful entanglement measures due to complex optimization processes leads to the need for computable lower bounds for parameterized entanglement monotones.

**Main result.** The authors derive new, tighter lower bounds for q-concurrence and alpha-concurrence using (N, M)-POVMs, which outperform existing bounds from PPT, realignment, and SIC-POVM criteria.

**Method.** The study employs informationally complete (N, M)-positive operator-valued measures, Lagrange multipliers for optimization, and convexity/concavity arguments to extend results from pure to mixed states.

**Model / system.** The research focuses on bipartite quantum states (two-qubit and two-qudit systems) and specifically analyzes isotropic states.

**Key observables.** q-concurrence (for q > 1) and alpha-concurrence (for 0 < alpha < 1).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimension d, power parameters q and alpha, and POVM parameters N and M.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes specific parameter regimes (e.g., 1 < q < 2, 1/2 < alpha < 1) and the existence of a twirling operator to reduce states to isotropic form.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the proposed lower bounds against existing criteria (alpha-concurrence and q-concurrence) for pure and isotropic states, demonstrating the increased tightness of the new bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of entanglement estimation, defines the parameterized monotones, introduces the (N, M)-POVM framework, provides theorems for new lower bounds, compares performance against existing criteria, and derives analytical formulas for isotropic states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Although numerous measures of entanglement have been proposed so far, the calculation of a given faithful entanglement measure is a hard work since it is always involved in some optimization process. It is, therefore, important to estimate the lower bound of a given entanglement measure for an arbitrary quantum state. This results in a subject of intensive mathematical research. In particular, along this line, the lower bounds of concurrence or other measures that are induced from concurrence have been explored a lot. Here, we investigate the lower bounds of two kinds of entanglement monotones, i.e., $q$-concurrence ($q>1$) and $α$-concurrence ($0<α<1$), or termed the parameterized entanglement monotone together. We obtain, in the light of the informationally complete ($N$, $M$)-positive operator-valued measure [($N$, $M$)-POVM], the lower bounds for the case of $\frac12<α<1$, $1<q<2$ for two-qudit states, and the case of $2\leqslant q<3$ for two-qubit states. We list several examples which show that the lower bounds based on ($N$, $M$)-POVM outperform that of GSIC-POVM and SIC-POVM, and all these measurement based bounds are better then the ones induced by positive partial transpose (PPT) and realignment criteria in literature. In addition, we obtain an analytical formula of the parameterized entanglement monotone with $\frac12<α<1$ and $1<q<2$ for the isotropic state.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22770"></a>
### [Adiabatic Quantum Phase Estimation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22770v1)

**Authors:** Alexander Schmidhuber, Seth Lloyd  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22770v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22770_figures/2605.22770_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. A visualization of three different schedule profiles: (a) The linear ramp profile s(t) = t/T. (b) The Roland- Cerf schedule (10) mirroring the behavior of the spectral gap. (c) The Roland-Cerf schedule reparametrized by a DC clock, which we call superadiabatic RC. Also shown is the behavior of the DC clock function, whose endpoint derivatives van- ish and which thereby near-exponentially suppresses diabatic transitions.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22770_figures/2605.22770_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Numerical computation of the diabatic leakage via exact diagonalization of the two-level Hamiltonian (8). Four different schedules are considered: linear, Roland-Cerf, su- peradiabatic Roland-Cerf, and the DC clock function, which can be interpreted as the linear schedule reparametrized by the DC clock. While the linear and Roland-Cerf schedules yield a power-law decay, the reparametrized schedules sup- press diabatic transitions near-exponentially. Both RC-type schedules display St¨uckelberg oscillations arising from coher- ent interference between nonadiabatic transitions.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces an adiabatic protocol for Quantum Phase Estimation that achieves optimal Heisenberg-limited scaling. By using a specialized 'superadiabatic' scheduling technique, the authors suppress errors exponentially, making adiabatic hardware a viable platform for high-precision phase estimation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in quantum computing and open quantum systems as it provides a way to implement fundamental algorithms on analog/adiabatic hardware with optimal scaling and robustness to dephasing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an adiabatic version of Quantum Phase Estimation (QPE) that achieves optimal Heisenberg-limited scaling in both precision and failure probability while avoiding the deep circuits required by gate-based methods.

**Main result.** The authors present a superadiabatic adiabatic protocol using a Denjoy-Carleman clock that achieves near-exponential suppression of diabatic leakage, resulting in a total runtime of O(1/epsilon * log(1/delta)).

**Method.** The protocol uses a multi-stage adiabatic evolution with a reparameterized Roland-Cerf schedule and a non-quasianalytic Denjoy-Carleman clock to ensure vanishing derivatives at the boundaries of each stage.

**Model / system.** An n-qubit system with Hamiltonian Hs coupled to an m-qubit ancilla register, utilizing transverse fields, pairwise ZiZj interactions, and a coupling between the system energy and a single ancilla qubit.

**Key observables.** Diabatic leakage (transition probability), precision (epsilon), and failure probability (delta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Precision epsilon, failure probability delta, adiabatic parameter A, and the number of stages m.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Hamiltonian must be smooth (C-infinity) with vanishing derivatives at the endpoints, and the eigenvalues must remain separated by a minimum energy gap.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares linear, Roland-Cerf, and superadiabatic schedules; Figure 2 shows numerical evidence that superadiabatic schedules suppress transitions exponentially compared to power-law decay in standard schedules.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of adiabatic QPE, defines the multi-stage Hamiltonian model, develops the mathematical framework for superadiabatic estimates using Denjoy-Carleman clocks, proves leakage bounds and runtime complexity, and demonstrates applications to adiabatic Grover search.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum phase estimation (QPE) is a central algorithmic primitive that estimates eigenvalues of a Hamiltonian up to precision $ε$ in Heisenberg-limited time $T=Θ(1/ε)$. Standard gate-based implementations of QPE require deep controlled time-evolution circuits and are not native to analog hardware. Here, we present a simple adiabatic protocol for QPE that achieves (up to logarithmic factors) the optimal Heisenberg-limited scaling $T = O\left( \frac{1}ε \log\left(δ^{-1}\right)\right)$ in both the precision $ε$ and failure probability $δ$. By encoding eigenvalues in populations of computational basis states rather than complex phases, our approach is naturally robust against certain dephasing errors. The adiabatic protocol only requires the ability to couple a single ancilla qubit to the system Hamiltonian as well as pairwise couplings within the ancilla register.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21833"></a>
### [Comment on "Entropic Costs of Extracting Classical Ticks from a Quantum Clock"](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21833v1)

**Authors:** Longyan Gong  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21833v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21833_figures/2605.21833_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21833_figures/2605.21833_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This Comment argues that a recently reported quantum clock based on a double quantum dot is actually a classical system. The author shows that the reported entropic costs are merely consequences of experimental engineering, such as measurement amplification, rather than fundamental limits of quantum timekeeping.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and non-equilibrium thermodynamics as it addresses the fundamental distinction between engineering dissipation and the intrinsic thermodynamic costs of quantum-to-classical information extraction.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper challenges the claim that a double quantum dot system functions as a quantum clock and disputes the reported fundamental thermodynamic costs of timekeeping.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that the system exhibits classical behavior lacking intrinsic temporal correlations and that the reported entropy production is due to engineering dissipation rather than fundamental quantum timekeeping costs.

**Method.** The author performs a formal critique of the original work's thermodynamic analysis and statistical estimators, specifically analyzing the nature of entropy production and the role of measurement amplification.

**Model / system.** A double quantum dot (DQD) system acting as an open system exchanging charge and energy with a thermal environment, including charge sensors (dc and rf reflectometry).

**Key observables.** Entropy production per tick (Sigma_tick), combined entropy from amplification and measurement (P/T), and time estimators (Theta_net and Theta_opt).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bias voltage (V_DQD) and the reported entropic cost of approximately 60 kB per tick.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The original work assumes DQD dynamics can be extended to coherent dynamics and relies on a Markov process assumption for the Theta_opt estimator.

**Figures summary.** Table I decomposes the DQD system into its constituent parts (DQD, sensors, and environment) to classify their quantum or classical nature.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying two primary flaws in the original Letter, then proceeds to critique the quantum nature of the device, followed by a critique of the statistical time estimation methods, and concludes with a rebuttal of the thermodynamic conclusions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A recent Letter by Wadhia et al. reports a realization of a quantum clock using a double quantum dot (DQD) [Phys. Rev. Lett. 135, 200407 (2005)]. This Comment identifies two fundamental issues: (I) the claimed ``quantum clock" exhibits only classical behavior and lacks intrinsic temporal correlations between ticks; it is not sufficient for accurate time as a good clock. (II) the thermodynamic analysis misassigns entropy production and conflates amplification with measurement; the reported combined entropy is an engineering dissipation, not a fundamental cost of quantum timekeeping.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22522"></a>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Fig. 3 The origin of the screening metal. a DMFT calculated spectra on the 4 (meta) stable moir´e positions identified in DFT (marked by corresponding symbols in Fig. 2c). b Comparison between the ZBCP width for xSe = 0% and 1%. Around 400 occupied sites spectra were analyzed for xSe = 0% and about 600 sites for xSe = 1%. The inset shows representative ZBCP spectra from both sample types. c Asymmetry of the LHB and the UHB energy relative to EF for xSe = 1%. About 250 occupied spectra were analyzed.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22522_figures/2605.22522_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 The effect of superconductivity on the breathing transition of the incommensu- rate potential. a Change of the enumerated ‘occupation level’ of the CDW sites ∆nocc between T = 4.2 K to T = 1.1 K for sample S1. All samples had xSe = 0.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper identifies that an intrinsic incommensurate potential in 4Hb-TaS2 regulates electronic correlations and charge transfer between its 1T and 1H layers. This modulation creates a doped Mott regime and establishes a complex competition between the incommensurate landscape, charge-density waves, and superconductivity. The findings highlight the importance of bulk incommensurability in tuning many-body physics in hybrid materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the origin of the electronic structure and the zero-bias conductance peak in the hybrid transition metal dichalcogenide 4Hb-TaS2, specifically investigating how incommensurate potentials and charge transfer influence electronic correlations and superconductivity.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered that an intrinsic incommensurate potential, caused by a 1.1% lattice mismatch between 1T and 1H layers, modulates interlayer hybridization and charge transfer, driving the system into a doped Mott regime where superconductivity competes with the incommensurate landscape.

**Method.** The study combines experimental Scanning Tunneling Microscopy (STM) and Spectroscopy (STS) with computational Density Functional Theory (DFT) and Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (DMFT) using a periodic Anderson model.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the bulk transition metal dichalcogenide polytype 4Hb-TaS2, consisting of alternating correlated 1T and metallic 1H layers. The theoretical model is a periodic Anderson model accounting for correlated d-orbitals and uncorrelated c-electrons.

**Key observables.** Zero-bias conductance peak (ZBCP), Upper and Lower Hubbard Bands (UHB/LHB), charge transfer (CT) magnitude, and spectral weight at the Fermi energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice mismatch (~1.1%), Hubbard interaction (U = 100 meV), superconducting transition temperature (Tc approx 2.6 K), and Selenium doping concentration (x = 0% to 5%).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The computational approach uses a local-registry approximation for the incommensurate potential rather than a full moire supercell, and DMFT is applied to a simplified periodic Anderson model.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the crystal structure and STM topography; Fig 2 illustrates the modulation of charge transfer and hybridization by lattice mismatch; Fig 3 shows DMFT calculations of ZBCP and Hubbard band evolution; Fig 4 demonstrates the effect of superconductivity on CDW configuration transitions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the hybrid TMD system, presents STM/STS evidence for an intrinsic incommensurate potential, uses DFT/DMFT to model the charge transfer and many-body effects, and concludes by discussing the competition between this potential and superconductivity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The engineering of superlattices in two-dimensional van der Waals materials has enabled the realization of rich phase diagrams hosting topological and strongly correlated phases. While incommensurability is widespread in three-dimensional systems, the role of moiré potentials in bulk materials remains largely unexplored. Here, using scanning tunneling microscopy, we demonstrate that a bulk transition-metal dichalcogenide polytype, 4Hb-TaS$_2$, hosts an emergent incommensurate potential between its alternating 1T and 1H layers. Interplay with a concomitant incommensurate charge-density wave suppresses the long-range order of this potential, leading to intricate coupling with electronic correlations in the doped 1T surface layer. Combining density functional theory with dynamical mean-field theory, we show that the lattice mismatch locally modulates the interlayer distance, thereby tuning both hybridization and charge transfer between the correlated 1T and metallic 1H layers. This redistribution of charge drives the system towards a doped Mott regime, in which the remaining local moments become self-screened, giving rise to a zero-bias resonance. We further find that bulk superconductivity competes with both the underlying landscape and the associated charge transfer. Our results establish incommensurate potentials as a previously overlooked ingredient in hybrid transition-metal dichalcogenides, highlighting their central role in the interplay between electronic correlations, charge-density-wave order, and unconventional superconductivity.

</details>

### [A2QTGN: Adaptive Amplitude Quantum-Integrated Temporal Graph Network for Dynamic Link Prediction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21916v1)

**Authors:** Nouhaila Innan, M. Murali Karthick, Simeon Kandan Sonar, Vivek Chaturvedi, Muhammad Shafique  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21916v1>

<sub>Fig. 2: Pipeline of the A2QTGN methodology. The system processes a temporal interaction stream {(ut, vt, Xt, Ft, t)} and converts it into future link predictions through four coordinated modules: (a) temporal interaction data forming the discrete-time graph sequence Gt = (Vt, Et, Xt, Ft); (b) the AAE, which computes temporal feature variation ∆xi,t, derives the activity factor αi,t = σ(β∥∆xi,t∥2), and conditionally re-encodes the node into a quantum state |ψi,t⟩whose expectation values yield the embedding zi,t; (c) the TGN memory mechanism, where node memories mi,t are updated using the fused representation hi,t = NN([mprev i,t ∥zi,t]); and (d) the link prediction module, which scores...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21916_figures/2605.21916_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Performance convergence on various datasets.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a hybrid quantum-classical framework for predicting future interactions in evolving networks. By using an adaptive mechanism to only update quantum embeddings when significant changes occur, it reduces computational overhead and improves prediction accuracy. The model is shown to be effective even under the noisy conditions of near-term quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of performing efficient dynamic link prediction in evolving networks by overcoming the limitations of classical temporal graph models and the high computational cost of re-encoding quantum states at every timestep.

**Main result.** The A2QTGN framework achieves superior predictive performance across multiple temporal graph benchmarks and demonstrates robustness to noise and limited shot counts on real IBM quantum hardware.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical approach using Adaptive Amplitude Encoding (AAE) to selectively update quantum embeddings only when temporal feature variations exceed a threshold, integrated with a Temporal Graph Network (TGN) backbone.

**Model / system.** A hybrid quantum-classical neural network architecture utilizing PennyLane for quantum circuit execution, specifically employing amplitude encoding on 8 qubits and evaluating performance on IBM quantum devices (e.g., ibm_torino).

**Key observables.** Expectation values of Pauli-Z operators, prediction accuracy, precision, AUC, and Mean Reciprocal Rank (MRR).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Activity factor (alpha), update threshold (tau), number of qubits (8), and measurement shot counts (100-shot vs 2048-shot).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes that temporal feature variations can be effectively captured by a threshold-based re-encoding strategy and that the quantum-classical interface can be optimized via gradient-based learning.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the concept of dynamic graphs; Figure 2 details the A2MTGN pipeline including the adaptive update loop; Figure 3 displays performance convergence curves; and various tables present ablation studies and hardware-aware performance metrics.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies limitations in classical and static quantum graph models, proposes the A2QTGN architecture and its adaptive mechanism, details the experimental setup on various benchmarks, presents ablation studies on encoding and update strategies, and concludes with hardware-aware performance evaluations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dynamic link prediction is important for modeling evolving interactions in complex systems, including social, communication, financial, and transportation networks. Classical temporal graph models capture sequential dependencies, but they may struggle to represent concurrent and rapidly changing node-edge interactions in large dynamic graphs. We propose A2QTGN (Adaptive Amplitude Quantum-Integrated Temporal Graph Network), a hybrid quantum-classical framework that combines adaptive amplitude encoding with a Temporal Graph Network backbone. The proposed mechanism represents node interaction features as quantum states and selectively refreshes amplitude embeddings based on temporal activity, preserving stable node states while emphasizing meaningful structural changes. This design reduces unnecessary quantum re-encoding and improves temporal representation for link prediction. Experiments on five Temporal Graph Benchmark datasets show that A2QTGN achieves strong predictive and ranking performance across diverse dynamic graphs. Ablation studies confirm the importance of both the quantum embedding module and the adaptive update strategy, while hardware-aware inference using a noisy backend and limited real-device execution supports the feasibility of near-term quantum-assisted temporal graph learning.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22226"></a>
### [Geometric Construction of Optimal Teleportation Witnesses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22226v1)

**Authors:** Yanning Jia, Fenzhuo Guo, Mengxuan Bai, Mengyan Li, Haifeng Dong, Fei Gao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22226v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22226_figures/2605.22226_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Geometric characterization. The set S of states not useful for teleportation is a convex set. For ρ /∈S, the closest point σ∗defines the distance D(ρ). The optimal supporting hyperplane at σ∗</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22226_figures/2605.22226_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Geometric distance D(ρW (p)) of the two-qubit Werner states as a function of p. The distance becomes nonzero at the critical point p = 1/3, indicating that the state becomes useful for teleportation.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22226_figures/2605.22226_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Geometric distance D(ρ(p)) of the maximally entangled mixed states as a function of p. The distance becomes nonzero at the critical point p = 0.25, indicating that the state becomes useful for teleportation.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22226_figures/2605.22226_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Geometric distance D(ρ(p)) of the NME states as a function of p. The distance becomes nonzero at the critical point p = 0.375, indicating that the state becomes useful for teleportation.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a geometric method to determine if a quantum state can provide a quantum advantage for teleportation. By calculating the shortest distance to a set of 'useless' states, the authors provide a necessary and sufficient criterion and a way to construct optimal witnesses for arbitrary two-qudit systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful tool for characterizing the operational utility of entanglement in higher-dimensional systems, which is essential for designing robust quantum networks and protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a systematic, constructive framework to identify whether an arbitrary two-qudit entangled state is useful for quantum teleportation, overcoming the limitations of existing criteria that are either restricted to qubits or only provide sufficient conditions.

**Main result.** The authors present a geometric method to construct optimal teleportation witnesses and establish that the shortest distance between a state and the set of useless states serves as a necessary and sufficient criterion for teleportation usefulness.

**Method.** A two-layer iterative cutting-plane algorithm is used to solve a convex semi-infinite programming problem, finding the projection of a target state onto the convex set of useless states via an outer-layer SDP and an inner-layer randomized fixed-point iteration.

**Model / system.** The study considers arbitrary bipartite two-qudit entangled states, specifically testing the method on two-qubit Werner states, two-qubit Maximally Entangled Mixed States (MEMS), and asymmetric two-qutrit non-maximally entangled states.

**Key observables.** Average teleportation fidelity, Maximal Entangled Fraction (MEF), and the geometric Frobenius distance D(rho) from the set of useless states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The visibility parameter p, the dimension of the qudit system d, and the critical threshold pc where teleportation advantage begins.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The inner optimization layer uses a randomized fixed-point iteration which may converge to a local optimum, potentially providing a lower bound on the MEF.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the geometric construction of the witness via a separating hyperplane; Figures 2, 3, and 4 show the geometric distance D(rho) as a function of visibility p for Werner, MEMS, and qutrit states, respectively, marking the onset of usefulness.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of teleportation usefulness, defines the geometric framework and the cutting-plane algorithm, presents the construction of the optimal witness, and validates the method through numerical applications to various two-qubit and two-qutrit state classes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Not all entangled states are useful for quantum teleportation. We present a geometric method to construct optimal teleportation witnesses, which provide operational necessary and sufficient criteria for identifying the teleportation usefulness of arbitrary two-qudit entangled states. Specifically, by developing a two-layer iterative cutting-plane algorithm to solve the shortest distance problem from the target state $ρ$ to the convex set $S$ of useless states, we obtain the projection point $σ^* \in S$ and then construct the optimal teleportation witness from the projection geometry. Moreover, the shortest distance $D(ρ)$ obtained during this construction also serves as a necessary and sufficient criterion for usefulness. We apply our method to identify the teleportation usefulness of three classes of entangled states.

</details>
