### [Self-testing of exact entanglement embezzlement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22713v1)

**Authors:** Samuel J. Harris  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22713v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22713_figures/2605.22713_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22713_figures/2605.22713_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22713_figures/2605.22713_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22713_figures/2605.22713_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22713_figures/2605.22713_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proves that exact entanglement embezzlement is a self-testing protocol for Cuntz isometries and a specific quasi-free state. It further characterizes the underlying von Neumann algebras as Type III factors, where the specific subtype is determined by the Schmidt coefficients of the embezzled state.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep mathematical insights into the resource theory of entanglement and the fundamental algebraic constraints required for exact entanglement manipulation, which is relevant to the study of infinite-dimensional quantum systems and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the self-testing properties and algebraic structure of exact bipartite entanglement embezzlement protocols.

**Main result.** It proves that exact entanglement embezzlement acts as a self-test for Cuntz isometries and a unique quasi-free state, and classifies the resulting von Neumann algebras as AFD Type III_lambda or Type III_1 factors.

**Method.** The author employs operator algebra techniques, including von Neumann algebra theory, Cuntz algebra properties, modular theory (Tomita-Takesaki), and GNS representations.

**Model / system.** A bipartite entanglement embezzlement protocol where two parties use a shared catalyst state psi in a von Neumann algebra M and its commutant M' to obtain a target entangled state phi via local unitaries or contractions.

**Key observables.** Schmidt coefficients of the target state, modular operator eigenvalues, and the Connes spectrum.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Schmidt coefficients (alpha_i), dimension (d), and the parameter lambda for Type III_lambda factors.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The target state phi has full Schmidt rank; the von Neumann algebra M is non-degenerate; the marginal states are faithful.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the bipartite embezzlement protocol and reducing it to a monopartite problem, to establishing the self-testing of Cuntz isometries via GNS representation, and finally using modular theory to classify the resulting von Neumann algebra types.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider bipartite exact entanglement embezzlement with a catalyst state vector $ψ$ in a Hilbert space $\mathcal{H}$ using unitaries (or more generally, contractions). If $\mathcal{M} \subseteq \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{H})$ is a von Neumann algebra and $U \in M_d \otimes \mathcal{M}$ and $V \in \mathcal{M}' \otimes M_d$ are unitaries (or more generally contractions), then such a protocol is of the form $(U \otimes I_d)(I_d \otimes V)(e_0 \otimes ψ\otimes e_0)=\sum_{i=0}^{d-1} α_i e_i \otimes ψ\otimes e_i$, where each $α_i>0$ and $\sum_{i=0}^{d-1} α_i^2=1$. We show that any such protocol must arise from a unique state on the tensor product $\mathcal{O}_d \otimes \mathcal{O}_d$ of the Cuntz algebra with itself. As a result, we prove that exact entanglement embezzlement is a self-test for a collection of $d$ Cuntz isometries for each party and a unique quasi-free state on the Cuntz algebra $\mathcal{O}_d$ in the sense of \cite{Iz93}. Moreover, we use modular theory to show that the von Neumann algebra generated by the copy of $\mathcal{O}_d$ is the unique separable approximately finite-dimensional Type $\text{III}_λ$ factor for some $0<λ\leq 1$, where $λ$ can be determined by an algebraic condition on the Schmidt coefficients of the state $\varphi=\sum_{i=0}^{d-1} α_i e_i \otimes e_i$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22392"></a>
### [The relative entropy of magic and its nonadditivity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22392v1)

**Authors:** Carolin Deckers, Justus Neumann, Hermann Kampermann, Dagmar Bruß  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22392v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22392_figures/2605.22392_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Bloch ball with stabilizer octahedron. The magic states are outside the stabilizer octahedron.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2: The purple lines are xρ(σ, t) for 0 &lt; t ≤tmax for some σ in the triangle spanned by s1, s2 and s3. The red circles represent the closest stabilizer states to the states on the purple line that they intersect. The stabilizer state at the center of the triangle is black. The intersection of the Bloch sphere and the purple line starting at the black dot is the T-state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22392_figures/2605.22392_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Magic states are represented by the lines starting at their closest stabilizer state (red circles) on the edge with vertices s1 and s3. The lines are the magic states for different values of c. For c = 1/ √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22392_figures/2605.22392_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Relative entropy of magic Rrel(ρ(σ, ϕ, tmax)) determined by Eq. (23) for stabilizer states in the triangle with the vertices s1, s2 and s3 including the hyperplanes with −b ≤c ≤0 for the states on the edges.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22392_figures/2605.22392_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Angle αd,n between the purple lines of Fig. 2b and the normal vector of the triangle n = (1, 1, 1)/ √</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a rigorous geometric characterization of magic states in the resource theory of magic and proves that the relative entropy of magic is nonadditive for multi-qubit systems. This finding is significant for understanding how quantum resources scale in stabilizer-based quantum computing architectures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the properties of the relative entropy of magic, specifically focusing on its geometric distribution in single-qubit systems and its nonadditivity in multi-qubit tensor product states.

**Main result.** The authors prove that the relative entropy of magic is nonadditive for an infinite class of n-qubit states where the magic states and their closest stabilizer states do not commute. They also characterize the symmetric arrangement of magic states around the faces of the stabilizer octahedron.

**Method.** The study uses the resource theory of magic, employing geometric approaches in the Bloch ball, analytical derivations using the Hadamard product, and a proof by contradiction to establish nonadditivity.

**Model / system.** The framework is the resource theory of magic applied to single-qubit and multi-qubit systems, where the set of free states is defined by the stabilizer polytope (the convex hull of stabilizer states).

**Key observables.** Relative entropy of magic (R_rel)

**Important parameters / regimes.** The parameter t representing the distance from a stabilizer state, the Bloch vector length, and the commutation relation between magic and stabilizer states.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The proof of nonadditivity assumes a specific class of states where at least two single-qubit magic states do not commute with their closest stabilizer states.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the stabilizer octahedron in the Bloch ball; Fig 2 and 3 illustrate the trajectories and tilting of magic states relative to stabilizer states on facets and edges; Fig 4 shows the distribution of relative entropy values on a facet; Fig 5 shows the linear correlation between the angle of inclination and the distance to T-states.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the resource theory and single-qubit geometry, moves to the analytical characterization of magic states and their symmetry, then presents a formal proof of the nonadditivity of the relative entropy of magic for multi-qubit systems, and concludes with conjectures and open questions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In most stabilizer-based quantum computing schemes, so-called magic states are a necessary resource for implementing non-transversal quantum gates. With the resource theory of magic, it is possible to analyze and quantify the generation of the non-stabilizer states. The relative entropy is a measure used in various resource theories. For single qubits, we characterize magic states and their closest stabilizer states by applying analytical results known from the relative entropy of entanglement and show that the magic states and their closest stabilizer states are arranged symmetrically around the states at the centers of the faces of the stabilizer octahedron. For tensor products of single-qubit states, we prove analytically that the relative entropy of magic is nonadditive in almost all cases.

</details>

### [Towards a quantum decision tree in a laser pumped four-level system](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21756v1)

**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21756v1>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a method to simulate quantum decision trees using a four-level atomic system driven by laser pulses. By controlling pulse amplitudes, the researchers can redistribute populations to mimic decision branches, offering a scalable approach for quantum-enhanced decision-making and optimization.

**Why it may be interesting.** It bridges quantum optics and quantum information by showing how specific atomic level configurations and pulse sequences can be engineered to perform algorithmic tasks like decision tree traversal, while also addressing the impact of environmental noise on quantum coherences.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a framework to implement quantum decision trees using a laser-driven four-level atomic system to achieve computational advantages over classical decision-making.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that controlled population redistribution in a diamond-shaped four-level system can effectively simulate the logic of a quantum decision tree, with potential scalability to N-level systems.

**Method.** The authors employ Lie-algebraic formalism (SU(4) group) and the Alhassid-Levine method to analyze system dynamics via a 15-dimensional vector representation of the density matrix.

**Model / system.** A four-level atomic system in a diamond-shaped configuration where states |0>, |1>, |2>, and |3> are coupled by pump lasers (alpha) and Stokes pulses (beta). The dynamics are governed by a time-dependent Hamiltonian under the Rotating Wave Approximation and a Liouville master equation.

**Key observables.** Population probabilities (diagonal elements of the density matrix) and coherences (off-diagonal elements).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pulse amplitudes and temporal profiles (alpha_j(t), beta_j(t)), detunings, and decoherence rates (pure dephasing and population relaxation).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is initially in a pure ground state |0>; detunings are assumed to be equal; and the model considers the specific case of a diamond-shaped energy configuration.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows an OR gate decision tree; Figure 2 presents a truth table; Figure 3 illustrates the diamond-shaped atomic coupling; Figure 4 shows time evolution of populations and coherences in an isolated system; Figure 5 shows the impact of dissipation; Figure 6 visualizes the proposed quantum tree.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining classical decision logic to proposing a quantum-mechanical emulation, establishing the SU(4) mathematical framework, analyzing the dynamics of isolated and dissipative systems, and discussing scalability and potential implementation in rare-earth-ion-doped crystals.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this study, we examine an innovative framework towards implementing quantum decision trees utilizing a laser-driven four-level system. We discuss a diamond-shaped atomic configuration, in which we apply Lie-algebraic formalisms to analyze the dynamics of the system. The system is perturbed by a Stokes pulse, represented as $β_j(t)$ (for $j=1,2$), which interacts with the atomic states $|0\rangle, |3\rangle$ and $|1\rangle, |2\rangle$. In addition, a pump laser, denoted as $α_j(t)$, couples the states $|0\rangle, |1\rangle$ and $|2\rangle, |3\rangle$. By employing pulse profiles that possess identical temporal behavior but differ in amplitude, one can effectively redistribute the population from the initial ground state to the other energy levels. This technique facilitates the mimicry of a quantum decision tree. We highlight that the proposed methodology is scalable to N-level systems, enhancing its adaptability and potential utility in quantum computing and various decision-making applications. We introduce a novel framework for implementing quantum decision trees using a four-level laser-driven atomic system. Employing a diamond-shaped energy configuration, we analyze system dynamics through Lie-algebraic methods. Using pulse profiles with identical temporal structures but varying amplitudes, we achieve controlled population redistribution among energy levels, effectively simulating a quantum decision tree. This methodology is scalable to systems of \(N\) levels, offering potential applications in quantum computing and decision-making processes.

</details>

**Summary.** This work explores how the long-distance behavior of defects in quantum field theories is governed by universal symmetry principles. It establishes effective field theory descriptions for complex objects like chiral defects and giant vortices in superfluids. The results show that microscopic details are often irrelevant to the macroscopic dynamics of these systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep connection between high-energy symmetry principles (like CFT and duality) and the emergent universal dynamics in many-body systems like ultracold atomic gases and superfluids.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the universal aspects of defect dynamics in quantum field theories, specifically focusing on how symmetry principles determine the infrared properties of boundaries, impurities, and vortices.

**Main result.** Demonstrates that defect dynamics (such as chiral defects in Maxwell theory and giant vortices in superfluids) are universally determined by symmetry, identifying new effective theories like Warped Conformal Field Theory (WCFT) for rotating superfluids.

**Method.** Uses symmetry-based analysis, Renormalization Group (RG) flows, Operator Product Expansion (OPE), and effective field theory approaches including the epsilon expansion.

**Model / system.** The work covers a variety of models including Maxwell theory with topological duality defects, O(N) models, and non-relativistic systems like atomic quantum gases and rotating 2D superfluids.

**Key observables.** Scaling dimensions, conformal weights, bulk-to-defect OPE coefficients, displacement and tilt operators, and particle density profiles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** UV and IR limits, codimension, conformal weights, angular velocity, and the large angular momentum limit (giant vortex regime).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes translation symmetry perpendicular to the defect is broken and that scaling invariance is enhanced to conformal symmetry at fixed points.

**Figures summary.** Visuals include configurations of various defects, RG and phase diagrams, correlation functions, and physical profiles of particle density and eigenmodes.

**Paper structure.** The dissertation progresses from a general framework of defect dynamics in QFT to specific applications in Maxwell theory, O(N) models, and finally to non-relativistic systems like quantum gases and rotating superfluids.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Defects are both physically rich objects and powerful tools in modern quantum field theory. They are extended operators, such as boundaries, impurities, and probe particles, embedded in many-body systems. In this dissertation, we study the universal aspects of defect dynamics from the perspective of symmetry principles. We bring together several themes, including defect renormalization group flows, effective string theory, and impurities in atomic quantum gases.

</details>

**Authors:** Seyed Navid Elyasi, Seyed Morteza Ahmadian, Rui Lin, Paolo Monti

<sub>Fig. 1. (a)–(c) Ring, line, and star QDC topologies. (d) Entanglement distribution via SWAP oper- ations. (e) Simulation layers: circuit, configuration, and experimental layers. (f) Collisional noise model for transduction and fiber effects.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22267_figures/2605.22267_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This paper uses a digital quantum computer to simulate different network architectures for quantum data centers. It finds that a star topology is the most efficient for balancing hardware cost with high-fidelity entanglement distribution. The study specifically highlights that transduction noise is the most significant hurdle for large-scale quantum interconnects.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics as it quantifies how environmental coupling and photon loss impact the scalability of distributed quantum networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Evaluating the trade-off between interconnection cost and fidelity in different Quantum Data Center (QDC) topologies, specifically addressing how transduction and fiber noise impact distributed quantum computing.

**Main result.** The star topology provides the optimal trade-off between cost and fidelity, outperforming ring and line topologies by minimizing noise accumulation and SWAP operations. Transduction noise was identified as the primary driver of fidelity degradation.

**Method.** The study uses a digital quantum computer (IBM Quantum Torino) to emulate optically interconnected QPUs using a three-layer simulation framework (circuit, configuration, and experimental layers) and a collisional model for noise.

**Model / system.** The system consists of interconnected QPUs in ring, star, and line topologies, each containing processing, communication, and flying qubits. The noise is modeled via a collisional model with an interaction Hamiltonian between communication qubits and an environment qubit to simulate transduction and fiber loss.

**Key observables.** GHZ-state fidelity, RCNOT gate performance, and communication cost (number of entanglement links).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transduction coupling strength (kappa = 0.5), fiber attenuation constant (0.0392 km^-1), and topology-specific link counts.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The noise is modeled using a Markovian evolution approach where the environment qubit is reset after each iteration.

**Figures summary.** Fig. 1 illustrates the QDC topologies, the simulation framework, and the collisional noise model; Fig. 2 details the GHZ-state circuit and the cat-comm protocol for RCNOT.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QDC scalability problem, describes the physical model and noise implementation, details the emulation framework on IBM hardware, presents the comparative results of different topologies, and concludes with a cost-fidelity analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We emulate optically interconnected quantum processors in ring, star, and line topologies using a quantum computer. GHZ benchmarks show that the star provides the best trade-off between cost and fidelity under transduction and fiber noise.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22807"></a>
### [How many systems can be dephased before the quantum switch becomes causally definite?](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22807v1)

**Authors:** Yassine Benhaj, Kuntal Sengupta, Cyril Branciard  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22807v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22807_figures/2605.22807_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Any bipartite process matrix in which the global past P and both parties’ input and output systems are fully dephased becomes causally separable, even if the global future F is left coherent. The red diamonds illus- trate dephasing in a fixed basis, which makes their incom- ing and outgoing systems effectively classical (double- stroke wires).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22807_figures/2605.22807_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Three explicit constructions derived from a standard quantum switch showing that causal indefiniteness can survive when all but one systems are fully dephased – as long as the single remaining coherent system is not F. (Left) Only the past control system remains coherent: the past is decomposed as P = Pc ⊗Pt, with the target state fixed to |0⟩Pt while the control Pc remains open; both internal parties’ input and output systems are dephased in the computational (Z) basis (red diamonds), the future target Ft is traced out, and the future control Fc is dephased in the X basis (yellow diamond). (Middle) Only one party’s input system AI remains coherent: the past state is fixed to |+,...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores the limits of how much decoherence a quantum process with indefinite causal order can tolerate. It proves that as long as at least one system other than the 'future' system remains coherent, the process can maintain its non-classical, causally nonseparable nature. This demonstrates that indefinite causal order is surprisingly robust to the dephasing of multiple subsystems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum information as it defines the fundamental limits of how much environmental noise (decoherence) a quantum-controlled causal structure can withstand before losing its non-classical advantages.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the robustness of causal nonseparability (indefinite causal order) against dephasing/decoherence, specifically determining how many quantum systems can be dephased before a process becomes causally separable.

**Main result.** The authors show that causal nonseparability persists if any single system other than the future system remains undephased, but vanishes if all systems are dephased or if only the future system remains coherent.

**Method.** The study uses the process matrix framework and Choi representation to analyze bipartite and multipartite processes, employing dephasing maps and semidefinite programming to test for causal nonseparability.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the Quantum Switch, a paradigmatic process with indefinite causal order, analyzed within the framework of bipartite and N-partite quantum circuits with quantum control (QC-QCs).

**Key observables.** Causal nonseparability vs. causal separability, and the presence or absence of quantum coherence in specific input/output slots.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dephasing regimes (computational vs. diagonal bases) and the specific subsystems (past, future, input, and output) being subjected to decoherence.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The proofs rely on the assumption that specific subsystems are dephased in predefined bases and focus on the class of quantum circuits with quantum control.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the transition to causal separability when all systems or only the future system are coherent; Figure 2 shows explicit constructions where nonseparability persists despite high levels of dephasing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of causal nonseparability robustness, provides a mathematical proof for the bipartite case involving global past and future, generalizes these findings to N-partite quantum circuits via induction, and concludes with explicit examples of partially dephased processes that retain indefinite causal order.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum processes with indefinite causal order -- so-called causally nonseparable processes -- can exhibit various advantages over quantum circuits with a fixed or a well-defined causal structure. A natural question is how much nonclassicality is required for a process to display causal nonseparability. Here we address this by investigating how many systems can be dephased (or decohered) before this property vanishes. First, for bipartite processes with open past and future we show that if all systems are dephased, or if only the future system is kept undephased, then the process becomes causally separable. However, if any single system other than the future system remains undephased, then there exist processes that retain causal nonseparability. Next, we demonstrate a similar behaviour in the multipartite case, when restricted to the physically motivated class of quantum circuits with quantum control (QC-QCs). Namely, dephasing all systems or keeping only the future system undephased renders any QC-QC causally separable; while causal nonseparability can persist if any non-future system is left undephased.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22316"></a>
### [Possible Topological Decoherence Transition in Relativistic Electron Beams Propagating through Coulomb-Disordered Media](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22316v1)

**Authors:** Yury A. Budkov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22316v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22316_figures/2605.22316_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic illustration of the emergence of an effective two-dimensional field theory for electron decoherence in a Coulomb-disordered medium and the possible Berezinskii–Kosterlitz–Thouless (BKT) scenario. (a) Relativistic electron propagation through a fluctuating Coulomb environment characterized by the screened correlation function K(r) = C</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that electron beams passing through disordered media undergo a topological transition similar to the BKT transition. As the sample thickness increases, the system moves from algebraic to exponential decoherence due to the proliferation of phase vortices. This effect is potentially observable in liquid-cell or cryo-electron microscopy.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates how a 3D disordered medium can give rise to an emergent 2D topological phase transition (BKT) in the coherence properties of a probe beam, bridging disordered systems and topological physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the asymptotic behavior of mutual coherence in relativistic electron beams passing through Coulomb-disordered media and determining if a BKT-type topological phase transition occurs as a function of sample thickness.

**Main result.** The study identifies a critical sample thickness Lc that separates a regime of algebraic decoherence from one of exponential decoherence driven by the proliferation of free vortices.

**Method.** The author uses the eikonal approximation and a paraxial reduction of the Dirac equation, followed by a renormalization-group analysis of a 2D Coulomb gas model.

**Model / system.** A relativistic electron beam propagating through a 3D classical one-component plasma (Coulomb-disordered medium) such as aqueous electrolytes or cryogenic samples, modeled via a 2D Gaussian free-field action.

**Key observables.** Mutual coherence function gamma(rho) and phase structure function Dphi(rho).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical thickness Lc, effective stiffness K, propagation length L, and the Debye screening length lambda_D.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The phase is assumed to be compact (phi = phi + 2pi) to allow for vortices, and the potential is treated as a static Gaussian random potential.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of electron propagation through a fluctuating environment, the resulting logarithmic phase correlation, the mapping to a 2D Gaussian free field, and the introduction of vortex configurations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical problem of electron beam decoherence, derives an effective 2D field theory from the eikonal approximation, applies renormalization-group analysis to a vortex gas model, and concludes with experimental implications for TEM.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the mutual coherence of a relativistic electron beam in a Coulomb-disordered medium is governed by an effective two-dimensional compact phase field with a logarithmic correlation function. The corresponding Gaussian free-field action exhibits a stiffness inversely proportional to the propagation length. When the compact nature of the phase is taken into account, the system supports vortex excitations that interact as a two-dimensional Coulomb gas. Renormalization-group analysis of this gas indicates the existence of a critical sample thickness $L_c$ at which a Berezinskii--Kosterlitz--Thouless (BKT) transition may occur, separating a regime of algebraic decoherence from one where free vortices proliferate and coherence is destroyed exponentially. The critical thickness is expressed through fundamental microscopic parameters and could be observed in transmission electron microscopy of liquid cells or cryogenic samples.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21933"></a>
### [Thermodynamic Irreversibility of Training Algorithms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21933v1)

**Authors:** Liu Ziyin, Yuanjie Ren, Adam Levine, Isaac Chuang  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21933v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21933_figures/2605.21933_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. All four potentials agree with each other at a small learning rate. As the learning rates increase, they start to deviate, but still have the qualitative agreement that a higher learning rate corresponds to a higher potential, signaling that they are all qualitatively good metrics of irreversibility of the training process.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21933_figures/2605.21933_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Anomalous fluctuation effect in transformer training dynamics. There is a qualitative distinction between the effect of changing the learning rate in a simple linear model (perceptron) and in nonlinear models such as transformers. In linear models, the classical optimization theory predicts an increase in the fluctuation of the gradient due to increased instability [22] – because there is a single local minimum, and so it is impossible for the model to move to a different, low-fluctuation model. In sharp contrast, the nonlinear-model experiments reported here show lower update fluctuation at larger learning rate, in agreement with the prediction of the irreversibility theory.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes that different ways of measuring the irreversibility of AI training algorithms are mathematically equivalent at small learning rates. It demonstrates that the error introduced by discrete training steps creates an emergent force that pushes models toward more stable and robust regions of the loss landscape.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper provides a rigorous mapping between the discrete-time optimization of neural networks and non-equilibrium stochastic thermodynamics, offering a way to treat learning dynamics as a driven particle system with emergent potentials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize the thermodynamic irreversibility of deep learning training algorithms and understand how discretization errors act as an emergent force in the optimization landscape.

**Main result.** The authors prove that four different measures of irreversibility (discretization error, time-renormalization, time-reversal asymmetry, and entropy production) are equivalent to leading order in the learning rate. They also show that this irreversibility generates an emergent force that drives training toward stable, low-fluctuation regions.

**Method.** The work uses backward error analysis, renormalization group (RG) approaches to time-axis coarse-graining, and stochastic thermodynamics. Numerical validation is performed using Transformers, RNNs, and linear perceptrons.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as a discrete-time stochastic Langevin process for neural network parameters, where the update rule is defined by a vector field (e.g., SGD or Adam) with a learning rate eta.

**Key observables.** Discretization error potential (phi_DE), time-reversal asymmetry (phi_TA), stochastic-thermodynamic entropy production (phi_ST), and update energy (eU_s).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Learning rate (eta), noise scale (sigma), and the Jacobian of the update field (J_U).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The update field is assumed to be smooth (C2 or C3) and possess a symmetric Jacobian (J_U = J_U^T). The analysis focuses on the small learning rate limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the qualitative and quantitative agreement between the four irreversibility potentials at small learning rates. Figure 2 illustrates 'anomalous fluctuations' in Transformers, where larger learning rates lead to lower update fluctuations.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes a theoretical framework for irreversibility, proves the equivalence of four different metrics using backward error analysis and RG, analyzes the symmetry-breaking properties of the resulting emergent force, and provides numerical experiments on various neural architectures to validate the theory.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The training algorithms for AI systems all introduce far-from-equilibrium dynamical processes, and understanding the irreversibility of these algorithms is a fundamental step towards understanding the learning dynamics of modern AI systems. In this work, we establish a general framework for defining and analyzing the irreversibility of training algorithms. We show that four different ways to characterize the irreversibility of dynamical processes are equivalent to leading order in the step size $η$: numerical backward error $φ_{\rm DE}$, time-renormalized correction $φ_{\rm TR}$, microscopic time reversal asymmetry $φ_{\rm TA}$, and the (regularized) stochastic-thermodynamic entropy production $φ_{\rm ST}$. The irreversibility gives rise to a time-reversal-symmetry-breaking emergent force that generically breaks non-isometric continuous reparametrization symmetries, preserves orthogonal symmetries, and leads to a universal preference for those learning trajectories that minimize the entropy production rate.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>Figure 4. (left) Angular-momentum Wigner distribution [72] of the CSS | 𝜋</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21643_figures/2605.21643_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. MZI phase uncertainty 𝑁Δ𝜙2 normalized to SNL (red) and susceptible to velocity selectivity and parasitic diffraction to the adjacent momentum classes in dependence on the coupling strength 𝜀= Ω0/𝜔𝑘. The sphere shows the input equator-OAT state |𝜒−𝛼0⟩ for 30 atoms. The uncertainty is shown for numerically optimized twisting for optimal performance in an ideal MZI 𝜒0 ≈1.61 × 10−3, and 𝑁= 2 × 104 atoms that initially have a Gaussian momentum distribution of width 𝜎𝑞that is centered at the respective momentum class’ center. The uncertainty was calculated by determining the interferometric contributions from Tab. II up to the second order in 𝜀. Dashed lines show two special cases: (i)...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how the competing effects of parasitic diffraction and velocity selectivity limit the precision of atom interferometers. It shows that by balancing pulse duration and optimizing the orientation of entangled states, one can maintain sub-shot-noise sensitivity despite these loss mechanisms.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous analytical framework for understanding how fundamental atom-optics imperfections limit quantum-enhanced metrology, offering specific strategies like 'signal cropping' and state optimization to recover sensitivity.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the degradation of phase sensitivity in atom interferometry caused by the trade-off between parasitic diffraction in the quasi-Bragg regime and velocity selectivity in long-pulse regimes.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that sub-shot-noise sensitivity is achievable in an intermediate pulse duration regime and that the deleterious effects of higher-order diffraction can be mitigated by optimizing the input quantum state (e.g., OAT state inclination).

**Method.** A second-quantized perturbative approach using a Dyson series expansion and adiabatic elimination is used to derive the time evolution of momentum-class field operators.

**Model / system.** A Mach-Zehnder interferometer (MZI) using a two-level atomic ensemble subject to first-order Bragg diffraction with counterpropagating lasers, modeled via a multi-mode bosonic Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Interference signal (atom-number difference), phase uncertainty, atom loss, and transmission/reflection coefficients.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rabi frequency (coupling strength epsilon), pulse duration, Doppler detuning, twisting strength (chi), and momentum distribution width.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The lasers are far-detuned from the atomic transition (allowing adiabatic elimination), the excited state is only virtually populated, and atom-atom interactions are negligible.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental setup and energy-momentum diagrams for Bragg diffraction; Fig 2 shows the MZI space-time diagram; Fig 3 compares full vs. cropped signals and spatial distributions; Fig 4 shows Wigner distributions for CSS and OAT states; Fig 8 and 9 show phase uncertainty comparisons and optimization results.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Bragg diffraction trade-off, develops a second-quantized perturbative framework for the MZI, analyzes the impact of velocity selectivity and parasitic loss, evaluates quantum-enhanced states (CSS and OAT), and concludes with optimization strategies for pulse shape and state preparation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin squeezing in atomic ensembles enables atom interferometry with sensitivities below the shot-noise limit, but the associated entanglement is highly susceptible to loss, making imperfections in atom optics a central limitation. Bragg diffraction is an established technique for driving transitions between atomic momentum states and enables large-momentum transfer through higher-order diffraction while preserving the internal state. However, it is intrinsically limited by two competing mechanisms: short light pulses induce parasitic diffraction into off-resonant orders beyond an effective two-level description, while long pulses face velocity selectivity. We derive analytical expressions in a second-quantized framework for the atom optics and phase uncertainty of a Mach-Zehnder interferometer including these effects. We demonstrate that sub-shot-noise scaling is achieved only in a regime of intermediate pulse duration. Furthermore, we show that deleterious effects of higher-order diffraction are partially mitigated by optimizing the input quantum state.

</details>

### [Three sins against physics by an exaggerated quantum information perspective](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22218v1)

**Authors:** Valerio Scarani  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22218v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22218_figures/2605.22218_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper critiques the 'quantum information perspective' for potentially distorting our understanding of physics. It argues that focusing too heavily on gates, ignoring Hamiltonians, and treating nature as an adversary can lead to incorrect physical conclusions. The work serves as a foundational reminder to ground quantum information theory in underlying physical reality.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a critical perspective on the foundational assumptions used in quantum optics and quantum information, specifically regarding the physical reality of quantum resources versus classical wave phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The author addresses how an over-reliance on the quantum information perspective can lead to physical distortions, specifically by over-abstracting reality through gates, ignoring Hamiltonians, and adopting an adversarial view of nature.

**Main result.** The paper identifies three 'sins': confusing classical mode coherence with quantum state coherence, focusing on unitary gates while ignoring their underlying Hamiltonians, and treating physical discovery as an adversarial game.

**Method.** The author uses conceptual and philosophical analysis of quantum information frameworks, such as resource theories, nonlocal games, and quantum circuits, contrasted with fundamental principles of optics and quantum mechanics.

**Model / system.** The discussion covers optical systems (linear and non-linear interferometry, Maxwell's equations), quantum information models (qubits, Clifford gates, Fock space), and the relationship between unitary evolutions and Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** First-order coherence (g1), second-order coherence (g2), photon counting statistics, and Bell nonlocality.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Single-photon regime (energy thresholds), Clifford operations, and the distinction between adversarial and non-adversarial settings.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The author critiques the assumption that an adversarial 'game' framework is the most appropriate way to understand the ontology of quantum mechanics.

**Paper structure.** The paper is structured around three main arguments (the 'sins'), followed by a concluding reflection on the philosophical implications of the adversarial view in experimental physics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

I point out three ways in which the perspective of quantum information may lead to distorted claims about physics: forgetting that light does not need to be quantised to show coherence; ignoring the generators of unitary evolutions; and approaching the discovery of nature as a fight against an adversary.

</details>

### [Device-Independent Quantum Secret Sharing Protocol Enhanced by Advantage Distillation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21880v1)

**Authors:** Yong-Hui Yang, Jian-Hong Shi, Hong-Wei Li, Hai-Long Zhang, Yun-Teng Yang, Yu-Bing Zhu, Yan-Yang Zhou  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21880v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21880_figures/2605.21880_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic of the DI-QSS protocol. A central source prepares a large number of identical three-photon polarization-entangled GHZ states, which are then divided into three sequences, denoted as E1, E2 and E3. The photons in these three sequences are distributed to three remote users, Alice, Bob and Charlie, respectively. Each user independently chooses a measurement basis and obtains a measurement outcome of -1, +1 or no-click event (⊥).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21880_figures/2605.21880_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Flowchart of the tripartite advantage distillation pro- cedure. This figure illustrates the post-processing procedure of the advantage distillation technique in a tripartite DI-QSS protocol.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21880_figures/2605.21880_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Comparison of the secret sharing rates (r) before and after advantage distillation under different fidelities. Dashed and solid lines respectively represent the protocol perfor- mance before and after advantage distillation under various fidelities. It can be intuitively observed that after applying advantage distillation, the η−r curves under all fidelities shift upward significantly</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21880_figures/2605.21880_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Three-dimensional plot of the secret sharing rate (r) versus the global detection efficiency (η) and fidelity (F) before and after applying advantage distillation. The blue surface (without advantage distillation) lies entirely below the red surface (with advantage distillation), indicating that the advantage distillation technique significantly enhances the secret sharing rate for any given combination of η and F. More importantly, the red surface maintains a positive secret sharing rate (r &gt; 0) even within regions of lower global detection rate and lower fidelity.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21880_figures/2605.21880_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 6. Three-dimensional surfaces showing the relationship between the secret sharing rate, bit-flip rate, and fidelity. The blue surface represents the protocol performance with advan- tage distillation, while the purple surface corresponds to the original protocol performance. The plot demonstrates that for any given fidelity F, the advantage distillation technique significantly enhances the protocol’s ability to generate secure secrets under higher bit-flip noise.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes an enhanced device-independent quantum secret sharing protocol by adapting the advantage distillation technique to a three-party setting. The method significantly improves noise tolerance and extends the maximum secure communication distance over optical fibers. It demonstrates that integrating distillation with post-selection and noise pre-processing makes DI-QSS more practical for real-world quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum networks as it provides a robust method to enhance the performance of multipartite entanglement-based protocols against realistic environmental decoherence and photon loss.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The practical implementation of Device-Independent Quantum Secret Sharing (DI-QSS) is severely limited by high requirements for detection efficiency, low noise tolerance, and short communication distances due to channel loss.

**Main result.** By extending the advantage distillation technique to a three-party setting, the authors significantly increased noise tolerance (from 10.17% to 28.49%) and extended the maximum secure fiber distance (from 0.16 km to 1.85 km).

**Method.** The authors redesigned a tripartite data interaction and verification procedure for advantage distillation and systematically integrated it with three strategies: noise pre-processing, post-selection, and a combination of both.

**Model / system.** A three-party DI-QSS protocol involving Alice, Bob, and Charlie connected to a central source distributing three-photon polarization-entangled GHZ states.

**Key observables.** Svetlichny polynomial (S_ABC), Quantum Bit Error Rate (QBER), secret sharing rate (r), global detection efficiency (eta), and entanglement fidelity (F).

**Important parameters / regimes.** GHZ state fidelity (F), global detection efficiency (eta), fiber attenuation (0.2 dB/km), and bit-flip probability (q).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The security analysis assumes Eve obeys the laws of quantum physics and focuses on collective attacks; the protocol relies on the violation of the Svetlichny inequality.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the protocol schematic; Figure 2 illustrates the advantage distillation flowchart; Figure 3 shows secret sharing rate vs. efficiency; Figure 4 is a 3D plot of rate vs. efficiency and fidelity; Figure 9 and 10 show performance vs. transmission distance under different noise levels.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the DI-QSS problem, describes the proposed tripartite advantage distillation framework, details three specific improvement strategies, presents numerical simulations comparing these strategies, and concludes with future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Device-independent quantum secret sharing (DI-QSS) provides high security by eliminating the need to trust devices, yet its practical performance is limited by channel loss and noise. This work extends advantage distillation from two-party quantum key distribution (QKD) to three-party DI-QSS, redesigning the corresponding data interaction and verification procedures. The technique is systematically applied to the basic protocol and three active improvement strategies: noise preprocessing, post-selection, and their combination. This approach enhances noise tolerance, reduces the required global detection efficiency threshold, and significantly extends the maximum secure communication distance. Numerical simulations demonstrate that for the basic protocol over fiber, the maximum secure distance increases from 0.16 km to 1.85 km, and the noise tolerance improves from 10.17% to 28.49%. The results show that generalizing advantage distillation to the three-party setting effectively strengthens the protocol's robustness and practicality, enhancing its adaptability to realistic noise and advancing the development of more reliable quantum secret sharing systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22439"></a>
### [Minimal Permutation-Invariant Qudit Codes from Edge-Colorings of Complete Graphs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22439v1)

**Authors:** Eric Kubischta, Ian Teixeira  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22439v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Rydberg arrays` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22439_figures/2605.22439_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22439_figures/2605.22439_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22439_figures/2605.22439_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22439_figures/2605.22439_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22439_figures/2605.22439_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes that four physical qudits are both necessary and sufficient to encode one logical qudit with distance two within the symmetric subspace. The authors provide a universal construction for any dimension q by mapping the quantum error-correction requirements to combinatorial edge-coloring problems on complete graphs.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum error correction in systems with inherent symmetries, such as trapped ions or Rydberg atoms, where permutation-invariant noise models are physically motivated.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the minimum block length n required to construct permutation-invariant (PI) quantum codes that can encode one logical qudit of dimension q with distance d=2.

**Main result.** The authors prove that n=4 is the minimum block length required and provide a general construction for ((4, q, 2))_q codes for all q >= 2 using graph-theoretic edge-colorings.

**Method.** The study uses representation theory of SU(q), linear programming constraints, and a combinatorial reduction of the Knill-Laflamme conditions to edge-coloring problems on complete graphs.

**Model / system.** The system consists of n qudits of local dimension q restricted to the symmetric subspace (permutation-invariant subspace) of the total Hilbert space.

**Key observables.** Knill-Laflamme error-detection conditions, weight/occupation numbers, and Cartan/root operator expectation values.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local dimension q, block length n, code distance d=2, and code dimension K=q.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The codes are restricted to the symmetric subspace and are designed specifically to detect single-site (one-body) traceless errors.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the problem of PI codes, uses linear programming to prove the non-existence of codes for n <= 3, then develops a construction for n=4 by decomposing error conditions into root and Cartan parts, and finally uses graph theory (edge-coloring and 1-factorization) to provide explicit constructions for both odd and even q.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study permutation-invariant quantum codes in the symmetric subspace $\mathrm{Sym}^n(\mathbb{C}^q) $ of $n$ qudits of local dimension $q$. For every integer $q\geq 2$, we construct a permutation-invariant code with parameters $((4,q,2))_q$. Thus four physical qudits suffice to encode one logical qudit with distance two in the symmetric sector for every local dimension. We also show, using linear-programming constraints for permutation-invariant quantum codes, that no permutation-invariant code of dimension $q$ and distance at least $2$ exists in $\mathrm{Sym}^n(\mathbb{C}^q)$ for $n\leq 3$. Hence four qudits are necessary and sufficient.   The construction has a simple representation-theoretic and combinatorial description. In the irreducible $\mathrm{SU}(q)$-module $\mathrm{Sym}^4(\mathbb{C}^q)$, the distance-two Knill-Laflamme conditions split into root and Cartan parts. By restricting supports to the even-entry occupation layer, all root-error conditions vanish automatically. The remaining Cartan conditions reduce to linear balancing constraints on packets of occupation vectors. These packets admit a natural graph-theoretic interpretation in terms of the vertices and edges of the complete graph $K_q$: for odd $q$, they are organized by the midpoint rule, while for even $q$, they are organized by a decomposition of $K_q$ into perfect matchings. In this way, the existence of minimal $((4,q,2))_q$ permutation-invariant codes is reduced to a parity-dependent edge-coloring problem on $K_q$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22614"></a>
### [Nonlinear Magnon Magnetic Moment Transport in Triangular-Lattice f-Wave Antialtermagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22614v1)

**Authors:** Volodymyr P. Kravchuk, Kostiantyn V. Yershov, Bastián Pradenas, Robin R. Neumann, Rodrigo Jaeschke-Ubiergo, Ricardo Zarzuela, Jairo Sinova, Jeroen van den Brink, Alexander Mook

**Summary.** This paper identifies a new class of f-wave antialtermagnets where magnons carry a magnetic moment perpendicular to the spin plane. It predicts that these systems will exhibit unique nonlinear thermal transport phenomena, such as the spin-splitter and Edelstein effects, which could be detected via Lorentz microscopy.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates spin excitations (magnons) in a triangular-lattice Heisenberg antiferromagnet to demonstrate that they carry a magnetic moment perpendicular to the spin-ordering plane, identifying the system as an f-wave antialtermagnet.

**Main result.** The researchers show that magnons in this system possess an out-of-plane magnetic moment and predict nonlinear thermal transport signatures, specifically the spin-splitter and Edelstein effects, in 3D-stacked versions of the lattice.

**Method.** The study employs the Holstein-Primakoff formalism, Nambu spinor representation, and the Boltzmann transport equation within the relaxation-time approximation, supplemented by Landau-Lifshitz spin-lattice dynamics simulations.

**Model / system.** A 2D triangular-lattice Heisenberg antiferromagnet with a 120-degree ground state, extended to a 3D stack of antiferromagnetically coupled layers (an antialtermagnet) under an external magnetic field.

**Key observables.** Magnon magnetic moment, spin-splitting parameter, spin current (spin-splitter effect), and net magnetization (Edelstein effect).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exchange constants (J, Jz), magnetic field (B), temperature gradients (grad T), and the low-temperature/small-field limits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the harmonic approximation (linearized spin-wave theory), a constant temperature gradient, and the relaxation-time approximation for the Boltzmann equation.

**Figures summary.** Figures include schematics of the lattice, energy isolines and spin-splitting parameter distribution in the Brillouin zone, Zeeman-splitting of magnon modes, and dispersion relations under different magnetic field strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the 2D triangular-lattice model, analyzes the symmetry and magnetic moment of magnons, extends the model to a 3D stacked antialtermagnet, and concludes by deriving nonlinear thermal transport coefficients and experimental signatures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the spin excitations in the frustrated coplanar 120-degree ground state of the triangular-lattice Heisenberg antiferromagnet and demonstrate that they carry a magnetic moment perpendicular to the plane in which the spins order, despite the ground-state sublattice moments having no out-of-plane component. The symmetry of the momentum dependence of the magnetic moment and energy of the magnons renders the system an odd-parity f-wave magnet. Extending this model to a stack of antiferromagnetically coupled triangular layers provides a realization of magnons in a three-dimensional f-wave antialtermagnet. We show that nonlinear thermal transport effects of magnons, such as Edelstein and spin-splitter effects, provide clear experimental signatures of magnons in f-wave antialtermagnets.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21795"></a>
### [ATHENA: A Compiler For Optimized Scheduling In Distributed Quantum Computers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21795v1)

**Authors:** Won Joon Yun, Dhilan Nag, Sneha Ballabh, Jiapeng Zhao, Eneet Kaur, Poulami Das  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21795v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Rydberg arrays` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21795_figures/2605.21795_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. (a) Current compilers have limited lookahead between blocks and commit to the schedule with fewest teleportations for each block while discarding the rest. They also defer scheduling future gates until preceding blocks complete. (b) Athena considers useful future blocks in its lookahead window and retains multiple candidate schedules. It also executes future operations and their teleportation early if EPR capacity is available.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21795_figures/2605.21795_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. DQC compilation for (a) first two blocks on (b) a 3-chip DQC using the state-of-the-art compiler QuComm [23]. QuComm groups first five CNOTs into two blocks, 𝐵1 and 𝐵2. (c) It selects 𝑆2 and relocates qubits 0 and 4 to execute 𝐵1, exhausting its EPR resources. (d) To free EPRs, QuComm relocates qubit 0 from 𝑆2 to 𝑆1. (e) It then relocates qubit 1 to 𝑆2 for 𝐵2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21795_figures/2605.21795_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. (a) An example circuit with blocks 𝐵1 and 𝐵2. (b) Dependency for CNOT 𝐶is resolved after executing CNOT 𝐴. But QuComm does not schedule it until it reaches 𝐵2. The teleportation required is also scheduled only when the compiler realizes that it is necessary to schedule CNOT 𝐶. (c) In contrast, Athena relocates qubit 2 and schedules CNOT 𝐶as early as possible. This improves RELOCATE and CNOT concurrency and reduces overall program execution time.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21795_figures/2605.21795_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. QuComm selects Schedule-A for 𝐵1 because it costs fewer teleportations (illustrated by the box width in this Figure) than Schedule-B. But the qubit relocations from Schedule-A eventually cost more teleportations in the fu- ture block 𝐵10. In contrast, Athena also retains Schedule-B and continues to schedule subsequent blocks. This allows Athena to eventually arrive at a more optimized executable for block 𝐵10. This approach allows Athena to select block schedules that are locally sub-optimal but globally optimal (such as Schedule-B here).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21795_figures/2605.21795_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Overview of Athena. It takes as input the program and initial qubit mapping and mainly focuses on instruction scheduling. This involves block formation (grouping operations into instruction blocks), an utility-driven lookahead-based scheduling involving multiple candidates, and optimizing teleportations via EPR-capacity-aware early relocate scheduling.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents ATHENA, a new compiler designed to optimize instruction scheduling in distributed quantum computers. By using a multi-candidate scheduling approach and early resource allocation, it significantly reduces the latency and teleportation overheads that currently bottleneck distributed quantum architectures.

**Why it may be interesting.** It addresses the fundamental architectural challenge of scaling quantum computers via modularity, specifically focusing on the interplay between entanglement resources (EPR pairs) and gate-level scheduling in atom-based distributed systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Distributed Quantum Computers (DQCs) suffer from high latency and error rates due to inefficient scheduling of non-local CNOT gates, which require costly qubit teleportation and EPR resource management.

**Main result.** The ATHENA compiler reduces teleportations by 34% on average and reduces program latency by 2x on average compared to state-of-the-art compilers like QuComm.

**Method.** ATHENA uses Utility-driven Lookahead with Multi-Candidate Block Scheduling (UMS) to avoid early commitment to sub-optimal schedules and EPR-Capacity-Aware Early Scheduling (EES) to increase concurrency by scheduling future operations early.

**Model / system.** A multi-chip Distributed Quantum Computer (DQC) architecture, specifically modeled on neutral-atom platforms using dual-species ($^{8TL}$Rb and $^{171}$Yb) for compute and communication qubits, featuring nearest-neighbor chip connectivity and finite EPR capacity.

**Key observables.** Teleportation count, program latency, program fidelity, and effective teleportation count ($T_{eff}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** EPR capacity, latency-weighting parameter ($\alpha$), decay factor for lookahead ($eta$), and lookahead window size.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The lookahead window is limited to a manageable size (e.g., 4 blocks) to maintain computational tractability, and the solution tree is pruned to the top-$w$ candidates.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares current compilers to ATHENA's approach; Figure 2 shows the QuComm compiler's logic; Figure 3 compares latency between QuComm and ATHENA; Figure 4 illustrates the 'early commitment' problem; Figure 7 shows the utility-driven lookahead process; Figure 9 shows solution tree pruning; Figure 10 illustrates the EES mechanism.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of DQC scheduling inefficiencies, describes the ATHENA compiler architecture (including UMS and EES components), details the implementation on neutral-atom models, provides performance evaluations against the QuComm baseline using various quantum algorithms, and discusses scalability and future work.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Distributed Quantum Computers (DQCs) enable large system sizes by connecting smaller chips via photonic interconnects. DQCs use teleportation to relocate qubits and execute CNOTs between qubits on different chips. However, non-local CNOTs are 4.3-7.7$\times$ slower and 4$\times$ more error-prone than local CNOTs within a chip, which degrades program fidelities. Existing compilers group CNOTs with overlapping qubits into blocks and collectively optimize teleportations for each block. However, block-level scheduling has two key drawbacks. First, it lacks lookahead ability across blocks because it selects the optimal schedule for one block before proceeding to the next. As a result, it cannot assess the impact of a teleportation on future blocks. Our studies show that naively expanding the lookahead window to include subsequent blocks does not address this issue. Second, existing approaches do not schedule future block operations or the teleportations they require until preceding blocks are fully scheduled, introducing delay and latency overheads.   We propose ATHENA, a DQC compiler that addresses these limitations using two key insights: Utility-driven Lookahead with Multi-Candidate Block Scheduling (UMS) and EPR-Capacity-Aware Early Scheduling (EES). UMS schedules a block by considering only useful future blocks in its lookahead window. A future block has utility if it shares overlapping qubits with the current block being scheduled. UMS also maintains multiple schedules during compilation, allowing it to defer commitment to globally sub-optimal schedules early in the compilation process. EES enables ATHENA to schedule future operations and their relocations early when EPR resources are available. Our evaluations show that ATHENA reduces teleportations by 34% on average and up to 65%, and reduces latency by 2$\times$ on average and up to 2.9$\times$ compared to the state-of-the-art.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22758"></a>
### [A sharp interaction-degree threshold for simulating QAOA](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22758v1)

**Authors:** Ralfs Āboliņš, Andris Ambainis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22758v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22758_figures/2605.22758_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22758_figures/2605.22758_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22758_figures/2605.22758_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22758_figures/2605.22758_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22758_figures/2605.22758_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a sharp complexity threshold for simulating QAOA based on the interaction degree of the cost function. It proves that while degree-2 QAOA is efficiently simulatable, degree-3 QAOA is classically hard to sample from, providing a fundamental boundary for quantum advantage in optimization algorithms.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether there is a sharp threshold for the interaction degree of cost functions that determines the classical simulability of the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (QAOA).

**Main result.** The authors prove a sharp transition: at interaction degree 2, depth-p QAOA is classically simulatable in polynomial time for p = O(log n), whereas at degree 3, sampling from depth-1 QAOA is hard enough to collapse the polynomial hierarchy.

**Method.** The study uses complexity reductions from PostBQP to PostQAOA, circuit transformation techniques to bound interaction degrees, and the Markov-Shi algorithm for tensor network contraction.

**Model / system.** The model consists of depth-p QAOA circuits with 2-local cost functions defined on an interaction graph, utilizing a mixing operator B = sum of X_j.

**Key observables.** Marginal output probabilities and sampling distributions from the QAOA circuit.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction degree (specifically the transition between degree 2 and 3), circuit depth p, and multiplicative error c.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The hardness result for degree 3 assumes a multiplicative error model; the simulation efficiency for degree 2 assumes p = O(log n).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the circuit preprocessing technique, showing how intermediate Hadamard gates are replaced by diagonal couplings to auxiliary qubits to bound the interaction degree.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of interaction-degree thresholds, presents a preprocessing method for circuit transformation, proves the hardness of degree-3 sampling via complexity reduction, proves the tractability of degree-2 simulation using tensor network contraction, and discusses the distinction between sampling hardness and optimization advantage.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We identify a sharp interaction-degree threshold for the classical simulation of QAOA with $2$-local cost functions. At degree $3$, classical sampling from depth-$1$ QAOA with small multiplicative error would collapse the polynomial hierarchy to its third level. At degree $2$, exact classical sampling from depth-$p$ QAOA on $n$ qubits runs in time $n^{O(1)}$ whenever $p = O(\log n)$. The hard degree-$3$ instances have trivially optimizable cost functions, so sampling hardness does not by itself imply a quantum optimization advantage.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21844"></a>
### [A Utility-Driven Bounded-Confidence Model for Opinion Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21844v1)

**Authors:** Alex Siebenmorgen, Juan G. Restrepo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21844v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21844_figures/2605.21844_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

A single cluster forms and its mean opinion fluctuates around the two local maxima of U(x). (Right) Utility function U(x) (blue solid line) and empirical histogram for Xt (orange shaded area).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21844_figures/2605.21844_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. (Left panel) Bimodal utility function U(x) [see Eq. (3)]. (Right three panels) Empirical steady-state histograms (light blue shaded areas) and theoretical steady-state curves of the population mean X (orange solid curves) for N = 10, 15, 50 at fixed noise standard deviation ∆= 0.01 and learning rate µ = 1/2. The theoretical stationary density p∗(X) ∝U(X)a is obtained from Eq. (9). As N increases, the theory predicts and the simulations confirm localization of the density around the global utility maximum.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>FIG. 4. Numerically estimated average merging time Tm (blue circles) and the theoretical value from Eq. (19) (solid line). Error bars indicate the empirical standard deviation. Agreement is strongest in the weak-noise regime where the assumptions leading to Eq. (19) apply.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21844_figures/2605.21844_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Comparison of theoretical and empirical µ dependence with error bars corresponding to the empirical standard deviation. Approximate linearity in 1/(1 −µ) indicates alignment between theory and simulation.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops an analytical framework for opinion dynamics where the desirability of certain states drives social influence. It shows that the system behaves like a thermodynamic system with an effective temperature, allowing for the prediction of opinion polarization and cluster merging using tools from statistical mechanics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how a utility function (representing the desirability of certain states) influences the evolution, polarization, and clustering of opinions in a bounded-confidence social dynamics model.

**Main result.** The authors derive that the mean opinion follows a Gibbs-like stationary distribution proportional to U(x)^a, where the effective temperature is controlled by the system size and learning rate, and they provide analytical estimates for opinion switching and cluster merging times.

**Method.** The study uses a small-spread expansion to derive a low-dimensional stochastic differential equation (SDE) and employs the Fokker-Planck equation, Kramers escape theory, and Ornstein-Uhlenbeck processes for analytical descriptions.

**Model / system.** An agent-based model of N agents with continuous opinions in [0, 1], interacting via a bounded-confidence mechanism where updates are driven by an asymmetric rule based on a utility function U(x) and subject to additive noise.

**Key observables.** Mean opinion (Xt), cluster variance (sigma^2), stationary distribution (p*(x)), Mean First-Passage Time (MFPT) for switching, and cluster merging time (Tm).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Learning rate (mu), confidence bound (epsilon), number of agents (N), noise intensity (Delta), and the utility function landscape U(x).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes a single-cluster regime, a first-order expansion of the utility function around the mean, and that fluctuations are weakly anti-correlated.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows time series of mean opinion fluctuations and comparisons between utility and empirical histograms; Figure 2 illustrates utility functions; Figure 5 validates the theoretical scaling of escape times against simulation data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the utility-driven model, derives the SDE and stationary distribution for the single-cluster regime, analyzes metastability and switching in bimodal landscapes, calculates cluster merging dynamics, and validates results via numerical simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a utility-driven bounded-confidence model of opinion dynamics in which opinions associated with higher utility exert stronger social influence. In the regime where all agents belong to a single opinion cluster, we derive a stochastic differential equation for the mean opinion and show that its stationary distribution is Gibbs-like, with an effective potential determined by the utility landscape and an inverse temperature controlled by the learning rate and the number of agents. For multimodal utility functions, the dynamics exhibit metastability and spontaneous switching between competing opinion states. The reduced stochastic description also captures the evolution and merging of multiple opinion clusters, in agreement with agent-based simulations.

</details>
