<a id="paper-2605.21591"></a>
### [Algebraic locality and non-invertible Gauss laws](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21591v1)

**Authors:** Nicholas Holfester, Jonathan Sorce  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21591v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21591_figures/2605.21591_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: The magnetic constraint g1g2g3g4 = e at a four-valent vertex. If edge j were directed inward instead of outward, one would replace gj with g−1 j .</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21591_figures/2605.21591_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: A four-valent vertex with edge labels g, h, k, and ℓ. For the chosen orientations, the magnetic constraint is gh = kℓ. The green-tinted “g” and “ℓ” edges are in the region R.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21591_figures/2605.21591_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Two seven-valent vertices, with the edges of R colored in green. The left vertex is cusped, while the right is not.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21591_figures/2605.21591_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 6: If one tries to make a “continuum picture” of a cusped vertex by drawing a continuous region that includes all edges of R and excludes all edges of R′, this region must necessarily meet itself at a cusp, like in the right side of this figure.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21591_figures/2605.21591_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 7: Exchanging a region R for its collared version R+. Only one vertex of R is cusped, and this is the only one that gets new edges in R+.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how non-invertible symmetries, such as those found in string-net models, affect the locality of physical operators on a lattice. It demonstrates that while standard locality principles like Haag duality can fail due to geometric 'cusps,' they can be recovered using a modified definition of local regions. This provides a rigorous foundation for understanding the algebraic structure of topological order with non-invertible symmetries.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the validity of algebraic locality principles, specifically Haag duality and disjoint additivity, in lattice models subject to non-invertible Gauss laws.

**Main result.** The authors show that non-invertible magnetic constraints violate exact Haag duality for 'cusped' regions, but a weak form of duality is restored by adding a 'collar' to the region. Exact locality is recovered for 'cuspless' regions, such as those on trivalent lattices.

**Method.** The study employs the framework of von Neumann algebras and the representation theory of finite-dimensional C*-Hopf algebras to analyze operator algebras within constrained subspaces.

**Model / system.** The models studied are 2+1D lattice gauge theories, specifically quantum double models and extended string-net models, where a magnetic constraint is imposed exactly via a non-invertible symmetry.

**Key observables.** Haag duality, disjoint additivity, Wilson line operators, and character operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The presence of non-invertible (non-group-like) symmetries and the geometric properties of the lattice (cusped vs. cuspless regions).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to bosonic algebras, closed lattices, and finite-dimensional C*-Hopf algebras with an involutive antipode.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geometric action of Wilson lines, the difference between cusped and cuspless vertices, and the construction of 'collared' regions to restore duality.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-invertible Gauss laws, develops the Hopf-algebraic framework for magnetic constraints, provides counterexamples for Haag duality violation in cusped regions, proves weak duality/additivity for general regions, and demonstrates exact locality for trivalent lattices.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study algebraic locality principles on a 2+1D closed lattice in the presence of a Gauss law for a non-invertible symmetry. Prior work in arXiv:2509.03589 showed that when enforcing the Gauss law of an invertible symmetry, the principle of "Haag duality" is preserved exactly, and "disjoint additivity" is preserved after appropriate treatment of discreteness artifacts. Here we show that for a large class of non-invertible on-site symmetries, Haag duality is preserved exactly only for sufficiently nice, "cuspless" regions. For cusped regions, we instead have a weak form of Haag duality that requires adding a collar. Our results apply to double models with a purely magnetic constraint, and to the more general framework of constraints induced by the on-site action of a Hopf algebra. In particular, we treat a class of extended string-net models explicitly. We also demonstrate disjoint additivity for double models based on a group, and a weakened form of disjoint additivity in the setting of a general Hopf algebra.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21774"></a>
### [Conductivity of a Non-Galilean--Invariant Fermi Liquid: Exact Solution of the Kinetic Equation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21774v1)

**Authors:** Tatia Kiliptari, Vladimir I. Yudson, Dmitrii L. Maslov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21774v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21774_figures/2605.21774_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: (a) ee contribution to the dc conductivity of a disordered FL, σee(0, T), as a function of γT τi in units of e2 and for Ω2 pτi/εF = 100. Main panel: exact solution, Eq. (2b). Top and bottom insets: high- and low-temperature limits, respectively. (b) Optical conductivity Re σee(Ω, T) of a disorder-free FL as a function of Ω/γT , in units of e2 and for T = 0.1Ωp = 0.01εF. . In both panels, dotted (blue) and dashed (red) curves correspond to analytical results.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides an exact solution for the conductivity of a non-Galilean-invariant Fermi liquid under various interaction models. It corrects previous misconceptions by demonstrating that electron-electron interactions influence transport only through the quasiparticle lifetime. The work also provides a way to access the optical response in the hydrodynamic regime, which is otherwise inaccessible via perturbation theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to find an exact expression for the conductivity of a non-Galilean-invariant Fermi liquid across various temperature and frequency regimes, specifically clarifying the role of electron-electron interactions in current relaxation.

**Main result.** The authors derive an exact solution showing that electron-electron interactions affect conductivity solely through the quasiparticle scattering time, rather than a separate current relaxation rate, and provide the optical response in the hydrodynamic regime.

**Method.** The study solves the linearized semi-classical kinetic equation by mapping it onto an inhomogeneous Legendre-type differential equation and using the Eliashberg approximation for the Hertz-Millis model.

**Model / system.** A 2D non-Galilean-invariant Dirac system subject to both screened Coulomb interactions (treated via RPA) and $z=3$ Pomeranchuk critical interactions (Hertz-Millis model) in the presence of impurity scattering.

**Key observables.** DC resistivity, optical conductivity (real part), and quasiparticle scattering rate.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Frequency ($\Omega$), temperature ($T$), impurity scattering rate ($\gamma_i$), quasiparticle scattering time ($	au_{ee}$), and the Planckian scale ($\Omega \sim T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglect of exchange processes in the Coulomb model, neglect of mass renormalization in the Coulomb model, and the use of the Eliashberg approximation (local self-energy and small vertex corrections).

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the kinetic equation and the physical models (Coulomb and Hertz-Millis), proceeds to the mathematical mapping onto a Legendre-type equation, derives the exact scaling form for conductivity, and concludes with an analysis of specific regimes (collisionless vs. hydrodynamic) and the role of mass renormalization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We obtain an exact expression for the conductivity of a disordered,   non-Galilean-invariant Fermi liquid by solving the kinetic equation with both screened Coulomb and $z=3$ Pomeranchuk critical interactions. While consistent with previous asymptotic results, our solution shows that electron-electron interactions enter the conductivity solely via the quasiparticle scattering time, $τ_\mathrm{ee}$. Accordingly, the crossovers between the collisionless and hydrodynamic regimes occur when $1/τ_\mathrm{ee}$ becomes comparable to the larger of the impurity scattering rate and the probe frequency, $Ω$. In addition, the exact solution yields the optical response in the hydrodynamic regime, $Ω\ll 1/τ_\mathrm{ee}$, which is inaccessible within perturbation theory. Near a $z=3$ Pomeranchuk quantum critical point, consistency between the kinetic-equation and Kubo approaches requires proper inclusion of mass renormalization within the Eliashberg approximation, which also ensures that the crossover between the collisionless and hydrodynamic regimes in the optical conductivity occurs at the Planckian scale $Ω\sim T$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22646"></a>
### [Directed extended-range percolation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22646v1)

**Authors:** Wenbo Liu, Yiwen Zeng, Xueming Liu, Ginestra Bianconi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22646v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22646_figures/2605.22646_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. DERP message passing mechanism and net- work components.(a) Schematic of the DERP connectivity mechanism. Node i is trusted (filled circle), while the unfilled circles are untrusted relay nodes. If both m &lt; R and n &lt; R, node i connects to the SCGC through incoming and outgoing paths within range R and thus belongs to the SCGC together with the relay nodes. If only m &lt; R (only n &lt; R), node i and the corresponding relay nodes belong to the IGC (OGC). Spa- tial distribution of IGC, OGC, and SCGC in a representative network for R = 2 (b) and R = 3 (c), showing that increasing the interaction range expands all three giant components.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22646_figures/2605.22646_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. DERP dynamics in a real-world p2p net- work [26] (N = 10, 876, R = 2). Symbols denote numerical simulations, and solid lines represent theoretical predictions. Despite the inherent network asymmetry causing the sizes of IGC and OGC to be unequal, the theoretical fits remain ex- cellent.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22646_figures/2605.22646_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. DERP dynamics in directed networks with varying degree correlations. Comparison of simulations (symbols) and theoretical predictions (solid lines) for UC (Poisson) (a) and MC (Poisson) (b) networks. The order pa- rameters S and U are shown as functions of p for R = 2. Network parameters are N = 20,000, c = 2.5. Data are aver- aged over 100 realizations.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22646_figures/2605.22646_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Dependence of the critical exponent β on the parameter γ for R = 2 in the DERP model. Critical exponent β as a function of γ for R = 2 in the DERP model. (a) In the UC (PL) model, a single transition occurs at γ = 3 (vertical dashed line). For γ &gt; 3, the system exhibits mean-field behavior. (b) In the MC (PL) model, four exponents (βS, βU, β± S , and β± U ) show boundaries at γ = 3 and γ = 4, becoming non-analytic at γ = 3. In both panels, symbols represent numerical simulations (N = 50, 000), and solid lines denote theoretical curves.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores a new model of directed extended-range percolation inspired by quantum communication. It demonstrates that network directionality can simplify the percolation problem and that degree correlations can lead to a vanishing percolation threshold in scale-free networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how network directionality and degree correlations affect the percolation threshold and critical behavior in extended-range percolation models.

**Main result.** The authors show that directionality reduces combinatorial complexity and that in maximally correlated scale-free networks, the percolation threshold can vanish for certain power-law exponents.

**Method.** The study uses a message-passing approach and linearization of recursive equations to derive analytical solutions, which are then validated using Monte Carlo simulations.

**Model / system.** The model is Directed Extended-Range Percolation (DERP), where connectivity between trusted nodes is enabled by directed paths of maximum length R, potentially using untrusted nodes as relays.

**Key observables.** Percolation threshold (pc), order parameters (S, U, S(+), U(+)), and critical exponents (beta, beta_S, beta_U).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction range (R), power-law exponent (gamma), and degree correlations (Uncorrelated vs. Maximally Correlated).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analytical derivation assumes locally tree-like structures and omits specific discussion of logarithmic corrections at the critical point gamma = 3.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the DERP mechanism; Figure 3 compares simulation results with theoretical predictions for order parameters; Figure 4 shows the dependence of the threshold on parameters; and supplementary figures show effective exponent plateaus and scaling plots.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the DERP model and its motivation, develops a message-passing framework for analytical solutions, analyzes critical behavior across different network topologies (UC and MC), and validates results via Monte Carlo simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

While for standard percolation directionality is known to increase the combinatorial complexity of percolation, here we show that when connectivity is ensured by paths of length $R\geq 2$, network directionality, impeding backtracking, can significantly reduce the complexity of percolation. To illustrate this finding, we introduce Directed Extended-Range Percolation (DERP), defined directed networks with non-reciprocal edges, motivated by applications in quantum communication. In this framework, message transmission is enabled between trusted nodes separated by a directed path of length at most $R$. Using a message-passing approach, we show that directionality enables an exact determination of the percolation threshold and the anomalous critical indices on locally tree-like structures. On random directed networks we find that the critical behavior of DERP depends sensitively on degree correlations. These analytical predictions are corroborated by extensive Monte Carlo simulations, highlighting the profound impact of directionality and correlations on long-range connectivity in complex networks.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22254_figures/2605.22254_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Comparison of heat maps of the final overlap obtained from direct simulation and DMFT for various initial overlaps M 0. In each row, the panels from left to right correspond to kA = 0, kA = 3.0, and their difference.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper studies how synapses that change alongside neurons affect memory in Hopfield networks. It finds that a moderate amount of plasticity helps the network remember patterns more robustly, but too much plasticity causes the network to mistakenly memorize the starting noise instead of the intended memory.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how online, activity-dependent synaptic plasticity affects the memory retrieval dynamics, capacity, and stability of Hopfield-type associative memory networks.

**Main result.** Moderate plasticity enhances retrieval by creating a positive delayed feedback that stabilizes the target memory, but excessive plasticity leads to 'cue-imprinting failure' where the network stabilizes spurious attractors based on the initial cue.

**Method.** The authors use the generating-functional formalism to derive a Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (DMFT) that reduces the many-body dynamics to an effective single-site stochastic process, which is then validated via large-scale numerical simulations.

**Model / system.** A fully connected Hopfield-type associative memory network where neural states and synaptic couplings coevolve. The model includes fixed Hebbian couplings and time-dependent plastic couplings that evolve via an online Hebbian update rule.

**Key observables.** Memory retrieval overlap (M), memory load (alpha), basin of attraction size, and integrated feedback kernels (Onsager and plastic components).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Plasticity strength (beta or kA), memory load (alpha), learning rate (gamma), and initial overlap (M0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the thermodynamic limit (N to infinity), a fully connected network architecture, and an odd activation function (tanh).

**Figures summary.** Figures show the time evolution of overlap, heat maps of the basin of attraction, comparison of DMFT with direct simulations, and the decomposition of feedback kernels into Onsager and plastic components.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of coevolving dynamics, derives the DMFT using generating functionals, presents numerical validation, analyzes the mechanism of feedback-driven stabilization, and discusses the optimization of plasticity strength.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a fully connected Hopfield-type associative memory network with online activity-dependent synaptic plasticity, where neural states and synaptic couplings coevolve during retrieval. Using the generating-functional formalism, we derive a dynamical mean-field theory (DMFT) in the large-system limit with extensively many stored random patterns, and show that the many-body dynamics reduces to an effective single-site stochastic process with colored Gaussian crosstalk noise and delayed feedback terms. Numerical solutions of the DMFT equations agree well with direct simulations. We find that moderate plasticity enlarges the basin of attraction and increases the maximum retrievable memory load by generating a positive delayed feedback that stabilizes retrieval against crosstalk noise. However, excessively strong plasticity causes the network to imprint the imperfect initial cue itself, leading to spurious attractors and degraded retrieval performance. Consequently, an optimal plasticity strength emerges from the trade-off between memory stabilization and premature cue imprinting. These results extend the DMFT description of associative memory to networks with coevolving neural and synaptic dynamics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22628"></a>
### [Ising surface defects can get dirty](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22628v1)

**Authors:** António Antunes, Apratim Kaviraj, Baishali Roy  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22628v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22628_figures/2605.22628_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Defect Feynman diagrams for same replica. The crosses indicate counter terms.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22628_figures/2605.22628_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Defect Feynman diagrams for different replicas.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22628_figures/2605.22628_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Flow plot for the surface defect RG flow. The two ordered fixed points are denoted by a red dot, and the disordered one by a blue dot.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22628_figures/2605.22628_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: The diagrams contributing to ⟨ϕi(τ1)ϕj(τ2)⟩.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how random magnetic fields on a surface can create new types of boundary conditions in critical systems. It specifically shows that these 'dirty' boundaries lead to the emergence of complex logarithmic structures in the theory's spectrum. This provides deeper insight into the interplay between disorder and criticality in the 3D Ising model.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how localized random magnetic fields on a 2D surface defect affect the boundary conditions and stability of the 3D Ising universality class.

**Main result.** The authors identify a new 'dirty' boundary condition fixed point and demonstrate the emergence of logarithmic conformal field theory structures in the replica limit.

**Method.** The researchers use the replica method for disorder averaging combined with Renormalization Group (RG) analysis and one-loop perturbative expansion in 4-epsilon dimensions.

**Model / system.** A massless interacting scalar field theory (Wilson-Fisher fixed point) in 4-epsilon dimensions featuring a 2D surface defect subject to a Gaussian random magnetic field and a local temperature term.

**Key observables.** Anomalous dimensions of bilinear defect operators, scaling dimensions of local operators, and one-point correlation functions of bulk composite operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The expansion parameter epsilon, the number of replicas N (taking the N to 0 limit), and the defect couplings h1 and h2.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the epsilon expansion and focuses on the co-dimension 1 limit for the physical 3D case.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Feynman diagrams for same-replica contributions; Figure 2 shows diagrams for different-replica contributions; Figure 3 displays an RG flow plot with fixed points; Figure 4 illustrates diagrams for defect correlation functions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the model of surface defects, applies the replica method to handle disorder, performs RG calculations to find fixed points and stability, analyzes the resulting operator spectrum, and discusses the emergence of logarithmic structures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Real critical systems, such as uniaxial ferromagnets in the 3d Ising universality class, are constrained by boundaries and subject to random couplings. We consider the Wilson-Fisher fixed point in $4-ε$ dimensions subject to a random magnetic field localized on a two-dimensional surface, which becomes co-dimension 1 in the physical $ε\to1$ limit. Using the replica method for the disordered field, we find that the ordinary boundary condition is stable under disorder but also discover a non-trivial ``dirty" boundary condition which can be reached by tuning the disorder strength or the local temperature. We also investigate the logarithmic structure of the defect spectrum and how it emerges via the replica formalism.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21722"></a>
### [MetaDNS: Enhancing Exploration in Discrete Neural Samplers via Well-Tempered Metadynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21722v1)

**Authors:** Xiaochen Du, Juno Nam, Jaemoo Choi, Wei Guo, Sathya Edamadaka, Junyi Sha, Elton Pan, Yongxin Chen, Molei Tao, Rafael Gómez-Bombarelli  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21722v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21722_figures/2605.21722_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Scheme for MetaDNS. (Left) State of the art (SOTA) discrete neural samplers suffer from mode collapse at low temperatures: samples concentrate in a single deep minimum of the multi-modal energy landscape. (Middle) MetaDNS adds a history-dependent bias potential (purple) that raises the energy in frequently visited regions, flattening the landscape and encouraging exploration across energy barriers to visit multiple minima. (Right) Samples drawn from the biased distribution are reweighted to recover the true multi-modal target distribution on the original energy landscape.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21722_figures/2605.21722_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Ising model results at L = 16: (a-c) Up-spin concen- tration distributions at three temperatures (β = 0.6, 0.44, 0.28). MetaDNS (ours) captures the full bimodal distribution at low tem- perature while MDNS suffers from mode collapse. (d-f) Free energy per site versus inverse temperature for x↑= 0.25, 0.5, and 0.75. Lack of samples at intermediate spin concentrations prevents MDNS from estimating free energies at low temperature.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21722_figures/2605.21722_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Collective variable (CV) distributions for Potts (q = 3, L = 16) models at two temperatures: (a) low and (b) critical. 2D CV space (CV 1 vs. CV 2) comparing SW (ground truth), MDNS, MetaDNS (ours), and MCMC-based WT-MetaD. At low and critical temperatures, MDNS exhibits mode collapse, discovering only one of three modes. MetaDNS successfully covers all modes and interspace regions, matching WT-MetaD coverage.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21722_figures/2605.21722_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Free energy profiles and convergence for Potts (q = 3) at L = 16 comparing MetaDNS (ours) with MCMC-based WT- MetaD. (a-c) Free energy profiles along CV 1 at high (β = 0.5), critical (β = 1.01), and low (β = 1.2) temperatures show- ing agreement with WT-MetaD. (d-f) Convergence speedup of MetaDNS over WT-MetaD at the same temperatures (RMSE vs. steps).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21722_figures/2605.21722_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Cu-Au alloy results at 4 × 4 × 4. (a-b) Crystal struc- tures of Cu3Au and CuAu ordered phases. (c-e) Au concentration distributions at 500K, 680K, and 1200K showing MDNS mode collapse at 500K, capturing only the xAu = 0.5 mode, whereas MetaDNS (ours) matches the ground truth. (f) Free energy profiles at 500K showing MetaDNS (ours) agreement with WT-MetaD. (g) Convergence speedup of MetaDNS over WT-MetaD at 500K (RMSE vs. steps).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces MetaDNS, a method that adds a history-dependent bias to neural samplers to prevent them from getting stuck in single energy minima. This allows the sampler to explore complex landscapes and accurately calculate free energies for systems like alloys and spin models. It provides a significant speedup in both exploration and inference compared to classical Monte Carlo methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Discrete neural samplers like MDNS suffer from mode collapse and fail to explore high-energy barrier regions, preventing accurate free energy estimation and phase transition analysis.

**Main result.** The MetaDNS framework successfully overcomes mode collapse, enabling the reconstruction of free energy landscapes and achieving faster exploration and inference compared to traditional MCMC-based metadynamics.

**Method.** Integration of well-tempered metadynamics into discrete diffusion or autoregressive samplers using an adaptive, history-dependent bias potential along selected collective variables.

**Model / system.** The method is tested on the Ising model, the Potts model (q=3), and a Cu-Au binary alloy system using cluster expansion Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** Magnetization, two-point correlation functions, energy distributions, gold concentration, free energy landscapes (PMF), and Jensen-Shannon divergence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inverse temperature (beta), bias factor (gamma), hill width (sigma), and initial hill height (h).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the neural sampler stays close to the biased target distribution and that the learning timescale is much faster than the bias update timescale.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the MetaDNS scheme; Figure 2 compares MDNS mode collapse vs MetaDNS in the Ising model; Figure 3 shows Potts model CV distributions; Figure 4 shows free energy convergence; Figure 5 illustrates Cu-Au alloy structures and concentration distributions; Figure 16 compares learning dynamics between MetaDNS and WT-MetaD.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the mode collapse problem in neural samplers, proposes the MetaDNS framework, details the mathematical integration of metadynamics with diffusion/autoregressive models, validates the method using Ising and Potts models, demonstrates application to the Cu-Au alloy, and performs sensitivity analyses on hyperparameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Sampling from discrete distributions with multiple modes and energy barriers is fundamental to machine learning and computational physics. Recent discrete neural samplers like MDNS suffer from mode collapse and fail to sample high-energy barrier regions between modes, which is critical for free energy estimation and understanding phase transitions. We propose Metadynamics Discrete Neural Sampler (MetaDNS), a general framework integrating well-tempered metadynamics into discrete diffusion or autoregressive samplers. By maintaining an adaptive, history-dependent bias potential along selected low-dimensional coordinates, MetaDNS forces exploration of previously inaccessible regions, enabling free energy reconstruction infeasible with standard neural samplers due to a lack of high-energy samples. On challenging low-temperature benchmarks including Ising, Potts, and the copper-gold binary alloy, MetaDNS reproduces the thermodynamic distribution. Compared to MCMC-based metadynamics, MetaDNS also achieves comparable exploration requiring fewer bias deposition steps.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22691"></a>
### [Posterior Collapse as Automatic Spectral Pruning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22691v1)

**Authors:** Johannes Hirn  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22691v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22691_figures/2605.22691_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Order-parameter collapse scan.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22691_figures/2605.22691_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Truncated distortion and rank pruning.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22691_figures/2605.22691_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Reconstruction utility vs collapse threshold.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22691_figures/2605.22691_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: WorldClim posterior-scale and Jensen-gap diagnostic for ranked latent modes.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reframes the 'failure' of posterior collapse in VAEs as a useful feature that automatically prunes unimportant latent dimensions. By treating the regularization parameter as an effective temperature, the author shows that latent modes disappear in a predictable sequence based on their contribution to data reconstruction.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Reinterpreting posterior collapse in beta-VAEs from a training failure to a functional mechanism for automatic spectral pruning and identifying the relationship between latent mode collapse and reconstruction utility.

**Main result.** In linear Gaussian VAEs, the collapse threshold, reconstruction utility, and PCA explained variance coincide, demonstrating that increasing regularization triggers a predictable cascade of mode collapses.

**Method.** The author employs a Landau-style stability analysis to treat latent mode collapse as a phase transition and uses a beta-scan protocol to rank latent modes by their utility.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on beta-Variational Autoencoders (beta-VAEs), specifically analyzing the linear Gaussian case and testing the framework on the WorldClim global climatology dataset.

**Key observables.** Posterior signal fraction (M^2), collapse threshold (T_k), reconstruction utility (delta D_k), signal-to-noise ratio (SNR), and the Jensen gap.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Regularization strength (beta), effective temperature (T), and decoder variance (sigma^2_dec).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary theoretical derivation assumes a linear Gaussian VAE with a fixed isotropic decoder variance; the thermodynamic analogy is structural rather than a literal marginalization over microstates.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the collapse scan via posterior mean-square and signal fraction; Figure 2 compares truncated distortion to PCA; Figure 3 demonstrates the utility-threshold duality; Figure 4 shows posterior-scale and Jensen-gap diagnostics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of posterior collapse, develops a Landau-style stability theory for mode inactivation, establishes the duality between collapse and utility in Gaussian models, and validates these findings via experiments on the WorldClim dataset.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that posterior collapse in $β$-VAEs implements automatic spectral pruning. A latent mode collapses if its contribution to reconstruction is below the cutoff set by $β$. Equilibrium solutions with different $β$ thus reveal a cascade of collapses as latent modes decouple from least to most useful.   We derive this as a consequence of the loss via a Landau stability analysis. We define a latent-rescaling-invariant order parameter that ranks active latent modes and whose collapse thresholds identify which effective variables to inspect first.   In the linear Gaussian case, the collapse spectrum, utility spectrum, and normalized PCA spectrum coincide, and each collapse follows a mean-field law. We test these predictions on the WorldClim dataset.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22744"></a>
### [Quantum circuit design via dynamic Pauli constraints](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22744v1)

**Authors:** James R. Wootton, Merlin Incerti-Medici, Daniel Bultrini, Pierre Fromholz  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22744v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22744_figures/2605.22744_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Generic structure of the Motte model. The user works in the Pauli-expectation vocabulary; the classical in- terface deduces the gate; the QPU executes it. Repeated for D circuit-depth layers before a final Z-basis measurement.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new way to program quantum computers by specifying desired physical observables instead of complex gate sequences. It proves that this 'Motte model' is as powerful as the standard circuit model and provides a natural interface for NISQ-era algorithms like quantum imaginary time evolution.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for a software-oriented quantum computing interface that uses physically observable quantities (Pauli expectation values) rather than complex unitary matrices, specifically addressing the constraints of the NISQ era.

**Main result.** The authors introduce and prove the universality of the 'Motte model,' a framework where gates are specified by Pauli constraints, showing it is equivalent to the standard circuit model with polynomial overhead.

**Method.** The model uses k-body quantum state tomography to reconstruct reduced density matrices, which are then used to analytically deduce unitaries via eigenstate or density matrix transformations.

**Model / system.** A software-oriented model of quantum computation (the Motte model) acting as an interface for a standard gate-model QPU with N qubits and a connected coupling graph G.

**Key observables.** Pauli expectation values (X, Y, Z) and k-local Pauli correlators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** N (number of qubits), D (circuit depth), k (tomographic locality), and delta (statistical sampling error).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The underlying QPU is assumed to be noiseless for the theoretical definition, and statistical noise is assumed to be sufficient to lift eigenvalue degeneracies.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the Motte model workflow: user-provided Pauli targets lead to classical gate deduction, execution on a QPU, and iterative feedback via shot statistics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Motte model, establishes its computational universality (BQP equivalence), addresses the technical challenge of phase ambiguity in gate reconstruction, demonstrates practical applications like QITE and GHZ state preparation, and provides a complexity analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a novel software-oriented model of quantum computation motivated by the practical constraints of near-term quantum hardware. In this model, gates are specified by constraints expressed in terms of Pauli observables, with each disjoint layer of gates accompanied by a pairwise or $k$-local quantum state tomography of the device. We prove that the model is equivalent to the coupling-graph-restricted circuit model and hence universal for BQP, with a polynomial overhead: simulating a depth-$D$ circuit on $N$ qubits requires at most $O(D^2 N \log N)$ complexity. The model formalizes an idiom shared by existing work that ranges from quantum imaginary time evolution for the study of quantum systems to the use of quantum computers for procedural generation in games. It therefore provides a natural interface for designing quantum software entirely in terms of physically observable quantities, relevant for the NISQ era and into fault-tolerance.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Statistical Interpretation of the Procedures Measurement of Physical Quantities](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22264v1)

**Authors:** Pandiscia Carlo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22264v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<sub>Figure 1.2: N displaced copies of the ensemble</sub>

<sub>Figure 1.5: Laboratory-type region</sub>

**Summary.** This paper provides a mathematical and conceptual synthesis of algebraic quantum theory and quantum probability. It argues for an operationalist approach where physical quantities are defined by their measurement procedures rather than purely abstract mathematical axioms. The result is a framework that maintains a connection between formal algebraic structures and empirical laboratory practice.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to bridge the gap between abstract algebraic quantum theory (such as C*-algebras) and the practical, operational reality of laboratory measurement procedures.

**Main result.** The author develops a conceptual framework that links algebraic formulations with quantum probability, demonstrating how a statistical model of measurement can lead to the reconstruction of algebraic structures like von Neumann algebras.

**Method.** The work uses a synthesis of existing models (von Neumann, Mackey, Segal) employing mathematical tools from measure theory, functional analysis, and operator algebra theory.

**Model / system.** The framework is based on a hypothetical 'Laboratory System' involving experimental procedures, measurement instruments, and a localized space-time region equipped with a clock.

**Key observables.** Physical quantities defined through measurement, including compatible/incompatible observables, joint spectra, and temporal evolution of observables.

**Important parameters / regimes.** L-uniform continuity of state transformations, spectral properties, and the distinction between axioms, postulates, and technical assumptions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The laboratory is spatially limited; time is measured by an internal clock; and physical quantities are only considered to exist if an effective experimental measurement method is available.

**Paper structure.** The paper is structured into two main parts: Part I focuses on laboratories and experimental procedures (statistics, reproducibility, and entropy), while Part II establishes the mathematical framework for these procedures (Mackey's model, spectral properties, and algebraic interpretation).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work develops a conceptual framework for the foundations of quantum physics, linking two main approaches: the algebraic formulation and quantum probability. Rather than proposing new axioms or theories, the text reorganizes and synthesizes existing models, highlighting their assumptions, conceptual structures, and operational significance. The analysis begins with von Neumann's measurement theory and its subsequent developments by Mackey, emphasizing the role of experimentally feasible procedures and the need for a statistical model grounded in laboratory practice. The work adopts an operational perspective, according to which physical quantities are defined solely through experimental measurement methods, and the corresponding probabilistic measures are derived from measurement outcomes. The introduction critically examines the limitations of purely mathematical formulations - such as the algebraic method - when separated from experimental interpretation. The text argues for a clear distinction between axioms, postulates, and presuppositions, and for a reconstruction of quantum theory that respects both empirical constraints and conceptual clarity.   Overall, the goal is to provide a coherent path from operational principles to algebraic structures, offering a basis for an axiomatic reformulation of quantum mechanics that remains faithful to physical practice.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22128"></a>
### [Superconductivity in doped spin multimer systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22128v1)

**Authors:** Ritsuki Hirabayashi, Masataka Kakoi, Ryota Ueda, Kazuhiko Kuroki, Tatsuya Kaneko  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22128v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22128_figures/2605.22128_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Two layers coupled via mediators. (b) Second- order process that yields the effective hopping teff of a hole- hole (h-h) pair. (c) Effective hardcore boson model.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22128_figures/2605.22128_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. (a) Two-leg Kondo-Hubbard ladder and (b) binding energy EB when t∥= 0. (c) Pair correlation function P(r) for JK/J⊥= 2 at nh = 0.125, where eC+−(r) = α2C+−(r) is given by the transverse spin correlation function C+−(r) of the effective XXZ chain with the renormalization factor α = cos θ</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22128_figures/2605.22128_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Comparison of the ground-state properties for JK &gt; 0 (upper panel) and JK &lt; 0 (lower panel), where t∥/J⊥= 1 is used in (a)-(d) while t∥/J⊥= 1/3 is used in (e)-(h) to equalize EB/t∥at |JK|/J⊥≫1. (a), (e) Spin correlations within a unit cell ¯F cc ⊥, ¯F ff ⊥, and ¯F cf ⊥, where the site-averaged values are plotted. (b), (f) Pair correlation function P(r). (c), (g) Decay exponent KSC of P(r). (d), (h) Fraction of paired holes among all holes ppair, where the insets present the binding energies in units of t∥.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22128_figures/2605.22128_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. (a) Difference of the binding energies with and with- out the interchain hopping t⊥, where ∆EB for t⊥/J⊥= 0.5 and t∥= 0 is plotted. (b) Pair correlation function P(r) and (c) decay exponent KSC for t∥/J⊥= 1.0 and t⊥/J⊥= 0.5.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that superconductivity can emerge in complex spin multimer systems through a robust pairing mechanism. By mapping the double Kondo lattice model to a hardcore boson model, the authors demonstrate that pair correlations persist even when moving away from the strong-coupling limit. This provides a general guideline for finding superconductivity in multi-orbital or multi-layer materials like nickelates.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the mechanism of pair formation and the robustness of superconductivity in doped spin multimer systems, specifically determining if pairing signatures persist away from the strong-binding-energy limit.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that hole-doped spin multimer systems exhibit a crossover from a BEC-like state to a more extended state while maintaining power-law pair correlations, and that these correlations are robust against variations in local spin structure provided the binding energy is sufficient.

**Method.** The study uses second-order perturbation theory to derive an effective hardcore boson model and employs the Density-Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) method for numerical simulations.

**Model / system.** A 1D double Kondo lattice model (a ladder structure) consisting of itinerant electrons interacting with localized spins via Kondo coupling ($J_K$) and interchain spin exchange ($J_{\perp}$).

**Key observables.** Binding energy ($E_B$), pair correlation function $P(r)$, spin gap ($\Delta_s$), and the decay exponent $K_{SC}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kondo coupling ($J_K$), interchain spin exchange ($J_{\perp}$), intrachain hopping ($t_{\parallel}$), interchain hopping ($t_{\perp}$), and on-site Coulomb repulsion ($U$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is analyzed in the limit of large $U$ and assumes the low-energy physics can be mapped onto a universal hardcore boson/XXZ model in the strong-binding-energy limit.

**Figures summary.** The figures likely illustrate the binding energy as a function of coupling, the power-law decay of pair correlations, and the mapping between the Kondo lattice and the XXZ model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of pairing in multi-spin systems, derives an effective Hamiltonian via perturbation theory, presents DMRG numerical results for pair correlations and binding energy, and discusses the universality and robustness of the findings.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Binding energy, which quantifies pair formation, is a key factor in the emergence of superconductivity. Here, we show that even when multiple spins are complexly coupled, hole-doped systems, which can be mapped onto the universal hardcore boson model in the strong-binding-energy limit, exhibit promising signatures of superconductivity. We analytically and numerically demonstrate this concept in the double Kondo lattice model. Using the density-matrix renormalization group method, we show that a pairing state is maintained via a crossover even for parameters away from the strong-coupling regime. Additionally, we find that once binding energies are sufficiently generated, pair correlations develop similarly regardless of the details of local spin correlations. Our findings suggest useful guidelines for research on superconductivity.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22341"></a>
### [A Boundary-Layer Mechanism for One-Third Scaling in Online Softmax Classification](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22341v1)

**Authors:** Marcel Kühn, Yoon Thelge, Bernd Rosenow  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22341v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22341_figures/2605.22341_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Left: The 1/3 law appears not only in the test loss but also in the generalization error ϵg, i.e., one minus test accuracy. Middle: The model captures both the growth of the centered student-teacher alignment D and the rotational alignment to the teacher. Right: Near a teacher decision boundary, the late-time loss is controlled by the student boundary layer of width O(D−1). The generalization error is controlled by residual boundary fluctuations only of order √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22341_figures/2605.22341_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Finite-N validation for fixed learning rates in the K = 3 online teacher–student model. The panels show the generalization error, centered overlap D, and residual variance ∆as functions of macroscopic time α = µ/N. The curves show representative seed trajectories, with envelopes indicating fluctuations across six simulation seeds. Within these fluctuations, the trajectories agree with the predicted power-law prefactors and exponents: D ∼α1/3, ∆approaches a learning-rate- dependent floor, and ϵg ∝ √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22341_figures/2605.22341_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Schedule dependence in the K = 3 online teacher–student model. For η(α) ∝α−γ, the theory predicts ϵg(α) ∼α−(2+γ)/6 for 0 ≤γ &lt; 1. Increasing γ slows the growth of the centered overlap, D ∝α(1−γ)/3 for γ &lt; 1, but decreases the residual variance, ∆∝η(α); the latter effect improves the classification-error exponent. The γ = 1 curve is a borderline case, where the adiabatic approximation for ∆breaks down. For reference, the adiabatic predictions of Eqs. (19) and (20) are also shown.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22341_figures/2605.22341_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Controlled departure from isotropic inputs. Inputs are Gaussian with diagonal covariance spectrum ⟨ξ2 i ⟩∼i−β, normalized so that the largest variance is one. Left: η = 0.5 is fixed and β is varied. Right: β = 1.0 is fixed and the learning rate is varied. Increasing β changes and lengthens the transient regime. The late-time decay remains consistent with the same boundary-layer asymptote, while smaller learning rates reduce asymptotic prefactors but can delay entry into the asymptotic regime.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22341_figures/2605.22341_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Non-Gaussian robustness test using whitened pretrained features. A linear soft- max readout is trained online on whitened ViT features with teacher-generated labels. Whitening controls the covariance, while the feature distribution remains non-Gaussian. The teacher-generated labels avoid an early real-label performance floor and allow the late-time regime to be observed.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper provides a theoretical derivation of the 1/3 power-law scaling observed in online softmax classification. It demonstrates that this scaling arises from the dynamics of a boundary layer near decision boundaries. The authors also show that decaying the learning rate can improve the convergence exponent.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the origin of the 1/3 power-law scaling in the generalization error and test loss of online softmax classification.

**Main result.** The study identifies a boundary-layer mechanism where the mismatch between smooth softmax loss and hard labels drives a 1/3 scaling law, which can be improved toward 1/2 via learning rate annealing.

**Method.** Statistical physics of learning using order-parameter methodology in the thermodynamic limit, combined with boundary-layer asymptotic analysis and numerical simulations.

**Model / system.** A one-layer K-class teacher-student model using online stochastic gradient descent with Gaussian, correlated Gaussian, and whitened ViT features.

**Key observables.** Generalization error (epsilon_g), test loss (L_g), centered student-teacher alignment (D), and residual student variance (Delta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Learning rate (eta) and its decay exponent (gamma), number of classes (K), and input covariance/anisotropy (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Thermodynamic limit (N to infinity), permutation symmetry, fixed features, and the 'almost-perfect-learning' regime where the margin is large.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the 1/3 power law for loss and error; Figure 2 validates dynamics for K=3; Figure 3 shows the effect of learning rate schedules; Figure 5 shows robustness to input correlation; Figure 6 shows scaling with the number of classes; Figure 7 shows performance on whitened ViT features.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scaling problem, derives the exact macroscopic dynamics for centered variables, provides an asymptotic boundary-layer evaluation to explain the 1/3 exponent, analyzes the effect of learning rate annealing, and concludes with robustness tests against non-Gaussian and correlated input distributions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hard-label classification is usually trained with smooth surrogate losses, most prominently softmax cross-entropy. We isolate an asymptotic mechanism by which this mismatch between smooth surrogate and discrete labels produces power-law learning curves in an online teacher-student model. After subtracting the mean logit, the thermodynamic-limit dynamics close in centered variables: a growing centered student-teacher alignment $D$ and the residual student variance $Δ$. At late times, examples away from teacher decision boundaries are already classified confidently and contribute exponentially little. Only boundary layers of width $O(D^{-1})$ remain active, while the noise of fixed-learning-rate online gradient descent maintains a nonzero $Δ$. As a function of the training time $α$ the late-time solution yields a $α^{-1/3}$ power law not only for the test loss but also for the generalization error $ε_g$, i.e., one minus test accuracy. This is much slower than the $α^{-1}$ Bayes-optimal reference for the same model. We further show that learning-rate schedules can improve the generalization error towards a $ε_g \sim α^{-1/2}$ power law. Simulations support the predicted order parameter dynamics and learning curves. Controlled experiments with correlated Gaussian inputs and whitened pretrained features show that data structure can dominate transients. Therefore, our result is an asymptotic, complementary mechanism rather than an alternative to spectral explanations of neural scaling laws.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22569"></a>
### [A Formal Basis for Quantum Cryptographic Exposure Measurement under HNDL Threat](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22569v1)

**Authors:** Matheus Rufino, Rafael Duarte Marcelino, Julio Smanioto Garcia  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22569v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22569_figures/2605.22569_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Structural organisation of the HNDL exposure assessment problem. Observable and declarative signals are mapped to the structural variables 𝑉(vulnerability fraction) and 𝐸(operational exposure); the proportional-hazards contest (Hypotheses 1–3) and temporal hazard 𝐻(Definition 1) produce the exposure score of Eq. (8). The contribution of this paper is the structural form of the lower two stages; the signal-to-(𝑉, 𝐸) mapping is implementation-specific.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22569_figures/2605.22569_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Structural properties of the IEQ under Hypotheses 1–3 with 𝑎= 1.0, 𝑏= 0.5, 𝜃= 1.0, 𝐻= 0.6, 𝑀= 1.15. (a) IEQ surface over the (𝑉, 𝐸) unit square (Eq. (8)). Contour density reflects score sensitivity: high in the defense-dominant regime (𝑉𝑎𝐸𝑏≪𝜃, lower left) and low near saturation (𝑉𝑎𝐸𝑏≫𝜃, upper right). The observed range reflects the representative parameter 𝜃= 1.0; the full [0, 100] scale is realized at lower 𝜃values. (b) Endogenous elasticities 𝛽(𝑉, 𝐸) and 𝛾(𝑉, 𝐸) (Eq. (7)) as functions of the regime ratio 𝑟= 𝑉𝑎𝐸𝑏∕𝜃. The dashed horizontal line shows the fixed elasticity assumed by CES and log-additive specifications; the multiplicative family predicts elasticities that decrease...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a mathematically grounded way to quantify the risk of data being stolen today to be decrypted by future quantum computers. It demonstrates that risk assessment must account for the interaction between how vulnerable a cipher is and how accessible the data is, rather than just adding these risks together.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a formal, structurally justified framework to measure quantum cryptographic exposure under the 'Harvest Now, Decrypt Later' (HNDL) threat.

**Main result.** The paper proves that an effective exposure score must be multiplicative rather than additive to capture the necessary interaction between cryptographic vulnerability and operational exposure, and that migration efficiency is highest in the low-exposure regime.

**Method.** The authors use a probabilistic contest model based on the Tullock Contest Success Function and proportional hazards, validated via structural specification diagnostics like the Vuong statistic and penalized splines.

**Model / system.** A probabilistic model of an adversarial contest where the probability of compromise depends on the relative rates of an attacker's effort (driven by quantum vulnerability and reachability) versus a defender's remediation rate.

**Key observables.** Quantum vulnerability fraction (V), operational exposure (E), and the Information Exposure Quotient (IEQ).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Adversarial attack rate (lambda_A), defender's rate of data-value decay (mu), temporal hazard (H), and the defense-attack intensity ratio (theta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes attack and defense are constant-rate exponential processes, and that cryptographic vulnerability and operational reachability are asymptotically independent.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the structural organization of the exposure assessment; Figure 2(a) displays the IEQ surface over the vulnerability-exposure plane; Figure 2(b) illustrates how elasticities decrease toward zero in the saturation regime.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the HNDL problem, defines a mathematical framework using a contest model, provides structural proofs for the functional form of exposure, performs statistical validation of the model, and concludes with migration strategy implications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

An adversary copies your encrypted traffic today and waits for a quantum computer to decrypt it later. How exposed are you?   We show that the functional form of the answer is not merely a calibration choice -- it is structurally justified by three assumptions about adversarial production and value-decay dynamics. Under those assumptions, the HNDL compromise probability factorises into a temporal hazard, a multiplicative cryptographic-vulnerability and operational-exposure term, and a saturation denominator governed by the defense-attack intensity ratio; the marginal sensitivity to each dimension is endogenous to the organisation's position in the vulnerability-exposure plane, not a fixed global constant. Additive scoring frameworks cannot reproduce this structure because the interaction between cryptographic vulnerability and operational exposure is absent by construction, regardless of calibration. The resulting framework provides a structurally grounded basis for operational HNDL exposure prioritisation under partial observability.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that the quantized motion of ultracold polar molecules in optical traps can be used to simulate the Asymmetric Quantum Rabi Model. It identifies a specific resonance that can degrade quantum gates and proposes optimized microwave pulse sequences to achieve high-fidelity entanglement despite motional effects.

**Why it may be interesting.** It connects fundamental light-matter interaction models (AQRM) to practical challenges in molecular quantum computing, specifically how motional degrees of freedom can be leveraged for new physics or must be mitigated for high-fidelity control.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the quantized motion of ultracold polar molecules in optical traps affects dipole-dipole interactions, specifically regarding the emergence of new physical models and the fidelity of quantum gates.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that molecular motion realizes an Asymmetric Quantum Rabi Model (AQRM) and identify a detrimental 'trap-dipole resonance' that must be avoided, while also proposing high-fidelity iSWAP and controlled-phase gates.

**Method.** The study uses analytical techniques like Bogoliubov transforms and displacement operators alongside numerical simulations using QuTiP to evaluate gate fidelities and energy spectra.

**Model / system.** The system consists of two ultracold polar molecules (e.g., NaCs) trapped in optical tweezers, where the coupling between their internal two-level states and their quantized motional modes realizes the Asymmetric Quantum Rabi Model.

**Key observables.** Quantum gate fidelity, energy spectra (eigenvalues/eigenstates), and population transfer to motional states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dipole-dipole interaction strength (J0), trap angular frequency (omega_z), microwave Rabi frequency (Omega_mu), and the coupling parameter (g).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Molecules are near the rovibrational ground manifold, radial motion is neglected, and the study focuses on the axial component of motion.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the trapped molecules and compares numerical vs. analytical energy spectra; Figure 2 shows the feasibility of tuning energy separations; Figures 5 and 7 demonstrate high gate fidelities for iSWAP and controlled-phase gates under thermal motional states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QM-DDI coupling, derives the AQRM Hamiltonian, identifies the trap-dipole resonance, and then evaluates the performance of specific quantum gate protocols (iSWAP and CZ) under motional effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optically trapped ultracold polar molecules can have multiple long-lived states for coding quantum information, and can exhibit electric dipole-dipole interactions~(DDI) which enables entanglement generation. The general understanding on the quantized motion~(QM) of molecules in the traps is that it causes fluctuation of DDI. Here, we find that the molecular QM can realize an asymmetric quantum Rabi model, which is of specific importance in the study of fundamental physics. The molecular QM can also lead to an exotic trap-dipole resonance, resulting in excess population loss to uncoupled motional states, and, hence, should be avoided in a general quantum control over polar molecules. To examine the impact of QM on quantum computing based on polar molecules, we introduce two gate protocols, a fast iSWAP gate which can be realized by a global microwave pulse of pulse area smaller than $2π$, and a controlled-phase gate with an arbitrary controlled phase, and find that both gates can attain a high fidelity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Berezinskii-Kosterlitz-Thouless-type Transition in Site Percolation on the Diamond Hierarchical Lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22103v1)

**Authors:** Takehisa Hasegawa, Kazuki Wataya, Tomoaki Nogawa

As the generation n increases, Pn(p) becomes progressively sharper and, in the limit n →∞, exhibits a finite jump at the critical point pc (vertical dotted line), followed by a continuous increase for p &gt; pc. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates that site percolation on a diamond hierarchical lattice undergoes a BKT-type transition into a critical phase that lacks a giant cluster. It proves that exponential volume growth is not a prerequisite for critical phases, as the hierarchical structure and site dilution create persistent connectivity bottlenecks.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether exponential volume growth is necessary for the emergence of critical phases in percolation and explores the nature of the phase transition in site percolation on a finite-dimensional fractal network.

**Main result.** The authors discover a Berezinskii-Kosterlitz-Thouless (BKT)-type transition characterized by an essential singularity in the correlation length, where a critical phase exists without a giant percolating cluster.

**Method.** The research employs exact generating-function analysis, renormalization-group (RG) recursion relations, and finite-size scaling (FSS) analysis, supplemented by Monte Carlo simulations.

**Model / system.** The system is site percolation on a Diamond Hierarchical Lattice (DHL), a finite-dimensional fractal network with fractal dimension d_f = 2 constructed via a recursive replacement rule.

**Key observables.** Conditional contact probability (P_n), correlation length (xi), fractal exponent (psi), susceptibility (chi), and cluster-size distribution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Occupation probability (p), critical threshold (p_c = 27/32), and the generation number (n) of the lattice.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation of scaling relations assumes that degree-2 nodes provide the dominant contribution to the root-cluster mass and that the correlation function follows a power-law decay.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the recursive construction of the DHL; Figure 2 demonstrates the sharpening of contact probability at p_c and the square-root singularity; Figure 4-6 show scaling of correlation functions, susceptibility, and data collapse via FSS; Figure 7 shows cumulative cluster-size distribution scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the lattice construction and model definition, followed by the derivation of exact generating functions and RG recursion relations, an analysis of the phase transition and critical exponents, and concludes with finite-size scaling validation and discussion of the physical mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study site percolation on the diamond hierarchical lattice, a finite-dimensional fractal network, using an exact generating-function analysis. In contrast to bond percolation, site percolation on this lattice does not undergo a transition from a nonpercolating phase to a percolating phase. Instead, the system exhibits a nonpercolating phase for $p<p_{\rm c}$ and a critical phase for $p>p_{\rm c}$. In the critical phase, the size of the largest cluster remains subextensive, scaling as $N^{ψ(p)}$, where the fractal exponent $ψ(p)$ varies continuously with $p$. By analyzing the renormalization-group recursion relation in the vicinity of $p_{\rm c}$, we show that the correlation length exhibits a Berezinskii-Kosterlitz-Thouless-type essential singularity, $ξ(p)\sim \exp \left({\rm const}/\sqrt{p_{\rm c}-p}\right)$ for $p \to p_{\rm c}^-$, which is further confirmed by finite-size scaling analyses showing excellent data collapse. These results demonstrate that critical phases in percolation can emerge even on finite-dimensional networks and that exponential volume growth is not necessary for such phases to appear. We argue that the critical phase on the diamond hierarchical lattice stems from site dilution remaining relevant under renormalization.

</details>

**Authors:** L. David Le Nir, Asimpunya Mitra, Yong Baek Kim

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21618_figures/2605.21618_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. The variation of the magnitude |∆k| and phase arg[∆k] of the gap function, local occupation nk, and the Berry curvature distribution Ωk, as the width of the Berry curvature distribution is varied by changing B. These gap functions are calculated for parameters, N = 8 (for the λN model), U = 5, σ2 = 1. The circular Fermi surface is marked by the dashed lines. As B increases, more vortices are nucleated until a critical value is reached beyond which the number of vortices reach its upper bound. More details shown in Figs. 3, 6.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21618_figures/2605.21618_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) The vortex number Q with increasing Bk2 F . For finite N, more vortices are nucleated with increasing Bk2 F , until vortex number saturation is reached when Q = 2N −3. The vertical dashed lines demarcate these two regions of vor- tex nucleation and vortex saturation. (b) The condensation energy δF = FSC −FN, shows shows jumps associated with vortex formation in (a), where the formation of vortices leads to a decrease in the condensation energy. Panel (c) shows the maximum value of the gap as these parameters are varied.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21618_figures/2605.21618_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. The real part of the Cooper vertex W(k∗, k) (Eq. 23) is shown in (a1)–(a3) for N = 4, B = 5.5, where k∗is fixed at the momentum marked by the green star and the dashed line denotes the Fermi surface. The corresponding gap magnitude |∆k|, and argument arg[∆k] are shown in (a4), and (a5). Row (b) shows the same for N = 6, B = 10. The vortices in abs[∆k] are represented by the white dots for clarity. For small values of k∗(column 1), one nodal plane is present in Re[W(k∗, k)] implying the formation of a winding +1 central vortex. For larger values of k∗(columns 2, 3), 2N −3 nodal planes appear implying the formation of additional momentum space vortices on a ring at larger values of k.</sub>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that non-uniform Berry curvature in a parent band can drive the formation of vortices within the momentum-space superconducting gap. It establishes a topological link between the parent band's Chern number and the resulting BdG topology, suggesting thermal Hall measurements as a way to detect these momentum-space vortices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how non-uniform Berry curvature in parent electronic states influences the emergence, topology, and experimental signatures of chiral superconductivity, specifically the formation of momentum-space vortices.

**Main result.** Non-uniform Berry curvature induces the nucleation of momentum-space vortices in the superconducting gap function, which lowers condensation energy and determines the BdG Chern number via the winding of a momentum-space phase current.

**Method.** Solving the full, non-linear BCS gap equation on a continuum Chern band using the $\lambda_N$-model and developing a gauge-invariant formulation for BdG Berry curvature.

**Model / system.** The $\lambda_N$-model, a tunable continuum platform for studying electronic states with non-uniform Berry curvature, motivated by rhombohedral graphene multilayers.

**Key observables.** Momentum-space vortices in the gap function, BdG Chern number, thermal Hall conductivity, and condensation energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Berry curvature strength (B), number of orbitals (N), interaction strength (U), interaction range ($\sigma$), and Fermi surface topology (circular vs. annular).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes a single-band projected superconductor (class-D), neglects spin degrees of freedom, and assumes a large band separation to neglect inter-band processes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Berry curvature distribution; Figure 2 displays the gap magnitude and phase; Figure 3 illustrates vortex number vs. Berry curvature strength; Figure 12/13 shows vortex number and condensation energy transitions; Figure 16 shows evolution in annular Fermi surfaces.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Berry curvature-induced superconductivity, defines the $\lambda_N$-model, presents the numerical solution of the gap equation, analyzes vortex nucleation and saturation regimes, derives the topological relation for the BdG Chern number, and discusses experimental signatures via thermal Hall effect.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate chiral superconductivity emerging from parent electronic states with non-uniform Berry curvature, motivated by recent experiments in rhombohedral graphene multilayers. Using the continuum $λ_N$-model-a tunable platform with independently controllable Berry curvature profiles-we solve the full BCS gap equation on a continuum Chern band beyond the weak-coupling limit. We find that a non-uniform Berry curvature of the parent band enriches the superconducting order parameter, leading to the formation of momentum-space vortices in the gap function away from high-symmetry points. By tuning the Berry curvature profile, we identify distinct regimes associated with vortex nucleation and vortex number saturation, and show that the nucleation of momentum-space vortices tends to lower the condensation energy. We then show analytically that the parent band Chern number constrains the number of momentum-space vortices that can nucleate in the gap-independent of details of the $λ_N$-model. We also provide a gauge-invariant formulation for computing the Bogoliubov-de Gennes (BdG) Berry curvature for continuum models, and find that it is determined by a momentum-space phase current. The winding of this current around vortices in the occupied region in turn determines the BdG Chern number. Finally, we discuss how thermal Hall measurements can be used to probe the formation of momentum-space vortices. Our results highlight the crucial role of Berry curvature in shaping chiral superconductivity, and offer guiding principles for its identification in systems such as rhombohedral graphene.

</details>

### [Completeness of the Klein-Gordon oscillator eigenfunctions via Hermite and Laguerre polynomials](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21791v1)

**Authors:** Kevin Hernández  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21791v1>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>
