<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides the rigorous mathematical proof that the eigenfunctions of the Klein-Gordon oscillator are complete in one and three dimensions. By linking the KGO to the properties of Hermite and Laguerre polynomials, it justifies the use of spectral representations for Green's functions and partition functions in relativistic scalar models.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to establish the formal completeness (closure relations) of the Klein-Gordon oscillator (KGO) eigenfunctions in one and three spatial dimensions, filling a gap in the literature where such proofs existed for the Dirac oscillator but not the KGO.

**Main result.** The author proves that the KGO eigenfunctions form a complete orthonormal set in L2(R) and L2(R3) by reducing the problem to the known closure relations of Hermite and generalized Laguerre polynomials.

**Method.** A constructive proof approach is used, employing separation of variables, reduction to the confluent hypergeometric equation, and the application of properties of special functions and spherical harmonics.

**Model / system.** The system is the Klein-Gordon oscillator, a relativistic generalization of the harmonic oscillator for spin-0 particles, where the potential is introduced via minimal substitution p -> p - imw r.

**Key observables.** Energy eigenvalues (E_n^2 and E_N^2) and the energy spectrum degeneracy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass (m), oscillator frequency (w), and the non-relativistic (m -> infinity) and massless (m -> 0) limits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The particle is treated as a scalar field; the proof is limited to 1D and 3D spatial dimensions and does not cover 2D or curved spacetimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a mathematical proof for 1D completeness using Hermite polynomials, followed by a 3D proof using radial Laguerre polynomials and angular spherical harmonics, concluding with a comparison to the Dirac oscillator and discussion of future extensions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Completeness of the Klein--Gordon oscillator eigenfunctions is proved in one and three spatial dimensions. The proofs establish the closure relations satisfied by the eigenfunctions and are based on standard properties of the Hermite and the generalized Laguerre polynomials, supplemented in three dimensions by the completeness of the spherical harmonics. The scalar nature of the Klein--Gordon field renders the argument strictly simpler than the analogous proof for the Dirac oscillator: no off-diagonal cancellation is required.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21898"></a>
### [Concatenating Algebraic Codes over High-Rate Quantum LDPC Codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21898v1)

**Authors:** Adam Wills, Michael E. Beverland, Lev S. Bishop, Jay M. Gambetta, Patrick Rall, Vikesh Siddhu, Andrew W. Cross  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21898v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21898_figures/2605.21898_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: The space overheads of various systems, allowing a maximum size of half a million physical qubits. Concatenation allows the gross code to work in the teraquop regime relevant for the execution of many large-scale quantum algorithms. There, it offers an improved space overhead versus the two-gross code, with several engineering benefits. We show the surface code, as well as the “yoked” (concatenated) surface codes from [Gid+25]. 1D and 2D concatenated surface codes have different layouts. The denser encoding of the latter makes the information harder to access. The concatenated gross codes have the same layout as the 1D concatenated surface codes.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 3: This figure illustrates the key technical idea of packaging the logical information in one gross code into a higher-dimensional qudit. The first logical qubit, the pivot, is sacrificed to enable the logical operations required to operate the memory. The 11 non-pivot logical qubits become one larger 211-dimensional qudit.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21898_figures/2605.21898_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: The left-hand figure illustrates our global memory system. 2 rows of the system are sacri- ficed for the fault-tolerant generation of the cat states used for syndrome extraction of neighbouring outer codeblocks. These ancillary blocks move through the system over the course of time to extract the syndrome of other codeblocks. On the right, we illustrate a Z error affecting an outer codeblock, labelled by some vector e ∈Fn q , where Fq is the finite field of q elements. The syndrome compon- ent of the qudit X-stabiliser (γ1, γ2, . . . , γn) ∈Fn q , which is some value in Fq, is extracted using the appropriate qudit cat state which we will go on to define. Full preliminaries on...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21898_figures/2605.21898_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Depiction of the fault-tolerant preparation and verification of the qudit cat state for n = 8 and R = 1. Every wire denotes a qudit, which may be imagined as the s non-pivot qubits of a cat state row gross code. The circuit proceeds via an initial non-fault-tolerant preparation, and then R rounds of fault-tolerant checking. Each set of n −1 measurements can be executed in two “layers”. In this diagram, all measurements are denoted by boxes. Those without meter symbols denote “measurement projections”, that is, measurements followed by the appropriate frame updates to ensure a trivial outcome. Those with meter symbols are not accompanied by frame updates following them. In this cat...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new quantum error correction architecture that concatenates algebraic outer codes with high-rate quantum LDPC inner codes. By treating inner code blocks as large-alphabet 'Galois qudits,' the scheme overcomes correlated error issues and provides a scalable path to the teraquop regime with significantly reduced space overhead.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Scaling high-rate quantum LDPC codes is difficult due to the 'discrete family' problem and the presence of correlated errors within high-rate inner code blocks. The authors seek to achieve the 'teraquop regime' with efficient space overhead and robust fault tolerance.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that concatenating quantum Reed-Solomon outer codes over 'gross' inner codes allows access to the teraquop regime with lower space overhead than existing architectures. They also prove that a lightweight fault-tolerance scheme, which fails for qubits, is effective for large-alphabet qudits.

**Method.** The approach uses code concatenation where inner code blocks are treated as single logical Galois qudits. They employ list decoding, a 'time-like' Reed-Solomon protection for syndrome extraction, and a 'spacetime error mixing' technique to bound failure probabilities.

**Model / system.** A modular superconducting hardware architecture using Bivariate Bicycle (BB) codes, specifically the [[144, 12, 12]] 'gross' code, concatenated with quantum Reed-Solomon outer codes operating on large-alphabet Galois qudits (q=2048).

**Key observables.** Logical error rates, space overhead (physical-to-logical qubit ratio), and block weight of errors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Physical noise rate of 10^-3, the teraquop regime (logical error rates of 10^-14 to 10^-13), and alphabet size q=2048.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes catastrophic errors are predominantly localized to individual chips and that non-local connectivity costs scale non-uniformly with range and degree.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares space overheads across various architectures (surface code, yoked surface codes, etc.) under a 500,000 physical qubit constraint, showing the concatenated gross code's path to the teraquop regime. Figure 4 illustrates the global memory system layout and the syndrome extraction process.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of scaling qLDPC codes, proposes a concatenated architecture using Galois qudits, develops a fault-tolerant syndrome extraction protocol, provides a mathematical framework for error bounding via error mixing, and concludes with performance analysis in the teraquop regime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Different quantum error correction schemes trade off overhead, error suppression, and hardware connectivity. Code concatenation can relax these tradeoffs by using an outer code whose non-local connectivity is supplied by logical operations of an inner code rather than directly by hardware. Prior works showed that this can reduce memory overhead for local low-rate inner codes such as the surface code. Here, we study concatenation over non-local, high-rate inner codes. Such inner codes experience correlated errors among the many logical qubits in a single codeblock. We handle this by treating each block as a single logical Galois qudit, enabling concatenation with algebraic outer codes with excellent parameters and, crucially, list decoders. In particular, we consider a memory system formed by concatenating quantum Reed-Solomon outer codes over the gross code. For fault-tolerant syndrome extraction, we develop a Galois qudit Shor scheme using "time-like" Reed-Solomon protection against measurement errors. Interestingly, a lightweight fault tolerance scheme, that would fail for qubits, works well for large-alphabet qudits, suggesting a very different theory of fault tolerance for such qudits. The whole protocol is optimised via improved bicycle instruction logical error rates, novel compilation strategies, and recent decoder post-selection rules.   At uniform $10^{-3}$ physical noise, the concatenated gross code reaches the teraquop regime, which it previously could not access, with a lower space overhead than the $288$-qubit two-gross code, while offering several advantages from the engineering standpoint. Beyond our main case study, we believe the core ideas of Galois qudits, quantum Reed-Solomon outer codes, and list decoding, will prove generically powerful and highly transferable ideas across high-rate quantum architectures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21648"></a>
### [Dropout Universality: Scaling Laws and Optimal Scheduling at the Edge-of-Chaos](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21648v1)

**Authors:** Lucas Fernandez Sarmiento  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21648v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21648_figures/2605.21648_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Critical scaling for smooth (tanh) and kinked (ReLU) activation functions, comparing tuning at zero dropout to tuning at the edge-of-chaos using a dropout field. The top row compares the different critical exponents at zero dropout and probes critical detuning decay, while the bottom row explores on critical networks with non-zero dropout. As the variables grow, higher-order effects become comparable and the linearized theory departs from the full recursion.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21648_figures/2605.21648_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Two-parameter crossover and scaling collapse of the dropout-deformed equation of state for the smooth universality class (tanh). Plots obtained using MFT recursion relations. The curves collapse onto a universal function after rescaling by ˜t and ˜m. The kinked counterpart is shown in Fig. 4.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 3. Magnitude of Hermite coefficients |an| for ReLU versus tanh. ReLU exhibits a slow (power-law) decay, reflecting multi-scale support across Hermite degrees, while tanh decays rapidly and concentrates most spectral mass in the lowest modes.</sub>

<sub>Figure 5. Finite-width MLP training and test curves at fixed mean dropout budget ¯h (with 0 ≤hℓ≤hmax). We compare uniform dropout, linear ramps (increasing/decreasing with depth), and step schedules that concentrate dropout in either the first or last half of the network, together with the no-dropout baseline.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper provides a theoretical framework to understand how dropout shifts the critical point of signal propagation in deep networks. It discovers that activation function smoothness determines the network's universality class and proves that applying dropout more heavily to early layers optimizes performance by preventing rank collapse.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding how dropout acts as a relevant perturbation that deforms the edge-of-chaos phase diagram and determining the optimal depth-dependent dropout schedule to prevent rank collapse.

**Main result.** Identified distinct universality classes for smooth vs. kinked activations and demonstrated that front-loading dropout (concentrating it in early layers) significantly improves generalization and prevents token diversity decay.

**Method.** Mean-field theory using a Landau-style expansion and renormalization group-like analysis of signal propagation, supplemented by numerical simulations on MLPs and Vision Transformers.

**Model / system.** Deep neural networks, specifically Multi-Layer Perceptrons (MLPs) and Vision Transformers (ViTs), analyzed in the infinite-width/large-channel limit.

**Key observables.** Order parameter (m), correlation length (xi), correlation susceptibility (chi), effective rank (participation ratio), and test loss/accuracy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dropout strength (h), detuning from criticality (t), activation function smoothness, and network depth/width ratio.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the large-width limit (L/N << 1) to control non-Gaussian corrections and i.i.d. Gaussian initialization.

**Figures summary.** Training curves for loss and accuracy under different schedules, visual comparisons of component-level ablations (Attention vs. MLP), and scaling collapse plots for different dropout strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of dropout-induced deformation of the critical point, develops a mean-field theory for smooth and kinked activation universality classes, derives scaling laws and optimal scheduling via variational calculus, and validates these predictions through empirical tests on MLPs and ViTs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a mean-field theory of dropout as a perturbation of critical signal propagation at the edge of chaos. Dropout shifts the perfect-alignment fixed point, making the depth scale for information propagation finite even at critical initialization. We derive critical and crossover scaling laws for correlation decay and establish that smooth activations and kinked, ReLU-like activations constitute distinct universality classes, with different critical exponents and a universal two-parameter scaling collapse in detuning and dropout strength. The distinction traces to the analytic structure of the correlation map: smooth activations admit a Taylor expansion near perfect alignment, while kinked activations develop a branch point with universal non-analyticity. As a corollary, the framework yields saturated dropout profiles under fixed budget; a rank-flow tie-breaker then selects front-loaded schedules, substantially reducing held-out test loss at no extra computational cost, with accuracy gains as a consistent secondary effect. We test the predictions in MLPs and Vision Transformers and discuss CNN/ResNet extensions.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21960"></a>
### [dSABRE: A SABRE-Style Router for Multi-Core Distributed Quantum Computers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21960v1)

**Authors:** Sanjiang Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21960v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21960_figures/2605.21960_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. DAG for a three-gate circuit on qubits q1, q2, q3. CX(q1, q2) and H(q3) share no qubit and have no incoming edges, so both appear in the initial front layer F = {g1, g2} and may execute in parallel. CX(q2, q3) depends on both via qubits q2 and q3; it enters F only after g1 and g2 have been executed. Circuit depth is 2 (two sequential layers).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21960_figures/2605.21960_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. DQC architectures used in this paper (B-grid and H-grid families introduced by TELESABRE [15]). Small grey circles are qubits; teal circles are inter-core communication ports; bold lines are EPR-channel links between cores. Each core is a 4×4 grid. (a) 2×2 B-grid. (b) 2×3 H-grid.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21960_figures/2605.21960_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. The DSABRE routing workflow. Each iteration drains the front layer (P1), classifies the remaining front into intra-core and inter-core gates (P2), applies one SWAP or one teleport (P3), and checkpoints when forward progress is made (P4).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21960_figures/2605.21960_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Running example for inter-core teleportation scoring (2×3 H-grid). Candidates A, B, and C below correspond row-by-row to Table I. Gate g = (q1, q2) has q1 in source core C1 (blue border) and q2 in target core C5. The router considers three candidates, each a triple (q1, cnext, (πs, πd)): A (teal) routes q1 towards C2; B (orange) routes towards C0, away from C5; C (violet) routes towards congested landing core C4 (orange border, fdst=2, where fdst counts free slots in cnext, not in ctgt). Step (1): intra-core SWAPs move q1 from p1 to staging slot ns (count shown on arrow). Step (2): a teleportation along link (πs, πd) delivers q1 to πd in cnext (dashed arc; score s annotated)....</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents dSABRE, a new routing algorithm designed to optimize circuit execution on distributed quantum computers. By specifically addressing core congestion and optimizing the cost of inter-core entanglement, it significantly reduces the number of required EPR pairs compared to existing methods. This is crucial for scaling quantum computing architectures where remote entanglement is a bottleneck.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Minimizing the consumption of EPR pairs and intra-core SWAP operations when routing quantum circuits on a multi-core distributed quantum computer (DQC).

**Main result.** The dSABRE router reduces geometric-mean EPR consumption by 41-44% over TELESABRE and 16-68% over pytket-dqc, demonstrating high scalability up to 360 qubits.

**Method.** A SABRE-style, lookahead-driven routing algorithm using a five-term gate-centric teleportation score, a proactive congestion-relief mechanism, and a BFS-layer construction for the inter-core extended set.

**Model / system.** A multi-core distributed quantum architecture consisting of multiple small quantum processing units (cores) interconnected via EPR links, modeled as a weighted graph with high-cost inter-core edges.

**Key observables.** Geometric-mean EPR consumption, intra-core SWAP costs, and inter-core teleportation costs.

**Important parameters / regimes.** SWAP cost (c_swap), teleportation cost (c_tele), capacity penalty (c_cap), lookahead decay parameter (gamma), and core capacity/demand thresholds.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the initial layout and communication-primitive selection stages are handled by other parts of the compilation pipeline; assumes a fixed grid-of-grids topology.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a circuit representation; Figure 3 illustrates the dSABRE routing workflow; Figure 4 provides a running example of inter-core teleportation scoring on an H-grid; Table I details scoring for specific candidates.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the routing problem, describes the dSABRE algorithm and its scoring mechanisms, presents ablation studies to validate individual components, compares performance against state-of-the-art baselines, and demonstrates scalability via large-scale QFT circuits.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Minimising EPR consumption is the dominant objective when routing a quantum circuit on a distributed quantum computer (DQC). We present dSABRE, a SABRE-style router for multi-core processors that, on each iteration of a lookahead-driven loop, first resolves any intra-core front-layer gates by SWAP scoring and only falls back to scoring inter-core teleportation candidates when the intra-core front is empty. Three mechanisms drive the improvement over the state of the art: a five-term gate-centric teleportation score that generalises the local SWAP heuristic to the inter-core setting, whose explicit capacity-penalty term keeps the scorer from teleporting into saturated cores; a proactive congestion-relief pass that redistributes idle qubits out of high-demand cores before deadlock; and a BFS-layer construction of the inter-core extended set that respects DAG dependencies layer by layer rather than mixing wires in topological order. Across 18 MQT-Bench circuits at 25, 36, and 64 logical qubits, dSABRE reduces geometric-mean EPR consumption by 41-44% over TeleSABRE and by 16-68% over the gate-teleportation-based pytket-dqc, using standard Qiskit SabreLayout for the initial layout. A large-circuit QFT sweep at 100-360 qubits confirms scalability. Code and online appendices are available at https://github.com/ebony72/dsabre.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21841"></a>
### [Effect of Pb Substitution at the Mo site on the Magnetic Properties of the Polar Magnet Fe$_2$Mo$_3$O$_8$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21841v1)

**Authors:** Takumi Shirasaki, Taichi Ishikawa, Shungo Nakayama, Hideki Kuwahara  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21841v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21841_figures/2605.21841_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. (a) Crystal structure of the M2Mo3O8 system (space group P63mc) visualized using VESTA [2]. The magnetic M2+ layers and Mo4+ layers are alternately stacked along the c-axis. (b) Honeycomb lattice formed by M2+ ions, occupying inequivalent octahedral and tetrahedral sites. (c) Kagome-like lattice formed by Mo4+ ions, in which three adjacent Mo ions form a spin- singlet trimer, rendering the layer non-magnetic.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

Asterisks denote peaks from unidentified impurities. (b) Powder X-ray diffraction patterns of polycrystalline Fe2(Mo1−xZrx)3O8 at RT. **Summary.** This study demonstrates that substituting non-magnetic ions into the Mo sites of the polar magnet Fe2Mo3O8 breaks up spin-singlet clusters, creating active spins that induce room-temperature ferromagnetism. This approach provides a strategy for designing new magnetoelectric materials by controlling non-magnetic cluster states.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how substituting non-magnetic Pb4+ and Zr4+ ions into the Mo sites of the polar magnet Fe2Mo3O8 disrupts Mo spin-singlet trimers to induce new magnetic functionalities.

**Main result.** The disruption of Mo spin-singlet trimers induces active spins in the Mo layer, leading to the emergence of ferromagnetic-like behavior that persists up to room temperature.

**Method.** Solid-state reaction synthesis followed by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) with Rietveld refinement and magnetic measurements using a vibrating sample magnetometer (VSM) up to 8 T.

**Model / system.** The system is the ternary transition-metal oxide Fe2Mo3O8, a polar magnet with a layered structure of magnetic Fe honeycomb lattices and non-magnetic Mo kagome lattices containing Mo4+ spin-singlet trimers.

**Key observables.** Magnetization (M) vs. temperature (T) and magnetic field (H), Neel temperature (TN), and magnetization increment (delta M).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Substitution level (x) from 0 to 0.20, Neel temperature TN approx 60 K, and effective spin S = 1/2 per active Mo ion.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a single dopant ion severs two of the three spin-singlet bonds in a trimer, leaving one intact dimer with localized spins.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the material and the problem of Mo-site substitution, describes the experimental synthesis and structural characterization, presents magnetic measurement results showing emergent ferromagnetism, and provides a quantitative statistical model to explain the observed magnetization increase.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The ternary transition-metal oxide Fe$_2$Mo$_3$O$_8$ is a polar magnet characterized by a layered structure of magnetic Fe honeycomb lattices and non-magnetic Mo kagome lattices. Whereas previous studies have primarily focused on the chemical substitution at the Fe sites to modulate the magnetic properties, the Mo sites have remained largely unexplored due to the strong spin-singlet trimerization of Mo$^{4+}$ ions. In this study, we investigated the effect of substituting non-magnetic Pb$^{4+}$ and Zr$^{4+}$ ions into the Mo sites to intentionally disrupt the Mo trimers. Our results reveal that the disruption of the Mo spin-singlet state induces active spins within the Mo layer, resulting in the emergence of a ferromagnetic-like behavior that persists even at room temperature. Quantitative analysis that takes into account the weight fraction of the main phase suggests an effective spin $S = 1/2$ state per active Mo ion upon trimer disruption. These findings demonstrate that controlling non-magnetic cluster states within a polar host via chemical substitution is a promising approach for designing room-temperature magnetoelectric materials.

</details>

### [Emergent magnetic and charge ordered phases in freestanding ultrathin \ce{LaVO3}](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22415v1)

**Authors:** Ashutosh Anand, Mukul Kabir

**Summary.** This paper uses DFT and Monte Carlo simulations to show how the magnetic and electronic properties of LaVO3 change when it is thinned down to a monolayer. It discovers that thickness and charge transfer from polar catastrophes can induce new ferromagnetic and charge-ordered phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the intrinsic electronic and magnetic properties of freestanding ultrathin LaVO3 films, specifically how thickness and charge transfer drive emergent magnetic and charge-ordered phases.

**Main result.** The study reveals a thickness-dependent magnetic transition from bulk-like C-type AFM to stripe-AFM in monolayers, and identifies that polar catastrophe-driven charge transfer in thicker films creates FM and stripe-AFM surface states.

**Method.** Density functional theory (DFT) using VASP with a Hubbard U correction, supplemented by classical Monte Carlo simulations using the Vampire code.

**Model / system.** Freestanding ultrathin LaVO3 films ranging from monolayer to 8 layers, modeled with an orthorhombic/monoclinic perovskite structure and a Heisenberg model for magnetic interactions.

**Key observables.** Magnetic phases (C-AFM, stripe-AFM, FM, Neel-AFM), charge ordering patterns, magnetic moments, and exchange interaction parameters (J1, J2, J_perp).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Film thickness (1L to 8L), doping concentrations (0.5h/fu, 0.25h/fu, etc.), and Hubbard U = 3 eV.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a classical Monte Carlo approach for magnetic transitions and the approximation of electron correlations via the DFT+U method.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1(a) shows the supercell structure; Figure 5 illustrates stripe and checkerboard charge ordering patterns and their relative energies.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the motivation of studying freestanding membranes, moves to the computational methodology, details the thickness-dependent magnetic transitions, explores polar catastrophe-driven charge transfer in thicker films, and concludes with an analysis of doping-induced charge ordering.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Transition metal oxide perovskites are an ideal platform for exploring the interplay between spin, orbital, charge and lattice degrees of freedom. Among them, \ce{LaVO3} has been extensively studied in heterostructures and superlattices, where exotic phases have been reported. Motivated by the advances in freestanding oxide membranes, we investigate the intrinsic properties of freestanding ultrathin \ce{LaVO3} films using density functional theory. Our calculations reveal a sequence of magnetic phase transitions with thickness, starting from stripe-AFM in monolayer until the bulk like C-AFM is recovered. Beyond four layers, polar catastrophe driven charge transfer dopes the surface layers giving rise to stripe-AFM and ferromagnetic surface states while the central layers remain bulk like. We further explore this fact by studying charge doped monolayer, discovering that hole doping drives the system into ferromagnetic state. Doping also induced charge ordering in the system. A striped charge ordering pattern is observed at 0.5 h/fu, while a 3:1 stripe pattern emerges at 0.25 h/fu, indicating that the periodicity of the superstructure changes with doping concentration.

</details>

### [Equilibrium Stabilization of a Hidden Phase Like Metallic State in 1T-TaS2](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22292v1)

<sub>Figure 4: Schematic phase diagram of 1T-TaS2 as a function of thickness and tem- perature. In bulk crystals, the well-known sequence of charge-density-wave (CDW) phases is recovered: commensurate (C-CDW, Mott insulating), nearly commensurate (NC), and incom- mensurate (IC), with the C-CDW transition occurring at ∼180 K on cooling and ∼240 K on warming. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the transient 'hidden' metallic phase of 1T-TaS2 can be stabilized as a stable equilibrium state by controlling the thickness of exfoliated flakes. This discovery provides a way to access and control competing electronic configurations in correlated materials without needing ultrafast laser excitation. The findings have significant implications for developing new phase-change technologies and quantum electronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand how to stabilize the metastable 'hidden' metallic phase of 1T-TaS2 in equilibrium and how electronic phases evolve with material thickness and temperature.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered that intermediate-thickness 1T-TaS2 flakes spontaneously host a stable, equilibrium metallic state (HPLMS) that mimics the transient hidden phase seen in bulk crystals under excitation.

**Method.** High-resolution angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (ARPES) was performed on mechanically exfoliated 1T-TaS2 flakes of varying thicknesses using the mu-ARPES technique.

**Model / system.** The physical system is 1T-TaS2 (tantalum disulfide), a layered transition metal dichalcogenide known for its complex charge density wave (CDW) and Mott insulating phases.

**Key observables.** Spectral weight at the Fermi level, metallic band (MB) intensity, hybridization gaps, Fermi surface maps, and energy-momentum (E-k) dispersions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Material thickness (ranging from 2.5 nm to 55 nm), temperature (50 K to 380 K), and out-of-plane momentum (kz).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the free-electron final-state approximation for determining out-of-plane momentum.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows dimensional control and thickness-dependent spectra; Figure 2 illustrates kz periodicity and Fermi surface maps in 25 nm flakes; Figure 3 details temperature-dependent EDCs and MDCs; Figure 4 presents a schematic phase diagram as a function of thickness and temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of metastable phases, describes the experimental setup and sample preparation, presents thickness-dependent spectroscopic results, analyzes temperature-driven phase evolution, and concludes with a proposed phase diagram.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Electronic phases that lie outside the equilibrium ground state offer a route to explore competing configurations in correlated materials. In 1T-TaS2, ultrafast excitation accesses a metallic hidden phase that is distinct from the commensurate insulating ground state. Here we use angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy to show that an equivalent electronic configuration is stabilized in exfoliated intermediate-thickness 1T-TaS2 flakes, where it persists up to room temperature before evolving through a different sequence of electronic transitions. This equilibrium hidden-phase-like state hosts a metallic band with finite Fermi-level spectral weight while retaining the characteristic hybridization gaps associated with the star-of-David band folding. These results establish a platform for controlling competing electronic states in layered materials, with implications for both quantum science and phase change technologies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21746"></a>
### [GeneCS: Synthesizing Resource-Efficient Code Surgery for Arbitrary Quantum Stabilizer Codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21746v1)

**Authors:** Junyu Zhou, Ali Javadi-Abhari, Gushu Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21746v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21746_figures/2605.21746_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Overview of GeneCS workflow</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21746_figures/2605.21746_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Ancilla system represented as a graph.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21746_figures/2605.21746_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Overview of code surgery construction</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21746_figures/2605.21746_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 6. Expand on path-matching graph. 𝛽: Cheeger con- stant.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21746_figures/2605.21746_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 7. Graph thickening and the trade-off between de- congestion and expansion</sub>

**Summary.** The paper introduces GeneCS, a compiler designed to automate the creation of efficient 'code surgery' protocols for quantum error correction. By treating the construction of ancilla systems as a graph optimization problem, it significantly reduces the physical qubit overhead required for logical operations without sacrificing error protection.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The high resource overhead (ancillary qubits and checks) required for performing fault-tolerant code surgery and cross-code communication between arbitrary stabilizer codes.

**Main result.** The GeneCS compiler achieves an average reduction of over 85% in ancillary qubits and checks for both single-code and cross-code operations while preserving logical error rates and scalability.

**Method.** A graph-based synthesis framework using three optimization strategies: structure-aware graph expansion, dynamic expansion-congestion optimization, and degree-constrained optimization via graph thickening and cellulation.

**Model / system.** The framework operates on quantum stabilizer codes, including topological codes (surface, color) and QLDPC codes (Bivariate Bicycle, Hypergraph Product, Quantum Tanner), modeled as low-dimensional chain complexes.

**Key observables.** Ancillary qubit/check overhead, code distance, logical error rate, and maximum stabilizer weight (degree).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cheeger constant (expansion), congestion (edge overlap), and maximum qubit/check degrees.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scalability results observed in surface codes are assumed to extend to other CSS stabilizer codes due to their locally repetitive structure.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the GeneCS workflow; Figure 2 maps surface codes to graphs; Figure 3 details graph modification; Figure 5-7 illustrate expansion, congestion, and graph thickening; Figure 9 shows logical error rate stability.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the code surgery problem, defines a graph-theoretic model for ancilla systems, presents the GeneCS compiler algorithms (expansion, congestion, and degree optimization), provides experimental benchmarks on various code families, and discusses scalability and error rate performance.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Efficiently realizing logical operations on general stabilizer codes remains a long-standing challenge in fault tolerant quantum computing. While code surgery provides a general framework with provable guarantees by joint logical measurements, existing constructions are largely theoretical and incur substantial ancilla overhead in practice. In this work, we propose GeneCS, a resource-efficient compiler for synthesizing code surgery protocols for arbitrary stabilizer codes. Our approach leverages structure-aware optimizations to eliminate redundancy in graph construction, dynamically balance expansion and congestion, and incorporate code degree constraints. Experimental results show that GeneCS achieves an average reduction of over $85\%$ in ancillary qubits and checks for both single-code and cross-code logical operations, while preserving logical error rates. Moreover, our compiler scales to codes with more than $10^4$ qubits with an amortized compilation time of about one second per instance. These results enable practical logical operations and efficient cross-code communication, thereby supporting the deployment of modern QLDPC codes and heterogeneous quantum architectures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21587"></a>
### [Large-flavor route to a stable U(1) Dirac spin liquid on the maple-leaf lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21587v1)

**Authors:** Yunchao Zhang, Andreas Feuerpfeil, Subir Sachdev, Ronny Thomale, Yasir Iqbal  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21587v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21587_figures/2605.21587_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Maple-leaf lattice and the generators of the microscopic space group used in the symmetry analysis. The unit cell contains six sites, and the relevant lattice symmetries are generated by translations T1, T2 and a sixfold rotation C6. (b) Pattern of mean-field amplitudes defining the uniform U(1) Dirac spin-liquid ansatz. The three inequivalent bond amplitudes are denoted by th, td, and tt.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21587_figures/2605.21587_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Mean-field spinon dispersions of the maple-leaf-lattice Dirac spin liquid obtained from the Hamiltonian in Eq. (10) at tt = 0. For th/td = −0.75 and at half-filling, the spectrum contains six Dirac nodes along the ΓM lines, related by C6 symmetry. This six-valley structure gives the infrared Nf = 12 QED3 theory studied in the main text. For comparison, at th/td = 1 the spectrum has two Dirac nodes at Γ; this distinct Nf = 4 mean-field DSL is unstable to infinitesimal changes of the hopping parameters.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates whether a large number of fermion flavors can stabilize a U(1) Dirac spin liquid on the maple-leaf lattice. It identifies that the stability is not symmetry-protected but depends on the dynamical irrelevance of specific allowed monopoles. The work provides specific spectral predictions to guide numerical studies using exact diagonalization.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the stability of a U(1) Dirac spin liquid (DSL) on the maple-leaf lattice against the proliferation of monopoles that could cause confinement.

**Main result.** The authors show that the maple-leaf lattice realizes a large-flavor QED3 theory with Nf=12, and while five spin-singlet monopoles are symmetry-allowed, their stability depends on whether they are dynamically irrelevant.

**Method.** Theoretical framework using fermionic parton construction, projective symmetry group (PSG) analysis, 't Hooft anomaly matching, and Wannier-center decomposition.

**Model / system.** A U(1) Dirac spin liquid on a frustrated maple-leaf lattice, described by an effective (2+1)D QED3 theory with Nf=12 Dirac fermions.

**Key observables.** Monopole quantum numbers (spin, lattice, and time-reversal), scaling dimensions of monopole operators, and symmetry-resolved spectral content.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Flavor number Nf=12, scaling dimension of the charge-one monopole (Delta_1), and hopping parameters (th, td, tt).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The stability of the phase is assumed to depend on the dynamical irrelevance of symmetry-allowed monopoles rather than symmetry protection.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the maple-leaf lattice structure and mean-field bond amplitudes; Figure 2 illustrates spinon dispersions and the Nf=4 regime.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the maple-leaf lattice as a large-flavor platform, derives the effective QED3 theory, performs a symmetry classification of monopoles, verifies anomaly matching, and provides numerical predictions for testing the phase.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The $\mathrm{U}(1)$ Dirac spin liquid provides a useful organizing framework for frustrated magnets: it offers an algebraic parent state from which competing orders, confinement patterns, and low-energy spectral features can be understood. Whether such a state can occur as a stable ground state of a two-dimensional spin Hamiltonian remains an open question, because monopole events of the compact gauge field can proliferate and confine the spinons. Here, we show that the maple-leaf lattice provides a distinct route to this problem. Its Dirac spin liquid realizes QED$_3$ with $N_f=12$ Dirac fermions, substantially more than the $N_f=4$ theories of the triangular and kagome lattices. We classify the fundamental monopoles under the full microscopic symmetry group and find five charge-one spin-singlet monopoles that are trivial under lattice symmetries, time reversal, and spin rotation. The phase is therefore not protected by symmetry in the usual sense: its stability depends on whether these allowed monopoles are dynamically irrelevant. Available large-$N_f$ and Monte Carlo estimates place the charge-one monopole dimension close to the relevance threshold in $(2+1)$ dimensions, making the maple-leaf lattice a concrete large-flavor platform for testing the stability of compact QED$_3$ in a quantum magnet. The same monopole classification gives direct numerical predictions, identifying the symmetry sectors in which singlet, triplet, and quintet monopole excitations should appear. This provides a route to testing the $N_f=12$ Dirac spin liquid through symmetry-resolved exact diagonalization and variational studies of maple-leaf spin Hamiltonians.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21616"></a>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21616_figures/2605.21616_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Schematics of (a) a multi-domain state in zero ﬁeld, (b) a set of orientational domain states selected by an in-plane ﬁeld Hb, (c), (d) Sets of antiphase domain states selected by an out-of-plane ﬁeld Hc, and (e) a single-domain state selected by an oblique ﬁeld Ho. (f) Magnetic susceptibilities of MnTe along the c-axis (χc, green), along the b-axis after ZFC (χZFC b , yellow), and along the b-axis after 5 T FC (χFC b , blue). The diﬀerence ∆χb = χFC b −χZFC b is scaled up by a factor of 10 and shown in gray. (g) Spontaneous WFM along the c-axis, mspon c , in MnTe. The excitation ﬁeld during the measurement (Hexc) was zero. The data were collected upon warming. (h) Cooling-ﬁeld...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21616_figures/2605.21616_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Components of the NMI vector, (a) Ry and (b) Rz, measured after ZFC, OFC, and FC at the Bragg peaks (001), (101), (003), (103), (201), and (203) at 100 K. Gaussian ﬁts with areas ﬁxed to the calculated values are shown as solid lines. The ﬁeld in OFC setup was applied in the bc-plane as shown in Fig. 2(e), and the ﬁeld in FC setup was applied in the c-axis. (c) Temperature dependence of Rz at (101) after 2.5 mT FC. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>FIG. 4. σM y (red) and σM z (blue) obtained using z-polarization after (a) ZFC, (b) OFC, (c) FC at 2.5 mT, and (d) FC at −2.5 mT at the pure magnetic Bragg peak (001) at 100 K. **Summary.** This paper provides direct experimental evidence for the existence and controllability of altermagnetic order in bulk MnTe. By using polarized neutron diffraction, the authors show that the Néel vector can be manipulated with very small magnetic fields, which is a crucial step toward using altermagnets in spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining if altermagnetic order in bulk MnTe can be directly observed and controlled by external magnetic fields in the absence of net magnetization.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated that the altermagnetic Néel vector and the associated weak ferromagnetic moment (WFM) can be switched using milli-Tesla-scale magnetic field cooling, providing direct evidence of a net Néel vector in the bulk.

**Method.** Polarized Neutron Diffraction (PoND) in half-polarized mode was used to probe nuclear-magnetic interference terms, complemented by magnetic susceptibility and spontaneous magnetization measurements.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on bulk MnTe, a prototype altermagnet with a centrosymmetric hexagonal lattice (space group P63/mmc) and an A-type antiferromagnetic ground state.

**Key observables.** Nuclear-magnetic interference (NMI) vector R, weak ferromagnetic moment (WFM), Néel vector (n), and magnetic susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Néel temperature TN = 307 K, magnetic field scales in the milli-Tesla range, and the presence of spin-orbit coupling (SOC).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the WFM arises from an imbalance in antiphase domains rather than from domain walls.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the MnTe domain states and the NMI vector mechanism; Fig 2 illustrates Néel vector configurations and susceptibility/WFM measurements; Fig 3 presents the NMI vector components under different cooling conditions and their temperature dependence.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of altermagnetism, describes the experimental setup for polarized neutron diffraction, presents the observation of NMI as evidence for the Néel vector, demonstrates domain switching via field cooling, and discusses the origin of the weak ferromagnetic moment.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnetic order, characterized by the Néel vector, breaks time-reversal symmetry (TRS) even in the nonrelativistic limit. Although spin-polarized and anomalous transport phenomena emerge with this order, they are mutually compensated by TRS-connected antiphase domains with opposite Néel vectors. Here we employ polarized neutron diffraction to directly probe the altermagnetic order in MnTe. Pronounced nuclear-magnetic interference terms were observed, providing direct evidence of a net Néel vector in the bulk crystal. Moreover, a weak ferromagnetic moment (WFM), originating from relativistic spin-orbit coupling, was found to be coupled with the altermagnetic order. Both the altermagnetic order and the WFM can be switched by milli-Tesla-scale magnetic field cooling.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22803"></a>
### [Persistence of asymptotic variance under transport: from hyperfluctuation to stealthy hyperuniformity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22803v1)

**Authors:** Luca Lotz, Michael A. Klatt  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22803v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22803_figures/2605.22803_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Illustration of our construction of an isotropic point process that is hyperuniform with an exceptionally large scaling exponent. In black and fading out from left to right, we show a realization of a random fair tiling, i.e., a random division of space into cells of equal volume; specifically, a realization of a fair STIT; see Subsection 5.1. To obtain the point process shown in blue, we concentrate the mass that is uniformly distributed over each cell into n points while preserving the first p moments, i.e., satisfying (1.8). Here, n = 12 and p = 6; see the cell highlighted in red. By our main Theorem 4.13 about the persistence of p-uniformity under transport, this point process...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a mathematical framework to understand how density fluctuations, ranging from hyperfluctuation to stealthy hyperuniformity, behave under random mass transport. It solves an open problem regarding the persistence of these fluctuations in dimensions d >= 3 and introduces new, efficiently simulatable point processes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the conditions under which the scaling of density fluctuations (p-uniformity) is preserved when a random measure undergoes a random transport process.

**Main result.** The authors establish a persistence theorem stating that p-uniformity is preserved under transport if the transport distance has a finite (d+p)-th moment and certain expansion terms are controlled.

**Method.** The study uses Fourier analysis of the Bartlett spectral measure, Taylor expansions of the transport kernel, and moment conditions on transport distance.

**Model / system.** The model involves stationary random complex measures on R^d, including point processes and mass-redistribution (transport) kernels that move mass from a source to a destination.

**Key observables.** p-uniformity exponent, asymptotic variance, Bartlett spectral measure, and structure factor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spatial dimension (d), hyperuniformity exponent (p), and transport distance moments.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The measures are assumed to be stationary (translation-invariant) and locally square-integrable; the expansion requires smoothness of test functions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the construction of a random fair tiling and a resulting point process, demonstrating an isotropic process with a very high scaling exponent.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining p-uniformity and transport kernels, develops a Taylor expansion for the destination measure, proves the main persistence theorem, and concludes with new point process constructions and an outlook on the converse statement.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce $p$-uniformity to characterize the scaling of density fluctuations in spatial random systems in $\mathbb{R}^d$, ranging from hyperfluctuation to stealthy hyperuniformity. Our central theorem establishes sufficient conditions to preserve $p$-uniformity under transport. The first condition, a finite $(d+p)$-th moment of the transport distance, allows for a Taylor expansion of the transport. The second condition controls the corresponding terms. We thus solve a previously stated open problem; indeed we extend it, since our result applies to a general $p$-uniform source in any dimension, and the source and transport may be dependent. As an application, we construct new classes of point processes that are isotropic and $p$-uniform with arbitrarily high $p$, and that can be simulated in linear time. We conclude with an outlook on a converse statement.

</details>

### [Reinforcement learning for ion shuttling on trapped-ion quantum computers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22463v1)

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22463_figures/2605.22463_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Comparison of ion shuttling durations using trajecto- ries optimized by different methods on the QVLS X-chip. For each type of problem within the MQT dataset, the largest ex- ample (the number at the end of the problem name indicates the qubit number) that could still be optimized by the SAT solver is shown. On average, the shuttling sequences gener- ated by our proposed RL compiler are 2.6 % worse than the optimal solutions of the SAT solver and 14.0 % better than those of the heuristic compiler. The SAT solver requires 12 h per problem on average, our approach 1.1 s.</sub>

Shaded areas indicate 95 % confidence intervals. With a com- parable inference time budget, our proposed compiler finds significantly more efficient ion shuttling trajectories. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

**Summary.** The paper introduces RLIonS, a reinforcement learning-based compiler designed to optimize the movement of ions in QCCD-based quantum computers. By treating shuttling as a sequential decision problem, the method significantly reduces the number of required transport operations compared to existing heuristics. This reduction is crucial for executing larger quantum circuits within the limited coherence times of trapped-ion qubits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for the development of scalable quantum hardware, as it addresses the critical classical control bottleneck of managing qubit movement to maintain coherence in trapped-ion systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing ion shuttling sequences in QCCD-based trapped-ion quantum computers to minimize latency and overhead caused by qubit transport.

**Main result.** The proposed RLIonS reinforcement learning compiler achieves up to a 36.3% reduction in shuttling operations compared to state-of-the-art heuristics and remains near-optimal relative to exact SAT solvers.

**Method.** A reinforcement learning approach using Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) and a ResNet architecture, formulated as a Semi-Markov Decision Process (SMDP) with a novel sinusoidal encoding for chip states.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the Quantum Charge-Coupled Device (QCCD) architecture for trapped-ion quantum computing, specifically evaluating X-chip and Q-chip (storage ring) layouts.

**Key observables.** Number of shuttling operations (steps), shuttling duration, and compilation/inference time.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth (k_lookahead), number of ions (up to 50), and the discount rate (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes deterministic transitions between states and that gates beyond a specific lookahead depth do not influence immediate decisions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Q-chip layout; Figure 2 illustrates the RLIonS encoding pipeline from chip state to matrix; Figure 3 compares compile time and shuttling duration across methods; Figure 4 shows performance scaling with Quantum Volume; Figure 6 presents an ablation study on sinusoidal vs. linear encoding.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the shuttling bottleneck, describes the QCCD architectures, details the RLIonS RL framework (SMDP formulation and encoding), presents benchmarking against SAT and heuristic methods, provides ablation studies on encoding and rewards, and concludes with future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scalable trapped-ion quantum computing is commonly realized with modular chips that feature distinct zones with specific functionalities, such as storage, state preparation, and gate execution. To execute a quantum circuit, the ions must be transported between these zones. This process is called ion shuttling. To achieve reliable computation results, the shuttling process must be optimized. However, as the number of ions increases, this becomes a high-dimensional optimization problem where optimal solutions cannot be computed efficiently. We demonstrate, to the best of our knowledge, the first use of reinforcement learning (RL) for the optimization of ion shuttling. RL is well-suited for such scenarios, as it enables learning a strategy through direct interaction with the problem. We show that our RL approach outperforms current state-of-the-art heuristic techniques, yielding a reduction in shuttling operations of up to 36.3 %. Furthermore, we show that our method is easily applicable to various chip architectures. Our approach offers a versatile method to study shuttling efficiency during chip design and, therefore, a highly relevant tool for future, more complex architectures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Revisiting the high-field phase diagram of the topological cubic helimagnet SrFeO$_{3}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22517v1)

**Summary.** This paper revisits the complex magnetic phase diagram of the topological helimagnet SrFeO3 under high magnetic fields. It discovers a new intermediate phase and demonstrates that the transition to the forced ferromagnetic state involves a valence transition related to ligand-hole density redistribution. These findings highlight the critical role of electronic itinerancy in stabilizing complex multiple-Q magnetic structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Resolving the microscopic mechanism of multiple-Q phases in the topological cubic helimagnet SrFeO3 and understanding the field-orientation dependence of its magnetic phase diagram.

**Main result.** The study identifies a new intermediate-field phase (Phase VI) and provides evidence for a valence transition driven by the redistribution of ligand-hole density when entering the forced ferromagnetic phase.

**Method.** The researchers combined pulsed high-field magnetization, magnetostriction, and ultrasound measurements with single-crystal neutron scattering and Monte Carlo simulations using an effective spin Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The system is SrFeO3, a cubic perovskite itinerant magnet characterized by a ligand-hole 3d5L electronic configuration. The theoretical framework uses an effective bilinear-biquadratic spin Hamiltonian with cubic single-ion anisotropy.

**Key observables.** Magnetization (M), differential susceptibility (dM/dH), longitudinal and transverse magnetostriction, longitudinal sound velocity, and magnetic Bragg peak intensities (Q1-Q4).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field strengths up to 50 T, temperatures down to 4.2 K, field orientations H || [001] and H || [111], and the modulation vector q approx 0.13 r.l.u.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The effective spin model assumes that the essential magnetic features can be captured by bilinear and biquadratic interactions and cubic anisotropy, while the valence transition is treated as a subtle redistribution of ligand-hole density.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the crystal structure; Figure 3 displays the metamagnetic transition, negative volume jump, and elastic anomalies; Supplemental figures show the evolution of neutron scattering profiles and integrated intensities of magnetic reflections.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the system and problem, details the experimental and computational methods, presents the reconstructed high-field phase diagram and new phase discovery, discusses the role of anisotropy and valence transitions, and concludes with a summary of the microscopic mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The cubic perovskite SrFeO$_{3}$ is a prototypical centrosymmetric itinerant magnet that hosts a quadruple-${\mathbf Q}$ hedgehog-antihedgehog lattice and exhibits a complex magnetic-field-temperature phase diagram. Yet, the microscopic mechanism underlying the emergence of its versatile multiple-${\mathbf Q}$ phases remains unresolved. Here, we elucidate the field-orientation dependence of the magnetic phase diagram and establish an effective spin Hamiltonian for SrFeO$_{3}$ that incorporates a cubic single-ion anisotropy together with bilinear and biquadratic interactions in momentum space. In addition, we observe magnetoelastic signatures of a first-order valence transition upon entering the forced FM phase at 40 T, which would be attributed to the suppression of negative charge transfer. These findings emphasize the pivotal importance of electronic itinerancy arising from the formation of a ligand-hole band in stabilizing multiple-${\mathbf Q}$ phases.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21585"></a>
### [Robust fluctuating intertwined charge stripes in the Emery model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21585v1)

**Summary.** This paper uses DMRG and DQMC to show that the Emery model supports robust, intertwined charge and spin stripes. It specifically highlights how oxygen orbital structure contributes to charge modulation and how temperature-driven symmetry crossovers link nematicity to stripe formation.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether the low-energy physics of the single-band Hubbard model is equivalent to the multi-band Emery model and explores the nature of intertwined spin and charge stripes and nematicity within the Emery model.

**Main result.** The researchers confirm the presence of oxygen-centered charge stripes and demonstrate that cooling the system triggers a crossover from B2g to B1g symmetry-breaking tendencies, linking nematicity to charge stripes.

**Method.** The study utilizes complementary Density Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) for ground-state simulations and Determinant Quantum Monte Carlo (DQMC) for finite-temperature properties.

**Model / system.** The Emery model, a three-band Hubbard model representing the CuO2 plane, consisting of copper-centered d-orbitals and oxygen-centered p-orbitals.

**Key observables.** Static charge and spin correlation functions, bond-charge nematic susceptibilities (B1g and B2g), and kinetic energy anisotropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Parameters include hopping integrals (tpd, tpp), Hubbard repulsions (Udd, Upp), charge transfer energy (pd), and hole doping levels.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The DQMC simulations are limited by the fermion sign problem, which restricts the exploration of very low temperatures or high interaction strengths.

**Figures summary.** Figures show real-space DQMC patterns of spin and charge stripes, momentum-space correlations illustrating the Yamada relation, and the temperature evolution of symmetry-breaking modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of model equivalence, describes the Emery model parameters, presents DMRG ground-state results, details DQMC finite-temperature findings regarding stripe evolution and nematicity, and concludes with the connection between orbital physics and stripe directionality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The single-band Hubbard model is one of the most extensively studied models in condensed matter physics, giving rise to intertwined spin and charge stripes that coexist with, or lie in the vicinity of, superconductivity in the phase diagram. However, whether the low energy physics of the single-band Hubbard model is fully equivalent to the multi-band (multi-orbital) Emery model remains an unsettled question. While the intertwined stripes and nematicity have been studied in the single-band Hubbard model, a comprehensive picture in the Emery model is lacking. In this paper, we focus on the less investigated intertwined charge stripes using complementary density matrix renormalization group (DMRG) and determinant quantum Monte Carlo (DQMC) techniques. Our ground state DMRG confirms the presence of the oxygen-centered charge stripes at a reduced amplitude in the Emery model parameter regime widely used in the study of intertwined stripes. Close analysis of the oxygen orbital structure of the static charge correlation function from DQMC reveals the charge stripe pattern in real-space, showcasing stronger charge density modulation on $p$-orbitals pointing along ``the rivers of charge'', consistent with DMRG. For the parameter set with the largest fermion signs, we managed to reach a temperature where the system first demonstrated tendencies to form purely unidirectional spin and charge stripes, and the $B_{1g}$ component becomes dominant in the bond-charge nematic susceptibility. This observation correlates with the doping dependence of the kinetic energy anisotropy, suggesting a close relation between the nematicity and charge stripes in the Emery model.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22318"></a>
### [Shielded inner-shell transitions in atomic samarium for tests of fundamental physics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22318v1)

**Authors:** R. Aramyan, D. Budker, V. A. Dzuba, V. V. Flambaum, S. G. Porsev, M. S. Safronova, O. Tretiak, K. Zhang

**Summary.** This paper reports the first experimental identification of a previously unobserved inner-shell energy level in neutral samarium. The discovery is significant because the 4f shell is shielded from external perturbations, making samarium an ideal candidate for high-precision tests of parity violation and searches for variations in the fine-structure constant.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant for AMO physicists as it presents a new atomic platform for precision spectroscopy, explores the interplay between atomic structure and fundamental symmetry tests, and discusses the feasibility of laser cooling and clock applications in a complex multi-electron system.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The search for physics beyond the Standard Model through low-energy tests of fundamental symmetries, specifically identifying and characterizing atomic transitions in samarium for parity violation studies.

**Main result.** The researchers successfully identified the previously unobserved 4f^6 6s^2 5D0 energy level in neutral samarium and demonstrated that its inner-shell nature provides a shielded, high-precision window for testing nuclear spin-dependent parity violation.

**Method.** The study combines dual-frequency-comb absorption spectroscopy and double-resonance population-depleation to identify energy levels, with many-body calculations (CI and MBPT) to predict transition properties and sensitivity coefficients.

**Model / system.** The physical system is neutral samarium (Sm) atoms in a high-temperature vapor cell, focusing on transitions involving the 4f inner-shell, specifically the 7F0 to 5D0 transition.

**Key observables.** Transition energy (14,564.90 cm-1), pressure broadening and shift, metastable lifetime, quality factor, and nuclear spin-dependent parity-violation amplitude.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quality factor Q ~ 3x10^14, metastable lifetime ~ 120 ms, pressure broadening ~ 0.12 MHz/torr, and sensitivity coefficients for the fine-structure constant.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theoretical calculations neglect QED corrections and assume a single-particle valence-nucleon model for estimating nuclear interaction constants.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for BSM tests, describes the experimental identification of the 5D0 level using frequency combs, presents the characterization of the 4f shell shielding via pressure studies, provides theoretical many-body calculations of transition properties, and concludes with proposed applications for parity violation and atomic clocks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Forbidden atomic transitions provide some of the most stringent low-energy tests of physics beyond the Standard Model, with sensitivity set by the interplay between the sought-for signals and systematics suppressed by symmetry. Here we identify the previously unobserved $4f^{6}6s^{2}\,{}^{5}$D$_{0}$ level of neutral samarium at $14\,564.90(2)\,\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$, opening the ${}^{7}$F$_{0}\rightarrow{}^{5}$D$_{0}$ inner-shell transition for precision spectroscopy. Candidate lines extracted from dual-comb absorption spectra were assigned using double-resonance population-depletion and sequential-excitation measurements. The observed pressure broadening, $0.12(2)\,\mathrm{MHz/torr}$, and pressure shift, $0.145(4)\,\mathrm{MHz/torr}$, indicate an inner-shell $4f$-transition shielded from external perturbations. Many-body calculations predict a $\sim\!120\,\mathrm{ms}$ metastable lifetime (quality factor $\mathcal{Q}\sim 3\times 10^{14}$), large sensitivity coefficients for variation of the fine-structure constant, and a nuclear-spin-dependent parity-violation amplitude comparable to that of cesium. Crucially, the $J=0\rightarrow J=0$ selection rule suppresses by symmetry both the nuclear-spin-independent parity-violation channel and the M1 and E2 backgrounds that complicated previous heavy-atom experiments, yielding a uniquely clean window onto the nuclear anapole moment. The two stable spin-$7/2$ isotopes of samarium provide a remarkable opportunity to largely cancel atomic-structure uncertainties by measuring the ratio of parity-violation effects in the two isotopes. These results establish neutral samarium as a platform for inner-shell precision spectroscopy and tests of physics beyond the Standard Model.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22764"></a>
### [Spin Glass Mapping of the Parallel Minority Game](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22764v1)

**Authors:** Aryan Tyagi, Soumyaditya Das, Soumyajyoti Biswas, Anirban Chakraborti  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22764v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22764_figures/2605.22764_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Variance evolution for D=101 and different values of g across time, for various strategies: A , B and C. The variance decreases initially and then stabilizes.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22764_figures/2605.22764_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Time evolution of the Hamiltonian (Eg. 7) for D=101 and different values of g, various strategies: A , B and C. The Hamiltonian decreases and reaches a saturation indicat- ing convergence to local minima of the spin energy landscape.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the optimization problem in the Parallel Minority Game is mathematically equivalent to finding the ground state of a Sherrington-Kirkpatrick spin glass. This mapping provides a theoretical framework to understand the emergence of glassy, sub-optimal states in resource allocation problems. It also reveals that the system's energy function can saturate much faster than the actual population variance.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the difficulty of minimizing population variance in the Parallel Minority Game (PMG) and seeks to understand the origin of 'frozen' sub-optimal configurations and glassy behavior in stochastic strategies.

**Main result.** The authors prove that minimizing population variance in the PMG is exactly equivalent to finding the ground state of an Ising spin glass (Sherrington-Kirkpatrick model) in the mean-field limit.

**Method.** The study uses a mathematical mapping that encodes agent choices as binary spin variables to transform the population variance formula into a quadratic Ising Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The system is the Parallel Minority Game (PMG), where N agents choose between two fixed locations from D possible sites. The problem is mapped to an Ising spin glass Hamiltonian with quenched random couplings (J_ij) and random fields (h_i).

**Key observables.** Population variance (efficiency metric), energy per spin (Hamiltonian value), and energy saturation time scales.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of agents (N), number of locations (D), target population (g), and switching probability (p_i).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mapping assumes a mean-field limit; the analysis of strategies assumes specific rules for how agents update their choices based on population information.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the time evolution of population variance for different strategies and target populations; Figure 2 shows the evolution of the spin glass Hamiltonian energy reaching saturation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the PMG problem, establishes the mathematical mapping to the Ising spin glass, analyzes the dynamics of different stochastic strategies, and discusses the implications of the energy-variance time scale discrepancy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The parallel minority game (PMG) extends the classical minority game to many choices, with each agent restricted to two predetermined alternatives. In this condition, minimizing the population variance across all choices is a complex combinatorial optimization problem. We show that this minimization is exactly equivalent to finding the ground state of an Ising spin glass in the mean-field limit, i.e., the Sherrington-Kirkpatrick model. By encoding the agent choices as spin variables, the variance becomes a quadratic Hamiltonian with quenched random couplings $J_{ij}$ and random fields $h_i$. This mapping reveals inherent frustration and connects the PMG to the well developed theory of spin glasses, providing a new perspective on the frozen, sub-optimal configurations observed in stochastic strategies.

</details>

### [The cell fluid model with Curie-Weiss interactions: special cases and analytical results](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22520v1)
