**Summary.** This paper provides the first explicit analytical solutions for the phase behavior of a cell fluid model with competing long-range attraction and short-range repulsion. It establishes that the model recovers van der Waals-like behavior in certain limits and proves the mathematical stability of the system even at the boundary of thermodynamic collapse.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to provide explicit analytical results for the Cell Fluid Model (CFM) with Curie-Weiss interactions, specifically addressing the lack of analytical descriptions for phase behavior, critical parameters, and the convergence of the partition function at the boundary of thermodynamic stability.

**Main result.** The authors derive closed-form expressions for the equation of state, critical points, and phase curves (binodal/spinodal) in the strong-repulsion limit, and prove that the partition function remains convergent at the marginal stability boundary (J1 = J2).

**Method.** The study employs a grand-canonical ensemble approach using Laplace's method, asymptotic expansions of deformed exponential functions, and Landau expansion near the critical point.

**Model / system.** A Cell Fluid Model (CFM) where a macroscopic volume is divided into non-overlapping cubic cells. The Hamiltonian features a competition between global Curie-Weiss attraction (J1) and local, short-range, soft-core repulsion (J2) between particles in the same cell.

**Key observables.** Equation of state, critical point parameters (Tc, Pc, density), binodal and spinodal curves, order parameter, and pressure isotherms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling constants J1 (attraction) and J2 (repulsion); the ratio f = J2/J1; temperature T; and chemical potential mu.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis primarily focuses on the thermodynamic limit (N -> infinity) and utilizes the large-f (strong-repulsion) approximation for several analytical derivations.

**Figures summary.** Phase diagrams showing coexistence lines and spinodal curves (P-T and density-T planes), pressure isotherms illustrating van der Waals loops, and plots of the energy function E(y) demonstrating the transition from single to double maxima.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the model definition and stability conditions, moves to the analytical derivation of the strong-repulsion limit and critical parameters, extends the analysis to the marginal stability case (J1=J2) via asymptotic expansion, and concludes with the construction of the full thermodynamic equation of state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Inspired by previous extensive numerical studies of a cell fluid model with Curie-Weiss interactions, we concentrate on some analytically tractable special cases in its description. The key ingredient of the model is a competition between global attraction and local repulsion interactions between particles with coupling constants $J_1$ and $J_2$, respectively. We provide analytical results in several limiting cases, including the ideal-gas limit $J_1=J_2=0$ and the strong-repulsion limit $J_2\gg J_1$. For $J_2\gg J_1$, a detailed analytical study is presented. We derive explicit expressions for the critical point parameters, the equation of state, and the binodal and spinodal curves in closed form. The equation of state is found to be in full agreement with that of the van der Waals lattice gas, and the order parameter satisfies the standard Curie-Weiss equation. In a neighborhood of the critical point, a Landau expansion is shown to have the same form and symmetry as that of the classical lattice gas within the mean-field approximation. Moreover, based on the explicit knowledge of a few leading terms in the asymptotic expansion of the deformed exponential function governing the physics of the cell model, we extend its validity range to include the marginal case of thermodynamic stability, $J_1=J_2$. In particular, this extension makes a consideration of the ideal-gas limit $J_1=J_2=0$ formally legitimate. For the generic marginal case $J_1=J_2\ne0$ systematically avoided in previous works, we present numerical data and phase diagrams that augment their findings for $J_2>J_1$.

</details>

### [Tunneling spectra of $\mathrm{TaO}_x$ junctions for van der Waals superconductors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21631v1)

**Authors:** Yixuan Niu, Jun Cheng, Shiji Ding, Zhongxin Guo, Shang Wang, Chenglong Li, Meining Zhang, Peng Cai

**Summary.** This paper presents a new method for studying van der Waals superconductors using a stable, TaOx-based planar tunneling junction. The platform allows for high-precision spectroscopy across large temperature and magnetic field ranges, successfully replicating complex electronic features like the pseudogap seen in Bi2212. This provides a robust alternative to Scanning Tunneling Spectroscopy for probing the properties of 2D quantum materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of performing high-quality, stable tunneling spectroscopy to track electronic evolution across temperature and magnetic fields in van der Waals superconductors, due to the limitations of Scanning Tunneling Spectroscopy (STS) and the technical hurdles of traditional planar junction barriers.

**Main result.** The development of a high-quality TaOx-based planar tunneling junction that successfully reproduces the intrinsic electronic signatures of Bi2212, such as the superconducting gap and pseudogap, with superior stability across wide temperature and magnetic field ranges.

**Method.** Magnetron sputtering was used to fabricate a TaOx-based barrier heterostructure, and planar tunneling spectroscopy was performed alongside transport measurements to analyze differential conductance and resistance.

**Model / system.** A planar tunneling junction architecture consisting of a multilayer stack (substrate, Au, Ta, TaOx) in contact with a van der Waals superconductor flake, specifically Bi2Sr2CaCu2O8+d (Bi2212).

**Key observables.** Differential conductance (dI/dV), superconducting energy gap (Delta_SC), transition temperature (Tc), pseudogap, and resistance-temperature (R-T) curves.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature range (1.5 K to 250 K), magnetic field up to 8 T, and bias voltage.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The TaOx barrier is assumed to be sufficiently uniform and stable to act as a reliable proxy for ultra-high vacuum STS measurements.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the device fabrication workflow and architecture; Fig 2 displays temperature-dependent transport and spectroscopy data including Tc identification; Fig 3 shows the evolution of dI/dV spectra under varying temperature and magnetic fields; Fig 4 provides a comparative analysis of the TaOx junction against STS and AlOx junctions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the technical challenge of spectroscopy in vdW materials, describes the fabrication of the TaOx-based junction, presents experimental validation using Bi2212 (including temperature and field dependence), and concludes with a comparison to established spectroscopic methods.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tunneling spectroscopy and its evolution are crucial for elucidating the intricate electronic structure and emergent phenomena in quantum materials.Nevertheless, high-quality measurements -- specifically those tracking evolution across temperature and external fields -- remain a formidable challenge. We have fabricated a high-quality $\mathrm{TaO}_x$-based planar tunneling junction by using magnetron sputtering for van der Waals (vdW) superconductors. Using the vdW superconductor $\mathrm{Bi}_2\mathrm{Sr}_2\mathrm{CaCu}_2\mathrm{O}_{8+δ}$ (Bi2212) as a benchmark, this platform yields high-quality tunneling spectra, reproducing the electronic signatures obtained from scanning tunneling spectra acquired from atomically clean surfaces under ultra-high vacuum conditions. This architecture enables high-precision spectroscopy across extensive temperature and magnetic field ranges, offering a universal strategy for probing the electronic structures of diverse two-dimensional systems and facilitating future explorations of material properties.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.21867"></a>
### [Zero-level $CCZ$ Distillation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21867v1)

**Authors:** Tomohiro Itogawa, Yutaka Hirano, Yutaro Akahoshi, Keisuke Fujii  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21867v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21867_figures/2605.21867_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Geometric representation of the J8, 3, 2K code. Physical qubits are located at the vertices of the cube. The yellow cells and faces represent the X-type and Z- type stabilizer generators, respectively. The red, blue, and green faces and edges correspond to the logical op- erators.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.21867_figures/2605.21867_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Overview of the proposed zero-level CCZ state distillation protocol. (a) Using a non-fault-tolerant encoding circuit and transversal CNOT gates between ancilla blocks, a |CCZ⟩state is prepared in the J8, 3, 2K code. Addition- ally, intermediate surface-code states are prepared for adaptively initialized teleportation (AIT). (b) AIT is performed by executing lattice surgery between the two-body logical operators of the J8, 3, 2K code and the three-body logical operators of the intermediate state. (c) While performing superdense syndrome extraction on the J8, 3, 2K code, the surface codes are completed through stabilizer measurements, thereby finalizing AIT.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.21867_figures/2605.21867_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Overall structure of zero-level CCZ state dis- tillation. A non-fault-tolerant encoding/decoding circuit is used to prepare the state |+ + +⟩, and a verification procedure is then applied to obtain a fault-tolerant en- coding. Transversal T/T † gates are subsequently applied to implement the CCZ operation, after which the three logical qubits are teleported to three surface-code logical qubits.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.21867_figures/2605.21867_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: (a) Non-fault-tolerant |+ + +⟩encoding circuit, consisting of a depth-3 minimal sequence of CNOT gates. (b) Required qubit connectivity for the encoding circuit, compatible with strictly nearest-neighbor interactions on a two-dimensional square lattice.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.21867_figures/2605.21867_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Fault-tolerant |+ + +⟩encoding circuit. The state is first encoded using the non-fault-tolerant circuit, followed by a transversal CNOT applied to ancilla qubits. The circuit is then decoded, and the four data qubits that were initially prepared in the |+⟩state are measured in the X-basis for verification. A second round of encoding and transversal CNOT is performed, and the output is accepted only when all measurement outcomes are +1.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a highly efficient 'zero-level' protocol for distilling CCZ magic states, significantly reducing the qubit and time overhead compared to standard methods. By leveraging a specific low-distance code and a new adaptive teleportation technique, it provides a practical path for implementing non-Clifford gates in early-stage fault-tolerant quantum computers.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The massive spatial and temporal overhead required for conventional magic state distillation (MSD) of non-Clifford gates like CCZ poses a bottleneck for scalable fault-tolerant quantum computation.

**Main result.** The authors propose a 'zero-level' distillation protocol that reduces space-time overhead by a factor of 5-10 compared to previous methods, achieving a logical error rate scaling as pL approx 300 * p^2.

**Method.** The protocol uses a J(8,3,2K) code to fault-tolerantly encode a CCZ state and introduces Adaptively Initialized Teleportation (AIT) via lattice surgery to transfer the state to surface-code logical qubits.

**Model / system.** A two-dimensional square lattice with nearest-neighbor interactions, specifically targeting architectures like superconducting qubits, using the J(8,3,2K) CSS code and surface codes.

**Key observables.** Logical error rate (pL), success probability, and space-time overhead.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Physical error rate p (analyzed at 10^-3 and 10^-4), circuit depth of 24, and a requirement of 22 physical qubits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a Clifford approximation for T/T-dagger gates is justified by the fact that they do not convert X/Y errors into Z errors in this specific code structure; the error model assumes depolarizing noise and single-qubit flip noise.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the J(8,3,2K) code geometry; Fig 2-3 illustrate the distillation protocol and AIT pipeline; Fig 4-5 detail the encoding circuit and connectivity; Fig 6-7 show the teleportation process; Fig 12-13 compare resource efficiency and error rates against existing methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of CCZ distillation overhead, presents the zero-level protocol and AIT method, details the encoding and superdense syndrome extraction, provides numerical validation via Stim/Qulacs, and concludes with a resource comparison against conventional T-gate-based approaches.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Magic state distillation is a key component of fault-tolerant quantum computation, as it enables the implementation of non-Clifford gates such as the $T$ gate and the $CCZ$ gate via gate teleportation. However, conventional distillation protocols require a large number of logical qubits and introduce substantial spatial and temporal overhead, posing a significant bottleneck for scalable fault-tolerant quantum computation. In this work, we propose a zero-level distillation protocol that efficiently generates a high-fidelity logical $CCZ$ magic state using only physical qubits on a two-dimensional square lattice with nearest-neighbor interactions. Our method leverages the transversal $T/T^\dagger$ operation of the $[[ 8,3,2 ]]$ code to fault-tolerantly encode the state $\overline{CCZ}|+++\rangle$, which is subsequently teleported to three surface-code logical qubits via lattice surgery. To enable teleportation between codes with different distances, we introduce adaptively initialized teleportation (AIT), a tailored initialization procedure for the surface code. Numerical simulations demonstrate that the logical error rate scales as $p_L \simeq 300 \times p^2$ with respect to the physical error rate $p$. For example, the proposed method improves the logical error rate by approximately one and two orders of magnitude at $p = 10^{-3}$ and $p = 10^{-4}$, respectively, compared to conventional seven-$T$-gate approaches. The distillation circuit requires only 22 physical qubits, 3 logical qubits, and a circuit depth of 24, reducing the space-time overhead by a factor of approximately 5-10 compared to previous methods. This result highlights the practicality of $CCZ$-state distillation in early fault-tolerant quantum computation and offers a new direction toward resource-efficient physical-level magic state distillation beyond conventional $T$-state generation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

</details>


## Secondary cond-mat archives

### Relevant in secondary archives (13)

<a id="paper-2605.22701"></a>
### [Monitored quantum transport through a disordered one-dimensional conductor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22701v1)

**Authors:** J. Sánchez Fernán, J. Tworzydło, C. W. J. Beenakker  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22701v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `measurement-induced transitions` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22701_figures/2605.22701_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Representation on a 1+1 dimensional space-time lat- tice of transport through a single-mode disordered conductor. Scattering centra (extending over a length L) are indicated by filled circles, while open circles represent the ideal leads (no backscattering). Electrons enter the conductor from one end and are either reflected back or transmitted to the other end.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22701_figures/2605.22701_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Scattering phase shift ϕ dependence of the transmis- sion probability T through N = 3 identical tunnel barriers in series, each with tunnel probability Γ = 1 −ρ = 1/2. The effect of decoherence with strength w = √1 −γ is computed from Eq. (6.6).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22701_figures/2605.22701_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Illustration of reciprocity breaking by monitoring [26]. Shown is a three-barrier structure (blue) without left- right symmetry (a thin barrier at the left, two thick barriers at the right). Time-resolved monitoring (on a time scale τc) measures the outcome of the scattering by the thin barrier, broadening the wave packet of the transmitted electron (from eV to ℏ/τc). The broadened wave packet overlaps with the bound state E0 in between the thick barriers. This increases the transmission probability if monitoring happens before the electron reaches the thick barriers (panel a), but not if it happens after (panel b).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22701_figures/2605.22701_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Reciprocal of the typical transmission probability Ttyp = eE[ln T ] as a function of conductor length L. The data points have been computed from Eq. (5.17) for tunnel prob- ability Γ = 0.8 and measurement strength w = 0.03. The expectation value E[ln T ] is an average over 104 random dis- order realizations. The dashed line is a linear fit to the large-L data, to show the crossover to the diffusive regime.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22701_figures/2605.22701_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Coherence length ℓϕ as a function of 1/w2 for three values of the tunnel probability. The solid lines are given by Eq. (7.6), which in dimensionless units reads ℓϕ = ξ ln(1/ξw2). The localization length ξ is not fitted to this data, it is obtained independently from the exponential de- cay of Ttyp ∝e−L/ξ for w = 0.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that monitoring quantum transport in a 1D disordered wire suppresses Anderson localization, leading to a diffusive regime. It provides a quantitative scaling law for the induced coherence length and shows that the electron transport statistics remain binomial.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems as it provides a rigorous master equation treatment of how continuous or periodic monitoring affects many-body transport and destroys quantum interference effects.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how environmental monitoring (time-resolved projective measurements) induces a transition from Anderson localization to classical diffusion in a one-dimensional disordered conductor.

**Main result.** Monitoring suppresses phase coherence, causing a crossover from exponential localization to Ohmic 1/L decay at a coherence length that scales logarithmically with the measurement interval.

**Method.** The authors develop a quantum master equation approach using Kraus operators and a 1+1 dimensional space-time lattice to track the many-particle density matrix evolution.

**Model / system.** A single-mode, one-dimensional electronic conductor with disorder, modeled as a series of scattering segments where decoherence is introduced via periodic projective measurements.

**Key observables.** Transmission probability (T), conductance (G), shot noise power, and full counting statistics (FCS) of transferred charge.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Conductor length (L), localization length (xi), phase coherence length (l_phi), measurement strength (w), and mean time between measurements (tau_phi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes a low-voltage, linear response regime and a discrete-time evolution model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a space-time lattice of the conductor; Figure 4 illustrates the crossover from exponential to linear decay in transmission; Figure 5 shows the logarithmic growth of coherence length with measurement strength; Figure 6/7 compare the master equation to the voltage probe model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the master equation framework, applies it to study the transition from localization to diffusion, analyzes the scaling of the coherence length, compares the results to the Büttiker voltage probe model, and discusses reciprocity breaking.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We formulate a quantum master equation for the many-particle density matrix of electrons propagating through a single-mode conductor, combining elastic scattering by disorder with time-resolved projective measurements that monitor the outcome of scattering events. The full counting statistics of transmitted electrons has a binomial distribution function, whose mean ${\cal T}$ and variance ${\cal T}(1-{\cal T})$ determine the conductance and shot noise power, respectively. Monitoring suppresses the phase coherence responsible for one-dimensional localization: The decay with conductor length $L$ of the typical transmission probability crosses over at $L\simeq \ell_φ$ from the exponential $e^{-L/ξ}$ (with localization length $ξ$) to the Ohmic $1/L$ decay. Numerical solution of the master equation gives, for weak monitoring, a logarithmic dependence $\ell_φ\simeq ξ\ln(v_{\rm F}τ_φ/ξ)$ of the coherence length $\ell_φ$ on the mean time $τ_φ$ between measurements.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22135"></a>
### [Gyromagnetic Quantum Friction in Rayleigh Vorticity Baths](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22135v1)

**Authors:** Mamoru Matsuo, Ryotaro Sano, Ai Yamakage, Hiroshi Funaki, Tatsuhiko N. Ikeda  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22135v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **4/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22135_figures/2605.22135_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. A near-surface spin-1/2 is located at a depth d below a traction-free surface (z = 0) of an elastic half-space. The spin couples linearly to the local lattice vorticity generated by Rayleigh surface acoustic waves with in-plane wave vector q. A static magnetic field B0 defines the quantization axis, tilted by an angle θB from the surface normal ˆz.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper identifies a new relaxation mechanism for spins near a surface called gyromagnetic quantum friction, driven by the vorticity of Rayleigh waves. Unlike thermal Raman relaxation, this process remains active at zero temperature due to zero-point fluctuations. This effect provides a way to use shallow spin sensors to detect acoustic quantum friction in solids.

**Why it may be interesting.** It identifies a fundamental, temperature-independent decoherence mechanism (quantum friction) driven by vacuum fluctuations of a mechanical bath, which is highly relevant to the design of surface-based spin qubits and quantum sensors.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Identifying an intrinsic, zero-temperature relaxation channel for near-surface spins caused by the vorticity of Rayleigh surface acoustic waves.

**Main result.** The discovery of 'gyromagnetic quantum friction,' a non-zero relaxation rate that persists even at zero temperature due to zero-point vorticity fluctuations, which can be used to detect surface acoustic waves.

**Method.** The authors use the Born-Markov approximation and Fermi's Golden Rule to calculate transition rates, alongside a spectral density analysis of quantized elastic modes.

**Model / system.** A single spin-1/2 (such as an NV center or quantum dot) located at a depth d below a traction-free, isotropic elastic surface, coupled to a bath of Rayleigh-wave vorticity and bulk phonons via a Barnett-type interaction.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal relaxation rate (T1 inverse), transverse coherence time (T2), and frequency renormalization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin depth (d), Larmor frequency (omega_0), Rayleigh-evanescent length (l_R), and the Raman crossover temperature (T_x).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The medium is an infinite, isotropic, elastic half-space; the interaction is treated in the weak-coupling regime; and the Rayleigh surface contribution is assumed to dominate the bulk contribution for shallow spins.

**Figures summary.** A visibility map summarizing dominant relaxation regimes based on spin depth and temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the spin-vorticity interaction, develops the quantization of the Rayleigh-wave bath, derives the spectral density and relaxation rates, compares the mechanism to Raman and g-modulation relaxation, and provides a benchmark analysis using diamond parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We identify an intrinsic zero-temperature relaxation channel for near-surface spins gyromagnetically coupled to Rayleigh-wave vorticity. This surface-mode contribution requires no thermal phonons, unlike Raman relaxation, and is fixed by Rayleigh vorticity rather than material-specific $g$-factor modulation. The Rayleigh-vorticity bath is super-Ohmic and evanescent with depth, producing field and depth scalings of spin relaxation. These scalings establish shallow spin sensors and hybrid surface-acoustic-wave spin interfaces as detectors of Rayleigh-wave acoustic quantum friction in solids.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22753"></a>
### [Signatures of quantum chaos in phonon-polariton billiards](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22753v1)

**Authors:** Yinan Dong, Felix Liu, Ekrem Demirboga, Andrey Grankin, Dihao Sun, Yuchen Lin, Lukas Wehmeier, Matthew Fu, Cory R. Dean, Song Liu, James H. Edgar, Michael M. Folger, Victor M. Galitski, Dmitri N. Basov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22753v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `scars & prethermalization` **4/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22753_figures/2605.22753_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22753_figures/2605.22753_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22753_figures/2605.22753_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22753_figures/2605.22753_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22753_figures/2605.22753_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper experimentally demonstrates quantum chaos in phonon-polariton billiards made of hBN. By varying the cavity geometry, the authors observe signatures like quantum scarring and the transition from Poisson to Wigner-Dyson statistics. This establishes these van der Waals materials as a versatile platform for studying complex wave dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides an experimental platform for studying the transition between integrable and chaotic dynamics in a dissipative, hybrid light-matter system, relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the signatures of quantum chaos, such as quantum scarring and random-wave statistics, in hybrid light-matter phonon-polariton systems.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates the transition from Poisson to Wigner-Dyson level statistics and observes quantum scarring and Berry's random-wave conjecture in hBN polariton billiards.

**Method.** The researchers used s-SNOM for near-field imaging and developed a numerical framework based on the Helmholtz equation with generalized boundary conditions and Green's functions.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of hyperbolic phonon-polaritons in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) cavities with varying geometries, including square (integrable) and Sinai (chaotic) billiards.

**Key observables.** Local density of states (LDOS), polariton wave patterns (scars), energy level spacing distributions, and Fourier transforms of measured signals (momentum distribution).

**Important parameters / regimes.** hBN thickness, excitation energy, cavity geometry (integrable vs. chaotic), quality factor (Q), and probe amplitude.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Maxwell equations are approximated by a scalar Helmholtz equation, and the boundary condition is approximated by expanding around normal incidence to maintain locality.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows hBN polariton nano-imaging and dispersion; Figure 2 displays s-SNOM data, simulations of scarring, and level spacing statistics; Figure 3 compares edge modes to chaotic interiors; Figure 4 demonstrates Berry's conjecture via FFT.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with experimental fabrication and imaging, moves to the development of a numerical Green's function framework for scattering, then presents the identification of quantum scars, energy level statistics, and the validation of Berry's conjecture.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We use scanning near-field optical microscopy to image hyperbolic phonon polaritons in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) billiards with integrable and chaotic geometries. In Sinai billiards, we observe irregular mode patterns consistent with quantum scarring, together with an unexpected sensitivity to weak probe perturbations. These random-wave features coexist with non-chaotic one-dimensional boundary modes arising from nontrivial polariton reflection at the billiard edge. As the billiard boundary becomes increasingly complex, the Fourier transforms of the measured signals evolve toward ring-like structures consistent with Berry's random-wave conjecture. We develop a numerical framework based on the Helmholtz equation with generalized boundary conditions that encode angle-dependent reflection phase shifts. The calculated level statistics exhibit a crossover from Poisson-like behavior in integrable billiards to Wigner-Dyson-like behavior in chaotic geometries, with small deviations from the canonical form arising from nonlinear boundary conditions that require self-consistent bulk-boundary analysis. Theoretical analysis based on dissipative Green's functions qualitatively reproduces the near-field data. These results establish mesoscopic van der Waals billiards as a rich platform for studying generalized chaotic dynamics of hybrid light-matter polaritons.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Klein tunneling of the laser coherence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22033v1)

(c,d): Near and far field of the laser array with the linear dispersion, confirming lasing around the K and K′ points and a chiral phase structure.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22033_figures/2605.22033_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3(a,b) shows the real (a) and imaginary parts (b) of ω(k) calculated by diagonalizing Eq. (6). The dispersion in Fig. 3(a) manifests characteristic Dirac cones, and the loss in Fig. 3(b) is minimized at the K points. Conversely, τNN = 0.1 and τNNN = 0.03i yield lasing at the Γ point of the Brillouin zone with parabolic (trivial) dispersion. Figure 3(c) shows the near field profile and Fig. 3(d) illustrates the far field, cal- culated as a Fourier transform of (c) in the form of E(k) = P</sub>

**Summary.** The study shows that laser arrays with linear (Dirac) dispersion can maintain frequency synchronization across much larger frequency barriers than those with standard parabolic dispersion. This enhanced robustness is attributed to the 'Klein tunneling' of laser coherence, where modes delocalize through the barrier.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a direct analogy between a fundamental relativistic particle phenomenon (Klein tunneling) and the macroscopic synchronization of non-equilibrium, non-Hermitian classical oscillators, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the robustness of frequency synchronization between two laser arrays separated by a spectrally detuned barrier, specifically exploring how different dispersion laws (linear/Dirac vs. parabolic) affect the synchronization threshold.

**Main result.** For linear (Dirac) dispersion, synchronization persists for barrier heights approximately an order of magnitude higher than for parabolic or flat dispersion, an effect interpreted as the Klein tunneling of laser coherence.

**Method.** Numerical solution of nonlinear laser rate equations (Lugiato-Lefever-type) combined with linear analysis via diagonalization of a non-Hermitian effective Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** 1D and 2D (hexagonal/honeycomb) arrays of coupled lasers described by a non-Hermitian effective Hamiltonian including dispersive and dissipative coupling components.

**Key observables.** Probability of desynchronization (P_desync), nonlinear transmission of coherence (T), mode localization/delocalization, and complex eigenfrequencies (dispersion and loss).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Barrier height (delta_B), barrier width (N_B), pump rate (p/gamma), and dispersion type (linear/Dirac, parabolic, or flat).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nonlinear lasing solutions can be approximated by linear combinations of the lowest-loss eigenmodes of the effective Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the 1D setup, coupling scheme, and band structure/transmission; Fig 2 compares desynchronization probability across different dispersion regimes; Supplemental figures show mode loss, localization, and the effect of pump strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the synchronization problem in laser arrays, presents the 1D model and the discovery of enhanced synchronization via Klein tunneling, extends the findings to 2D honeycomb lattices, and provides a detailed analysis of mode localization and symmetry effects in the supplement.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study theoretically the lasing synchronization of the two arrays of lasers with the complex mode dispersion, separated by a spectrally detuned barrier. We demonstrate that for lasing at the Dirac point, the synchronization persists for an order of magnitude higher barriers than in the arrays with a usual parabolic dispersion or a purely dissipative coupling. We interpret this effect as the Klein tunneling of the laser coherence through the barrier. Our numerical findings are supported by an analysis of the delocalization of the linearized eigenmodes of the arrays, which enhances the synchronization.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21983"></a>
### [Nonlinear Photonic Tripartite Phase](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21983v1)

**Authors:** Xiangrui Hou, Fangyu Wang, Zhaoxin Wu, Shuming Zhang, Shan-Zhong Li, Lei Ying, Haiqing Lin, Baile Zhang, Zhi Li, Shi-Liang Zhu, Zhaoju Yang  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21983v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 4. State-selective nonlinear transport and re-entrant localization. Numerical (a-d) and experimental (e-h) spatial intensity distributions for the low-energy localized state (a, e), critical state (b, f), extended state (c, g), and high-energy localized state (d, h). **Summary.** This paper experimentally demonstrates a tripartite phase in a nonlinear quasiperiodic photonic lattice where localized, critical, and extended states coexist. It reveals that nonlinearity acts selectively, capable of driving low-energy localized states into a critical window before eventually inducing self-trapping. This discovery offers a mechanism for using interactions to control state access in quasiperiodic landscapes.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rare experimental demonstration of a tripartite phase and shows how nonlinear interactions can be used as a dynamical tool to navigate between different localization regimes, which is highly relevant to many-body dynamics and controlled transport in disordered systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the lack of experimental realization of a tripartite phase (coexisting localized, critical, and extended states) in quasiperiodic systems and investigates whether nonlinearity can provide controlled access to the critical window.

**Main result.** The researchers experimentally realize a tripartite phase in a nonlinear photonic lattice and discover a state-selective mechanism where weak nonlinearity 'thaws' low-energy localized states into the critical window, while stronger nonlinearity leads to self-trapping.

**Method.** The study uses femtosecond-laser-written waveguide arrays to implement a quasiperiodic diamond lattice, employing site-selective excitation and tracking wavepacket dynamics and fractal dimension evolution.

**Model / system.** A nonlinear quasiperiodic diamond photonic lattice implemented via waveguide arrays, governed by the discrete nonlinear Schrodinger equation with a quasiperiodic potential applied to one sublattice.

**Key observables.** Fractal Dimension (FD), Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR), wavepacket spatial intensity evolution, and energy-dependent transport regimes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength (lambda), nonlinearity strength (gP/J), and energy levels (low-energy vs. high-energy localized states).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The paraxial approximation is used to map spatial propagation in waveguides to temporal evolution in quantum systems.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the waveguide array schematic and excitation sites; Fig 2 visualizes spatial intensity evolution; Fig 3 displays the fractal dimension as a function of nonlinearity and its dynamical evolution; Fig 4 shows the monotonic drive of certain states into the soliton regime.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the experimental gap, describes the photonic lattice platform and Hamiltonian, presents the realization of the tripartite phase in the linear regime, explores the state-selective response to Kerr nonlinearity, and concludes with the underlying mechanism of spectral flow.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Anderson localization is usually understood as a transition between extended and localized phases, with criticality confined to a single mobility edge. Recent advances predict that quasiperiodic systems can instead host a finite critical window bounded by mobility edges, in which localized, critical and extended states coexist. Yet both the experimental realization of this regime and whether interactions can provide controlled access to it remain unknown. Here, we realize such a tripartite phase in a nonlinear quasiperiodic photonic lattice and show that Kerr nonlinearity, acting as an effective interaction, enables state-selective access to the critical window. By tracking wavepacket dynamics, we distinguish localized, critical and extended transport regimes and uncover a state-selective response: rather than simply reinforcing localization through self-trapping, weak nonlinearity drives low-energy localized states into the critical window, whereas stronger nonlinearity restores localization. By contrast, critical, extended and high-energy localized states evolve monotonically towards self-trapped behaviour. Our results reveal a state-selective mechanism by which interactions provide controlled access to a pre-existing critical window in quasiperiodic systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22768"></a>
### [Generation of Quantum Turbulence by Neutrino Cooling in Neutron Stars](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22768v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the rapid neutrino cooling in young neutron stars acts as a thermal quench that triggers the Kibble-Zurek mechanism. This process generates a high density of quantized vortices, resulting in a state of quantum turbulence within the star's superfluid core. These findings have significant implications for understanding pulsar rotation and thermal evolution.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper explores non-equilibrium phase transitions and the generation of topological defects via rapid quenches, which is highly relevant to the study of dynamics in many-body systems and the formation of defects in condensed matter.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the rapid, non-equilibrium neutrino cooling in young neutron stars triggers the generation of quantum turbulence through the formation of topological defects.

**Main result.** The application of the Kibble-Zurek mechanism (KZM) predicts that rapid cooling leads to a very high density of quantized vortices and loops, creating a random network of quantum turbulence in the neutron star interior.

**Method.** The author uses the Kibble-Zurek mechanism (KZM) and scaling relations derived from the Cooper pair fluctuation propagator to calculate vortex densities based on Urca and modified Urca cooling rates.

**Model / system.** The system is the interior crust and liquid core of a young neutron star, modeled as a quantum liquid mixture of neutron and proton superfluids and a relativistic electron liquid undergoing a second-order phase transition.

**Key observables.** Areal density of phase vortices (nv), inter-vortex spacing (dv), and the quench rate (1/tau_Q).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Neutrino cooling timescales (tau_Q), transition temperatures (Tc), pairing gaps (Delta), and neutron density (kfn).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The cooling is modeled as a temperature quench; the fraction of phase windings (f) is non-universal; the system is treated within the weak-coupling limit for pairing.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows neutron superfluid pairing calculations; Figure 2 shows a diagrammatic representation of the Cooper pair propagator; Figure 3 and 4 show the areal density of vortices as a function of neutron density for different cooling processes and nuclear potentials.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the neutron star interior system, applies the Kibble-Zurek mechanism to the non-equilibrium phase transition, derives scaling relations from the Cooper pair propagator, calculates vortex densities for specific cooling mechanisms (Urca/modified Urca), and compares results to standard pulsar rotation models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The interior crust and much of the liquid core of neutron stars is believed to be a quantum liquid mixture of neutron and proton superfluids and a relativistic electron liquid. Quantized vortices in the neutron superfluid and quantized flux lines in the proton superconductor are topological defects of these hadronic condensates. I consider the formation of the superfluid state in young neutron stars under non-equilibrium conditions imposed by the neutrino cooling rate. The nonequilibrium phase transition implies that the onset of superfluidity is accompanied by the generation of quantized vortices based on the mechanism envisioned by Kibble in the context cosmic string formation in an evolutionary models of an expanding universe, and further developed by Zurek for nonequilibrium phase transitions in quantum liquids such as \Hefour. I discuss the Kibble-Zurek mechanism (KZM) and scaling relations for topological defect formation starting from the Cooper pair fluctuation propagator for temperatures approaching $T_c$. I then calculate the predicted vortex densities based on Urca and modified Urca cooling mechanisms in the cores of neutron stars for several models of the superfluid gap and transition temperature of the interior neutron superfluid. In all cases studied the KZM leads to a large density of topological defects in the condensate phase, which in 3D form a random network of vortex lines and loops, i.e. the generation of quantum turbulence.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that in a SAW-driven resonator, the excitation of counterpropagating spin waves leads to a unique positive nonlinear frequency shift and bistable 'foldover' behavior. These findings provide a new mechanism for controlling magnon-phonon interactions and suggest potential for wave-based information processing and power-limiting devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the origin of nonlinear frequency shifts and the emergence of bistability in strongly coupled magnon-phonon hybrids (magnon-polarons).

**Main result.** The researchers discovered that the nonlinear frequency shift is positive and dominated by a cross-shift term arising from the simultaneous excitation of counterpropagating spin waves (+k and -k) by the SAW resonator.

**Method.** The study combines microwave reflection measurements (VNA) with polarization-resolved micro-focused Brillouin light scattering (uBLS) and utilizes a vector Hamiltonian formalism for theoretical modeling.

**Model / system.** A magnetoacoustic resonator consisting of a YIG/ZnO heterostructure in a backward-volume magnetic configuration, where surface acoustic waves (SAWs) are strongly coupled to finite-wave-vector spin waves (SWs).

**Key observables.** Electrical reflection (S11), frequency-resolved magnon and phonon spectra, and power-dependent redistribution of magnon populations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strong-coupling regime (g/2pi = 5.05 MHz), acoustic wavelength (2 um), and magnetic field detuning relative to the anticrossing point.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nonlinear dynamics are modeled using the vector Hamiltonian formalism for nonlinear spin-wave dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the device geometry and measurement setup; Fig 2 details the linear coupling and experimental parameters; Fig 3 presents nonlinear coefficients and calculated dispersion relations; Fig 4 and 5 illustrate the power-dependent nonlinear shifts, hysteretic power sweeps, and the resulting bistability/foldover behavior.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the experimental setup and linear regime characterization, moves to the observation of power-dependent frequency shifts and nonlinear scattering, provides a theoretical explanation via the vector Hamiltonian formalism, and concludes with the demonstration of bistability and potential applications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the nonlinear dynamics of strongly coupled surface acoustic waves (SAWs) and spin waves (SWs) in a magnetoacoustic resonator based on a YIG/ZnO heterostructure by combining microwave reflection measurements with microfocused Brillouin light scattering spectroscopy. In the linear regime, the electrical response reveals clear hybridization between standing SAW cavity modes and finite-wave-vector SWs, resulting in pronounced avoided crossings. At elevated drive powers, the hybrid system exhibits a strongly field-dependent nonlinear response characterized by a positive frequency shift of the driven SW mode. Using the vector Hamiltonian formalism for nonlinear spin-wave dynamics, we show that this shift is dominated by a cross-shift term. In our resonator geometry, this contribution becomes significant because the standing SAW cavity mode simultaneously excites counterpropagating SWs with wave vectors $+k$ and $-k$. For suitable field detuning, the nonlinear shift drives the SW mode into resonance with the SAW excitation, leading to a strong enhancement of the magnon population, broadband nonlinear scattering, and bistable foldover behavior. Beyond the foldover threshold, both the magnon and phonon responses stabilize. These results establish SAW-driven $k \neq 0$ magnon-phonon hybrids as a promising platform for nonlinear magnetoacoustics and wave-based information processing.

</details>

### [Anomalous acoustoelectric signatures of chiral superconductivity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21936v1)

**Authors:** A. N. Osipov, V. N. Ivanova, V. M. Kovalev, I. G. Savenko  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21936v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.21936_figures/2605.21936_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

<sub>Figure 3 shows a monotonic decay of both the lon- gitudinal and Hall current densities with increasing SAW frequency. To understand this dependence, let us collect the corresponding terms in the normal-state limit, which yields j ∼[1 + (Dpk2/w)]−2. The term Dpk2/w = (τDω)−1 represents a ratio between the acoustic wave period and the characteristic diffusion time τD = (Dpk2)−1. Since k ∝ω, the inverse diffusion time τ −1 D scales quadratically with frequency. This re- sult can be interpreted as follows: at higher acoustic fre- quencies, the diffusion response time becomes shorter. Rather than being dragged by the wave, the quasipar- ticles rapidly diffuse across the shortened wavelength,...</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.21936_figures/2605.21936_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using Surface Acoustic Waves to detect chiral p-wave superconductivity in 2D materials. It demonstrates that the asymmetric scattering of quasiparticles generates a measurable transverse DC current. This provides a high-sensitivity electrical alternative to traditional optical methods for identifying unconventional pairing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of identifying chiral p-wave pairing symmetry in 2D superconductors using traditional optical probes due to low signal-to-noise and substrate interference.

**Main result.** The authors propose that the anomalous acoustoelectric effect, specifically a transverse DC current caused by skew scattering, serves as a robust electrical signature of chiral order parameters.

**Method.** A kinetic equation approach using the Boltzmann equation and T-matrix approach to analyze subgap dynamics and asymmetric scattering probabilities.

**Model / system.** A 2D chiral p-wave superconductor (such as MoS2 or twisted bilayer graphene) placed on a piezoelectric substrate where Surface Acoustic Waves (SAWs) induce a dragging potential.

**Key observables.** Transverse (Hall) current density, longitudinal current density, and the induced superconducting condensate phase difference across sample boundaries.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Superconducting gap (Delta), transition temperature (Tc), SAW frequency (omega), momentum relaxation time (tau_p), and the diffusive limit (kl << 1).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is in the diffusive limit, the SAW frequency is much smaller than the superconducting gap, and the acoustic wave velocity is much less than the Fermi velocity.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows an experimental setup of a 2D superconductor on a piezoelectric substrate with IDTs; Figure 2 shows the longitudinal and Hall current densities as functions of temperature and gap magnitude.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of detecting chiral pairing, proposes the acoustoelectric effect, develops the kinetic theory framework including skew scattering, derives the current density components, and discusses the experimental observability of the results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The identification of unconventional pairing in two-dimensional materials is a central challenge in modern condensed matter physics. While chiral p-wave superconductivity offers a promising platform for topological quantum computing, its detection remains elusive due to the inherent limitations of optical probes in the two-dimensional limit. We propose the anomalous acoustoelectric effect as a robust, alternative to optical signature of p-wave paring symmetry. We demonstrate that an acoustic wave induces a transverse dc current resulting in a measurable condensate phase difference on sample boundaries originating from the anisotropic scattering of quasiparticles in the absence of an external magnetic field. Crucially, the quasiparticle-mediated acoustoelectric response dominates near the critical temperature and, unlike the superconducting condensate, is not suppressed by electron-hole asymmetry factor. These results establish the anomalous acoustoelectric effect as a high-sensitivity electrical probe of the chiral order parameter, providing a tool for experimental detecting of unconventional pairing in superconductors.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that injecting a charge current into a multichannel superconductor breaks time-reversal and chiral symmetries, which suppresses higher-order topological phases. It identifies that while the two-mode phase is fragile under current, it can be stabilized if the superconducting channels remain decoupled.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how a finite charge current induces symmetry breaking that reduces the topological protection and suppresses higher-multiplicity Majorana modes in multichannel superconductors.

**Main result.** The study shows that a finite current drives the system from the BDI class to the D class, making the two-mode topological phase fragile, unless the channels are decoupled (interchain pairing is zero).

**Method.** The authors use a real-space Hamiltonian approach, combining bulk topological invariants (Pfaffian) with real-space entanglement diagnostics like edge-edge quantum conditional mutual information (Iee).

**Model / system.** A Kitaev ladder model consisting of two coupled spinless p-wave superconducting chains with longitudinal and interchain hopping and pairing.

**Key observables.** Topological invariants (Z and Z2), Bogoli-de Gennes (BdG) energy spectra, Majorana Bound State (MBS) probability densities, and edge-edge quantum conditional mutual information (Iee).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chemical potential (mu), longitudinal hopping (t), interchain hopping (t1), longitudinal pairing (Delta), interchain pairing (Delta1), and current-induced momentum (q).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The current is modeled via an effective Hamiltonian using a Peierls-like substitution (q-dependent phase factor).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows phase diagrams comparing equilibrium and current-carrying regimes; Figure 2 shows energy spectra and mode densities illustrating the lifting of the two-mode configuration; Figure 3 shows the persistence of the two-mode phase when interchain pairing is zero; Figure 4 shows energy spectra as a function of chemical potential and interchain hopping.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of current-induced symmetry breaking, defines the Kitaev ladder model and current-carrying Hamiltonian, presents bulk and real-space topological analysis, discusses the transition from BDI to D class, and concludes with the implications for superconducting nanostructures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the robustness of topological phases in a Kitaev ladder composed of two coupled superconducting chains under the perturbing influence of a finite charge current. By introducing an effective Hamiltonian depending on the quasiparticle momentum induced by the current, we show that the two-mode topological phase, present in the isolated ladder, is fragile against a finite current flux. To characterize this behavior, we combine bulk topological invariants with real-space diagnostics, including the edge-edge quantum conditional mutual information Iee, which provides an entanglement-based signature of topological order. Our results provide an effective framework to describe how current injection and measurement processes can affect topological protection in superconducting nanostructures.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22227_figures/2605.22227_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) Magnitude of the quantum-metric current jqm along ˆx for the tilted Dirac model [Eq. (19)] as a function of the applied field for various chemical potentials µ, with an inhomogeneous electric field applied along ˆx with a finite longitudinal gradient Exx. The current approaches a finite saturation in the high-field limit, implying a vanishing differential conductance and providing the main transport signature discussed in the text. Here L denotes the nearest-neighbor lattice spacing set by the primitive lattice vectors ai, and l is the sample length along the current direction. (b) Longitudinal conventional Bloch current versus field, showing negative differential conductivity...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22227_figures/2605.22227_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Panel (a) shows the longitudinal and transverse quantum-metric currents, j|| qme and j⊥ qme, as functions of the chemical potential (µ) for ˜E = (E1 + E11x) ˆx. Panel (b) shows the longitudinal and transverse Bloch currents, j|| Bloch and j⊥ Bloch, as functions of the chemical potential for the same field. The figure highlights that the quantum-metric response is strongest near charge neutrality and that tilt enhances the longitudinal current. The numerical parameters are η = 1, ℏvF = 1 eV, and vt = 0.2vF .</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that an inhomogeneous electric field can drive Bloch-like oscillations through the quantum metric, even in the absence of Berry curvature. It shows that this quantum-metric-driven current behaves differently from conventional Bloch oscillations, specifically approaching a finite saturation in high fields. This provides a new mechanism for observing band-geometric effects in synthetic lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether a band-geometric analog of Bloch oscillations can exist in systems where the Berry curvature vanishes by utilizing the quantum metric.

**Main result.** A weakly inhomogeneous electric field introduces a distinct quantum-metric term to semiclassical dynamics, generating oscillatory real-space motion even when Berry curvature is zero.

**Method.** Extended semiclassical equations of motion for wavepacket dynamics and the semiclassical Boltzmann equation using a relaxation-time approximation.

**Model / system.** A tilted Dirac model used as an illustrative system, considering electrons in a periodic potential subject to a weakly inhomogeneous electric field.

**Key observables.** Real-space position (drift and oscillatory components), quantum-metric-induced current (longitudinal and transverse), and differential conductance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Berry curvature, quantum metric, electric field gradient, static electric field, and chemical potential.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The electric field varies slowly over the wavepacket size (weak-gradient approximation) and the semiclassical wavepacket approximation is valid.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows schematics of Berry-curvature-driven vs. quantum-metric-induced Bloch oscillations; Figure 2 shows quantum-metric components in a tilted Dirac model; Figure 3 compares the scaling of quantum-metric current (saturation) vs. conventional Bloch current (negative differential conductance).

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the problem of Berry-curvature-independent oscillations, derives modified semiclassical equations of motion including field gradients, applies these to a tilted Dirac model, calculates transport signatures (current and conductance), and discusses experimental platforms like moire superlattices.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Geometric analogs of Bloch oscillations studied so far have relied on Berry curvature. We show that a weakly inhomogeneous electric field adds a distinct quantum-metric term to semiclassical wavepacket dynamics, generating an oscillatory real-space contribution even when the Berry curvature vanishes. The associated transport response comprises an intrinsic and a scattering-time-dependent part. In the regime studied, the latter can dominate and approach finite saturation at high field when the relative field inhomogeneity is held fixed. A tilted Dirac model illustrates the mechanism. Realistic platforms will likely require synthetically engineered superlattices, with a finite quantum metric and an adequate band gap.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Signatures of the Quantum Geometric Dipole of Interlayer Excitons in Counterflow Conductivity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22810v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the counterflow conductivity of interlayer excitons can serve as a tunable probe for their internal quantum geometric dipole. By manipulating periodic potentials and electric fields, one can observe how the quantum geometry of many-body excitations influences macroscopic transport.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a theoretical bridge between the abstract quantum geometry of many-body wavefunctions and measurable transport coefficients, which is highly relevant for understanding topological transport in correlated bilayer systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how the internal quantum geometric dipole (QGD) of interlayer excitons manifests in transport properties, specifically looking for experimental signatures in counterflow conductivity.

**Main result.** The authors show that the off-diagonal counterflow conductivity $\sigma_{xy}^{(CF)}$ is proportional to the gradient of the QGD with respect to momentum, and that this conductivity can be tuned by varying the periodic potential strength and the layer-antisymmetric electric field.

**Method.** The study employs a semiclassical Boltzmann transport approach including inter-band Landau-Zener tunneling and numerical diagonalization of a tridiagonal Hamiltonian in an extended zone scheme.

**Model / system.** A 2D bilayer system (such as TMDs or graphene heterostructures) in a strong perpendicular magnetic field, subject to a unidirectional 1D periodic potential (superlattice).

**Key observables.** Counterflow conductivity tensor components ($\sigma_{xy}^{(CF)}$, $\sigma_{yx}^{(CF)}$, $\sigma_{xx}^{(CF)}$, $\sigma_{yy}^{(CF)}$) and the Quantum Geometric Dipole (QGD).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Periodic potential strength ($W$), layer-antisymmetric electric field ($E_-$), dielectric constant ($\kappa$), magnetic length ($\ell$), and layer separation ($d$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Strong magnetic field limit (lowest Landau level), high-temperature/dilute limit (ignoring BEC and exciton-exciton interactions), and neglect of electron/hole recombination.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows schematics of exciton counterflow and the QGD definition; Figure 2 illustrates the QGD vector structure and its dependence on potential strength; Figure 3 shows interband tunneling at avoided crossings; Figure 4 displays exciton band structures; Figure 6 shows conductivity dependence on $\kappa$ and $E_-$.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the QGD and its relation to exciton polarization, moves to the semiclassical equations of motion and the Boltzmann transport framework, analyzes the effects of periodic potentials and electric fields on conductivity, and concludes with an appendix deriving the explicit QGD-conductivity link.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Collective excitations of many-body electron systems can carry internal structure, supporting novel quantum geometric and topological properties. Among these are a quantum geometric dipole (QGD), which for excitons have direct significance as an internal polarization. For interlayer excitons of a bilayer system, this represents an in-plane dipole moment, which can be used to drive them with in-plane electric fields. In this work, we consider counterflow electric currents associated with driven excitons in such a bilayer system as a probe of their QGD structure. As a simple but non-trivial example, we analyze a structure with a one-dimensional periodic potential in a strong perpendicular magnetic field. The resulting magnetoexciton bands host QGD structure that distinguishes it from the exciton QGD of a uniform system. To model exciton transport we adopt a Boltzmann approach that includes inter-band tunneling, allowing us to consider non-equilibrium momentum distributions that result from strong layer-antisymmetric driving fields. We show how linear response to a layer-symmetric component of the driving fields provide information about the QGD, and that the broad QGD structure of the exciton bands can be probed by the varying the layer-antisymmetric field. Our results demonstrate that counterflow conductivity serves as a tunable probe of the internal quantum geometric structure carried by the interlayer excitons, connecting transport to the quantum geometry of many-body excitations.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how electrical current can be used to actively engineer the band structure of a magnonic crystal. By applying inhomogeneous spin-orbit torque, the researchers show they can induce mode hybridization and create tunable band gaps. This provides a pathway for developing programmable, reconfigurable magnonic and spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the need for active, electrical control over spin-wave propagation and band structures in magnonic crystals, moving beyond passive systems.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that inhomogeneous current-induced spin-orbit torque can induce band hybridization, create tunable hybridization gaps, and enable the reconfiguration of magnonic states through mode conversion.

**Method.** The study uses the Plane Wave Method (PWM) to solve the linearized Landau-Lifshitz-Gilbert (LLG) equation in the reciprocal lattice, applying perturbation theory to model the periodic spin-torque term.

**Model / system.** A 2D bicomponent magnonic crystal consisting of a Permalloy/heavy metal bilayer nanopatterned with a 2D array of Cobalt nanodots, modeled using the LLG equation with a field-like torque term.

**Key observables.** Spin-wave band structures, magnonic eigenmodes, hybridization gaps, avoided crossings (anticrossing), and mode profiles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Current density (J), spin-torque scaling factor, saturation magnetization (Ms), lattice constant (a), and torque efficiencies (c_||, c_perp).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglected Gilbert damping and damping-like torque; assumed uniform static magnetization; used a linearized approximation for spin-wave dynamics; neglected magnetocrystalline anisotropy.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the crystal structure and Brillouin Zone; Fig 2 compares dispersion with and without spin torque; Fig 3 shows eigenmode profiles at the Gamma point; Fig 4 shows the evolution of the anticrossing gap; Fig 5 and 6 show the evolution of modes through hybridization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of active magnonic control, describes the bicomponent crystal system and the LLG-based mathematical model, presents numerical results using the Plane Wave Method regarding band engineering and mode hybridization, and concludes with implications for reconfigurable spintronic devices.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin wave propagation and dynamic control are essential for reconfigurable magnonic and spintronic devices. Here, tunable mode coupling and band hybridisation are demonstrated in a nanopatterned bicomponent magnonic crystal consisting of a Permalloy/heavy metal bilayer patterned with a two dimensional array of Co nanodots. Using the plane wave method and the linearised Landau Lifshitz equation with a field like torque term, we show that inhomogeneous current induced spin torque produces periodic modulation of the magnonic frequency, enabling dynamic control of spin-wave dispersion. A pronounced avoided crossing between localised and propagating Damon Eshbach modes is observed, leading to tunable hybridisation gaps, band deformation, and enhanced mode mixing. The spin torque induced modulation enables controlled mode conversion and reconfigurable hybrid magnonic states, demonstrating efficient electrical tuning of nanoscale spin-wave dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22452"></a>
### [Transport Enhancement and In Situ Control of Electronic Correlation via Photoinduced Modulation Doping of van der Waals Heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22452v1)

**Authors:** Collin R. Sanborn, Son T. Le, Thuc T. Mai, Maria F. Munoz, Riccardo Torsi, Angela R. Hight Walker, Curt A. Richter, Samuel W. LaGasse, Aubrey T. Hanbicki, Adam L. Friedman

**Summary.** The researchers developed a way to use light to precisely control the number of electrons and the amount of disorder in graphene devices. This technique allows them to switch between different transport regimes and reveal complex quantum Hall states that were previously hidden by disorder. This provides a new, accessible tool for engineering high-quality 2D materials for studying correlated physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper demonstrates a method to deterministically tune the strength of Coulomb interactions and disorder in a 2D system, which is highly relevant for studying many-body dynamics and the stability of correlated phases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for precise, non-destructive, and in situ control over carrier concentration and electronic disorder in 2D van der Waals heterostructures to study fragile correlated states.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that photoinduced modulation doping can reversibly tune carrier density and mobility, enabling the transition from diffusive to quasi-ballistic transport and the observation of quantum Hall isospin ferromagnetic states.

**Method.** Using standard white light sources to induce charge trapping at the hBN-SiO2 interface (photodoping) and prolonged illumination to reduce disorder (photoannealing).

**Model / system.** hBN-encapsulated graphene Hall bar heterostructures on a SiO2 backgate, analyzed using Drude and Landauer transport models.

**Key observables.** Carrier mobility, carrier density, residual carrier density, scattering length, and Landau level degeneracy lifting.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transition between diffusive and quasi-ballistic transport regimes; tuning of mobility between 200,000 and 300,000 cm2/V-s; magnetic fields up to 9 T.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scattering model assumes 2D Coulomb disorder located at a distance of more than a few nm from the graphene channel.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Schematic of the device and photodoping mechanism. Fig 2: Transfer curves showing CNP shifts, mobility extraction, and reversible tuning of transport regimes. Fig 3: Quantum Hall measurements showing the emergence of split plateaus (degeneracy lifting) after photoannealing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the photodoping technique, demonstrates its ability to control carrier density and reduce disorder via photoannealing, quantifies the transport improvements using Drude and Landauer models, and concludes by showing the emergence of many-body correlated states in the high-quality regime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Modulation doping, a well-established technique for traditional semiconductor heterostructures, is a promising approach for tailoring carrier concentration in 2D materials devices. In this letter we report on photoinduced modulation doping in hBN-graphene-hBN-SiO2 heterostructures utilizing standard white light sources and no additional fabrication complexity. We establish the use of this technique to both dope the channel material and to photoanneal devices, providing control over electronic doping and disorder in the graphene channel. We analyze the transport properties by employing Drude and Landauer transport models, highlighting the ability to reversibly tune the mobility and mean scattering length of the graphene with a high degree of accuracy. This tunability allows us to switch our device between the diffusive and quasi-ballistic transport regimes in situ. We utilize the exceptional control our technique provides over local disorder to realize quantum Hall isospin ferromagnetic states in a device whose initial quality would otherwise leave such states unobservable. These results demonstrate precise manipulation of carrier density and charge disorder in van der Waals heterostructures, providing a highly accessible approach to creating high-quality devices capable of realizing correlated electronic states.

</details>
