### [Antiferromagnetic Ordering Enhanced Magnetic Damping in Mn2Au/CoFeB Bilayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22382v1)

**Authors:** Donghang Xie, Haozhe Wang, Zhe Zhang, Zishuang Li, Jiahua Lu, Ronghua Liu, Jun Du, Bo Liu, Yu Yan, Liang He, Jing Wu, Rong Zhang, Bo Liu, Tiejun Zhou, Yongbing Xu, Xuezhong Ruan  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22382v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22382_figures/2605.22382_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) In-plane magnetic hysteresis loop of Mn2Au(2)/CoFeB at RT. The saturation magnetization 𝑀𝑀𝑆𝑆 is 1140 emu/cc. (b) Temperature-dependent exchange bias field 𝐻𝐻𝐸𝐸𝐸𝐸 and increment of coercivity Δ𝐻𝐻𝐶𝐶.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22382_figures/2605.22382_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. (a) Schematic illustration of the FMR setup. (b) FMR signals at 11 GHz 𝐻𝐻𝑟𝑟𝑟𝑟 for</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22382_figures/2605.22382_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) Temperature-dependent magnetic damping 𝛼𝛼 of Mn2Au(2)/CoFeB,</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22382_figures/2605.22382_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Schematic illustration of the physical mechanism of spin angular momentum transfer and dissipation in the Mn2Au(2)/CoFeB bilayer. Below 160 K, AFM order (represented by paired blue arrows in the Mn2Au layer) and AFM-FM exchange coupling (depicted as the spring between Mn2Au and CoFeB) are established and progressively enhanced with decreasing temperature. This leads to a synchronized increase of 𝐻𝐻𝑟𝑟𝑟𝑟𝑟𝑟 and efficient spin angular momentum transfer (illustrated by the gray span with the dashed red arrows moving from CoFeB into Mn2Au) and dissipation (illustrated by the randomly arranged dashed red arrows in Mn2Au), thereby enhancing the magnetic damping 𝛼𝛼.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The study investigates how the onset of antiferromagnetic ordering in ultrathin Mn2Au layers enhances the magnetic damping of an adjacent CoFeB layer. By ruling out spin pumping and two-magnon scattering, the authors demonstrate that the effect is driven by exchange-mediated spin angular momentum transfer. This provides critical insights for developing next-generation antiferromagnetic spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism behind the dramatic increase in magnetic damping and the emergence of rotatable anisotropy in Mn2Au/CoFeB bilayers as temperature decreases.

**Main result.** The increase in magnetic damping is attributed to enhanced spin angular momentum transfer from CoFeB to Mn2Au, mediated by AFM-FM exchange coupling that strengthens with the development of AFM ordering.

**Method.** Ferromagnetic Resonance (FMR) spectroscopy, SQUID magnetometry, and temperature-dependent analysis of magnetic properties.

**Model / system.** Mn2Au/CoFeB bilayers, specifically 2-nm and 4-nm thick Mn2Au layers on 4-nm CoFeB, studied across a temperature range from 10 K to 300 K.

**Key observables.** Magnetic damping (alpha), rotatable anisotropy field (Hrot), exchange bias field (Heb), Neel temperature (Tn), and saturation magnetization (Ms).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (10 K to 300 K), Mn2Au thickness (2 nm and 4 nm), and the critical temperature threshold (around 160 K for 2 nm films).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows hysteresis loops and temperature dependence of exchange bias; Fig 2 presents the FMR setup and frequency-dependent resonance signals; Fig 4 provides a schematic of the spin angular momentum transfer mechanism.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of AFM/FM dynamics, describes the experimental fabrication and characterization of Mn2Au/CoFeB, presents temperature-dependent FMR and SQUID results, rules out alternative mechanisms like spin pumping and two-magnon scattering, and concludes with a proposed physical mechanism for spin transfer.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Antiferromagnets (AFMs) hold significant potential for spintronic devices owing to their insensitivity to external magnetic fields and the absence of stray fields. Beyond these inherent advantages, an AFM can manipulate the magnetic dynamics of a ferromagnet (FM) layer in AFM/FM bilayers, whereas the mechanism of such manipulation remains controversial. Here, we investigate the magnetic dynamics of AFM/FM Mn2Au/CoFeB bilayers via Ferromagnetic Resonance (FMR). It is found that the Néel temperature of 2-nm-thick Mn2Au is as low as ~40 K, in sharp contrast to that of bulk Mn2Au, which exceeds 1000 K. In the Mn2Au(2 nm)/CoFeB(4 nm) bilayer, the magnetic damping $α$ of the CoFeB layer increases from 0.013 to 0.047 as temperature decreases from 160 K to 10 K, accompanied by a synchronous increase in the exchange coupling field H_rot. Such an increase in $α$ is attributed to the enhanced spin angular momentum transfer from CoFeB to Mn2Au, mediated through AFM-FM exchange coupling between Mn2Au and CoFeB, which is enhanced by the Mn2Au antiferromagnetic ordering as the temperature decreases. Our study provides deeper insights into AFM/FM dynamics and spintronic storage technology.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.21577"></a>
### [Chiral Electromagnetic Surface Waves on Chern-Simons Interfaces](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21577v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that a topological Chern-Simons interface can intrinsically support chiral electromagnetic surface waves even in a pure vacuum. It shows that the interface acts as a frequency-independent waveguide for a single helicity of light, driven by topology rather than material properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether a Chern-Simons interface between two regions of vacuum electrodynamics can support localized, chiral electromagnetic surface waves without the need for external material response or geometric confinement.

**Main result.** The study finds that the Chern-Simons interaction acts as an attractive delta-function potential for one photon helicity, creating a unique, gapless, and chiral surface photon with a frequency-independent index of refraction.

**Method.** The author solves the exact boundary value problem for Maxwell's equations by using a 2D helicity eigenbasis and applying Gaussian pillbox integration to derive matching conditions at the interface.

**Model / system.** The system is a 4D Maxwell theory featuring a codimension-1 (planar) interface where a pseudo-scalar theta field undergoes a discrete jump (delta-function potential).

**Key observables.** Photon helicity, dispersion relation, effective refractive index, and the time-averaged Poynting vector.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Chern-Simons jump (delta-theta), frequency (omega), momentum parallel to the interface (k_parallel), and the UV cutoff scale.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The background theta field is treated as a fixed, non-dynamical background, the interface is assumed to be planar, and no external charges or currents are present.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the Chern-Simons interface model, proceeds to derive the modified Maxwell boundary conditions, solves the boundary value problem to identify the localized mode, analyzes the dispersion relation and energy transport, and concludes with a discussion on the UV limits of the theory.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that Maxwell theory with a codimension-$1$ Chern-Simons interface supports chiral electromagnetic surface waves on the interface, even when the bulk theory on both sides is conventional vacuum electrodynamics in infinite space. Solving the exact boundary value problem we find that the Chern-Simons interaction acts with opposite sign on the two helicities oriented along the interface, giving rise to one normalizable mode localized on the interface. This mode is a gapless chiral surface photon with linear dispersion and a frequency-independent index of refraction set by the Chern-Simons coefficient. This mode exists despite the absence of ambient material response or geometric confinement.

</details>

### [Direct observation of the transverse near field of an edge excitation and associated slow secondary dynamics in a fractional quantum Hall state](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21989v1)

**Summary.** This paper uses time-resolved microscopy to observe the electromagnetic field of an edge excitation in a fractional quantum Hall state. It demonstrates that these excitations are not purely 1D but include a spatially extended, quasi-electrostatic near-field that reaches deep into the bulk. This reveals that edge-driven dynamics can trigger complex, multi-scale responses in the adjacent electronic fluid.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To determine the spatial and dynamical structure of edge excitations in fractional quantum Hall states, specifically whether edge magnetoplasmons are strictly 1D charge-density oscillations or involve spatially extended electromagnetic fields.

**Main result.** The study reveals that edge magnetoplasmons possess a long-range, nearly instantaneous transverse near-field extending over 30 micrometers into the bulk, alongside slower secondary bulk-side dynamics.

**Method.** Stroboscopic time-resolved photoluminescence (PL) microscopy and spectroscopy using a Ti:sapphire laser synchronized with electrically launched voltage pulses.

**Model / system.** A nu=1/3 fractional quantum Hall state in a 15-nm GaAs/AlGaAs quantum well at high magnetic field (14 T) and ultra-low temperatures (40-50 mK).

**Key observables.** Trion photoluminescence spectra (singlet and triplet peaks), triplet PL intensity maps, peak energy shifts, and amplitude ratios.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Filling factor nu=1/3, magnetic field 14 T, temperature 40-50 mK, voltage pulse amplitude +/- 50 to 100 mV, and repetition periods of 13 ns and 52 ns.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The linewidth parameter is assumed to be common to both singlet and triplet trion components to stabilize the spectral fitting.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental schematic; Fig 2 displays PL spectra snapshots showing downstream spectral changes; Fig 3 shows spatial maps of PL intensity and reflectance; Fig 4 presents color maps of triplet peak energy shifts and amplitude ratios as functions of position and time.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the experimental setup and device geometry, presents the time-resolved spectroscopic results showing the transverse near-field, analyzes the temporal evolution and secondary dynamics, and concludes by discussing the implications for the nature of edge excitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report stroboscopic time-resolved photoluminescence (PL) microscopy and spectroscopy revealing the transverse near field of an edge excitation in a $ν=1/3$ fractional quantum Hall (FQH) state. Time-resolved $y$-$t$ maps reveal an immediate PL response extending more than $30~μ\mathrm{m}$ into the bulk transverse to the edge when the edge magnetoplasmon (EMP) passes the mesa boundary. The nearly instantaneous nature of this long-range response identifies it as the non-radiative, quasi-electrostatic near field, revealing the EMP as a spatially extended collective excitation rather than a strictly one-dimensional charge-density oscillation. We also observe secondary bulk-side responses distinct from the immediate transverse near-field response. The coexistence of these immediate and secondary responses shows that electrically launched edge excitations produce bulk-side dynamics on widely separated time scales.

</details>

### [Enrichment of rhombohedral stacking by mechanical exfoliation of graphite](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22282v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that a simple blade-assisted exfoliation technique can significantly increase the amount of rhombohedral (ABC) stacking in graphite. By using mechanical shear and bending, the researchers can create samples with much higher ABC content, providing a new way to study the correlated and topological phases associated with ABC-stacked flat bands.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The scarcity of rhombohedral (ABC) stacking in natural graphite limits the experimental study of its surface-localized flat bands and associated correlated and topological electronic phases.

**Main result.** Mechanical exfoliation and a novel blade-assisted exfoliation step significantly enrich the ABC stacking content in graphite, with thin flakes reaching up to 75% ABC area fraction.

**Method.** The study uses conventional and blade-assisted mechanical exfoliation (a tape-graphite-tape sandwich passed over a blade) combined with large-area Raman 2D-band skewness mapping and uniaxial strain application.

**Model / system.** The physical system is graphite, specifically focusing on the transition between Bernal (AB) and rhombohedral (ABC) stacking phases in thick flakes and few-layer graphene (FLG).

**Key observables.** 2D-band Raman skewness (gamma), ABC area fraction, Raman intensity, and wrinkle angles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Layer number (3 to 22 layers), ABC area fraction, and applied uniaxial strain.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that 2D-band skewness is a robust descriptor for stacking types that is resistant to strain-induced peak shifts.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows schematics of AB/ABC stacking and the blade-assisted method; Fig 2 shows Raman 2D-band skewness as a function of layer number; Fig 3 compares Raman maps and skewness distributions across different preparation methods; Fig 4 shows optical and Raman maps of wrinkles; Fig 5 shows Raman maps of a 14-layer flake under varying strain.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of ABC scarcity, describes the new blade-assisted exfoliation method, presents Raman-based quantification of stacking enrichment, details the mechanism of domain wall formation via wrinkles and shear, and demonstrates domain dynamics under strain.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Rhombohedral (ABC) graphite hosts a surface-localized flat band that supports correlated and topological electronic phases, but its experimental study is limited by the scarcity of ABC stacking in natural graphite, which is dominated by Bernal (AB) stacking. Here we show that the routine mechanical exfoliation step itself enriches the rhombohedral content of graphite flakes, and that a simple blade-assisted exfoliation step, which introduces additional shear, amplifies the effect further. Using large-area Raman 2D-band skewness mapping we measure ABC content at area fractions of 3\% in the pristine source crystal, 16\% after conventional exfoliation, and 26\% after blade-assisted exfoliation for thick flakes. In thin flakes ($<20$ layers) the per-flake area fraction reaches 75\% in the upper tail of the distribution. Tracking individual flakes before and after blade-assisted exfoliation shows that wrinkles seed AB-ABC domain walls, and uniaxial strain can move these walls. Blade-assisted mechanical exfoliation therefore removes one of the bottlenecks to the preparation of ABC-rich graphite samples for studies of correlated and topological phases in rhombohedral graphite.

</details>

**Summary.** The researchers developed a way to transfer ultra-thin high-temperature superconducting YBCO films onto silicon substrates using a nanomembrane technique. This process preserves the material's high-quality superconductivity and creates a thermal barrier that could improve the performance of single-photon detectors.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for a scalable fabrication method to integrate high-Tc superconductors like YBCO with standard silicon-based photonic architectures for applications like superconducting nanowire single-photon detectors.

**Main result.** Successful fabrication and transfer of ultra-thin YBCO films on STO nanomembranes to SiO2/Si substrates, maintaining high crystallinity and robust superconducting properties (Tc = 88.8 K) while significantly reducing thermal boundary conductance.

**Method.** High-pressure sputtering of YBCO/Au on an STO/SCAO bilayer, followed by the dissolution of the water-soluble SCAO sacrificial layer and a controlled transfer process using a pre-conditioned DGL polymer film.

**Model / system.** An ultra-thin (20 nm) YBa2Cu3O7-x (YBCO) film on a SrTiO3 (STO) nanomembrane, transferred onto a SiO2/Si substrate, forming a van der Waals interface.

**Key observables.** Critical temperature (Tc), critical current density (Jc), thermal boundary conductance (TBC), and X-ray diffraction (XRD) lattice constants.

**Important parameters / regimes.** YBCO thickness ~20 nm, Tc = 88.8 K, Jc = 6.8 MA/cm2 at 77 K, and TBC reduction of nearly two orders of magnitude.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reduction in thermal boundary conductance is assumed to be the primary driver for enhanced stability of transient resistive states in the nanowires.

**Figures summary.** XRD scans of STO and the full YBCO/STO stack, AFM images of the surface, and the experimental procedure for the DGL-mediated transfer process.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for high-Tc SNSPDs, describes the fabrication and transfer protocol, presents structural and electrical characterization of the transferred films, analyzes the thermal boundary conductance, and concludes with the implications for photonic integration.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The fabrication of free-standing ultra-thin films from high-temperature (high-Tc) superconductors is of great interest for the development of superconducting nanowire single-photon detectors with high operating temperatures. We successfully fabricate a millimeter-sized, high-quality, ultra-thin YBa$2$Cu$3$O$_{7-x}$ (YBCO) film on an SrTiO$_3$/Sr$_{1.5}$Ca$_{1.5}$Al$_2$O$_6$ (STO/SCAO) bilayer using high-pressure sputtering. The STO nanomembranes with the YBCO films are released by dissolving the water-soluble SCAO sacrificial layer and transferred onto the SiO2/Si substrate. X-ray diffraction confirms that STO and YBCO crystallinity is preserved following transfer onto SiO2/Si substrates. Microbridges patterned from the transferred YBCO films exhibit a critical temperature of 88.8 K and a critical current density of 6.8 MA/cm2 at 77 K, demonstrating robust superconducting transport after transfer. The thermal boundary conductance across the van der Waals interface between the STO nanomembrane with YBCO film and the SiO2/Si substrate, measured over 15-75 K, is significantly reduced compared to that of epitaxial YBCO film on bulk STO leading to modified energy relaxation and enhanced stability of transient resistive states. These results establish ultra-thin YBCO films on optically transparent STO nanomembranes as a platform for integrating high-Tc superconducting devices with SiO2/Si-based photonic structures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22370"></a>
### [Generation of an anomalous linearly dispersing spin-polarized band in Bi-based topological insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22370v1)

**Authors:** Matthias Kronseder, Thomas Mayer, Jan Minár, Magdalena Marganska, Hedwig Werner, Florian Schmid, Rebeca Diaz-Pardo, Ivana Vobornik, Jun Fuji, Cornelia Streeck, Alexander Gottwald, Hendrik Kaser, Bernd Kästner, Christian H. Back

For all plots the onset of the Dirac cone is highlighted by a dashed cone with the same size in all plots. **Summary.** This paper reports the experimental generation of a new, highly energetic, and spin-polarized electronic band in Bi-based topological insulators using ion bombardment and annealing. This 'anomalous' state is unique because it possesses a spin helicity opposite to the standard topological surface state and spans an unusually large energy range. The study provides a detailed investigation into the possible physical origins of this state, such as surface reconstruction or chemical changes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The emergence of an unexpected, highly energetic, and spin-polarized electronic band (the ALS) in Bi-based topological insulators following surface treatment via ion bombardment and annealing.

**Main result.** The discovery of an 'anomaline linearly dispersing state' (ALS) that coexists with the regular topological surface state, possesses an opposite spin helicity, and spans a large energy range of ~650 meV with a Fermi velocity nearly identical to the regular TSS.

**Method.** The researchers used a 'sputter-heating cycle' (Ar-ion bombardment followed by annealing) on BSTS/Bi2Se3 heterostructures and characterized the electronic and structural changes using Spin-resolved ARPES, XPS, and AFM.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of (Bi1-xSbx)2(Te1-ySey)3 (BSTS) / Bi2Se3 heterostructures grown on SrTiO3(111) substrates, subjected to surface modification via soft Ar-ion sputtering and annealing.

**Key observables.** Electronic band dispersion, spin-momentum locking (helicity), Fermi velocity (vF), energy range of the ALS, surface roughness, and chemical stoichiometry.

**Important parameters / regimes.** ALS energy range (~650 meV), Fermi velocity (approx. 5.1-5.3 x 10^5 m/s), and sputtering voltage (250-500 V).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The authors evaluate several models (Rashba 2DEG, TSS relocation, Bi-bilayer formation, etc.) but note that no single current model fully accounts for all observed properties.

**Figures summary.** Figure S1 shows the evolution of ARPES scans through different treatment stages: thermal decapping, sputtering (blurred scans), and annealing (improved contrast with the emergence of the ALS feature).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of defect-induced states in TIs, describes the experimental generation of the ALS via surface treatment, presents the characterization of the new band's dispersion and spin properties, discusses the surface morphology changes, and evaluates various physical mechanisms for the origin of the ALS.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report the generation of an anomalous linearly dispersing, spin-polarized band in Bi-based topological insulator (TI) thin films, induced by soft Ar-ion bombardment followed by annealing. This extra band -- which we call the anomalous linearly dispersing state (ALS) -- is superimposed on the regular band structure including the topological surface state (TSS), spans an unusually large energetic range of up to ${\sim}\,\SI{650}{\milli\electronvolt}$ at the $Γ$-point, and appears near the Fermi energy. Spin-resolved measurements indicate spin-momentum locking with a helicity \emph{opposite} to that of the regular TSS. The Fermi velocity of the ALS, $v_\mathrm{F} = (5.1\pm 0.4)\times 10^{5}\,\frac m s$, is indistinguishable from that of the regular TSS, $(5.3\pm 0.5)\times 10^{5}\,\frac m s$. The observation is reproducible across samples of varying thickness and was confirmed at two independent synchrotron radiation facilities. We discuss different mechanisms for the physical origin of the observed ALS including sputtering-induced TSS relocation, bi-layer formation by,e.g., chalcogen removal, and high-index surface relocation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22167"></a>
### [High-throughput study of electrical conductivity in ordered metals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22167v1)

**Authors:** Thalis H. B. da Silva, Hai-Chen Wang, Tiago F. T. Cerqueira, Simone Di Cataldo, Silvana Botti  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22167v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** Researchers developed a high-throughput framework combining machine learning with first-principles calculations to screen 2.8 million metallic compounds for high electrical conductivity. They identified new intermetallic candidates that could rival aluminum's conductivity by using light elements to reduce electron-phonon scattering. The study demonstrates that ML can effectively bridge the gap between massive chemical searches and expensive, high-accuracy quantum mechanical simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of efficiently predicting and optimizing electrical conductivity in metals across a massive chemical space of millions of compounds.

**Main result.** The study identifies several intermetallic candidates, such as LiBePt2, with predicted conductivities comparable to aluminum, and demonstrates that machine learning can accurately screen 2.8 million compounds.

**Method.** A multi-stage high-throughput workflow integrating machine learning surrogate models (ALIGNN) with ab initio calculations (DFT, BoltzTraP2, and EPW) to predict conductivity and electron-phonon coupling.

**Model / system.** The study screens approximately 2.8 million ordered inorganic metallic compounds from the Alexandria database, focusing on the interplay between electronic structure and electron-phonon scattering.

**Key observables.** Electrical conductivity (sigma), electron-phonon coupling constant (lambda), Fermi velocity (vF), and carrier density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Distance to the convex hull (E_hull) <= 25 meV/atom for stability; conductivity threshold > 15 x 10^6 S/m.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial large-scale screening uses the constant relaxation-time approximation, which neglects material-specific scattering variations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the computational workflow; Figure 2 illustrates the distribution of predicted conductivity vs. electron-phonon coupling; Figure 3 validates ML predictions against DFT; Figure 4 shows the fit of a derived empirical formula to experimental data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational challenge, describes the multi-stage ML and ab initio workflow, presents the screening results and physical insights into high-conductivity mechanisms, and validates the models against experimental benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a computational framework that integrates machine learning with high-throughput \textit{ab initio} calculations to screen over 2.8 million compounds for metallic transport. We identify several intermetallic candidates with predicted high conductivities comparable to that of aluminum ($36.59 \times 10^6$~S/m). We perform full electron--phonon coupling calculations for the top-performing materials, yielding results in excellent agreement with available experimental data. Our analysis reveals that while the noble metals (Ag, Au, Cu) define the practical ceiling for conductivity due to their unique electronic structure and low scattering, compounds like $\text{LiBePt}_2$ can achieve comparable performance by utilizing valence electrons from light elements to shift high-scattering $d$-states beneath the Fermi level. This study not only identifies novel high-performance conductors but also demonstrates the predictive power of combining statistical learning with detailed ab initio calculations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [On the Riemann problem for the Adlam-Allen model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22610v1)

**Authors:** Su Yang, Marco Calabrese, Vassilis Koukouloyannis, Panayotis G. Kevrekidis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22610v1>

**Summary.** This paper provides a systematic toolkit for analyzing shock wave dynamics in the Adlam-Allen model, a fundamental model for plasma physics. By using Whitham modulation theory and KdV reduction, the authors successfully predict the complex structures of dispersive shock waves and rarefaction waves. The results show that even simple asymptotic reductions can accurately capture the behavior of more complex nonlinear plasma waves.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the formation and structure of rarefaction and dispersive shock waves (DSWs) in the Adlam-Allen model, specifically addressing the Riemann problem with jump initial data.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the DSW-fitting method and the KdV reduction provide highly accurate theoretical predictions for the edge features and amplitudes of shock waves in the Adlam-Allen model.

**Method.** The research employs Whitham modulation theory, the DSW-fitting method, and a KdV asymptotic reduction, validated against numerical simulations using RK4 and pseudospectral methods.

**Model / system.** The Adlam-Allen model is a system of nonlinear partial differential equations describing hydromagnetic waves in cold, collisionless, magnetospheric plasmas.

**Key observables.** Dispersive shock wave (DSW) amplitudes, edge speeds (solitonic and linear), wavenumber, and rarefaction wave profiles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The non-dissipative regime, the dispersionless limit, the small-amplitude/long-wavelength (KdV) limit, and the carrier density/magnetic field constants (R0, B0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The KdV reduction is an approximation valid primarily for small-amplitude jumps, and the analysis focuses on the non-dissipative, one-dimensional case.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows permitted parameter regions for traveling waves; Figure 2 shows space-time evolution of a DSW; Figure 3 shows rarefaction wave profiles; Figure 4 and 5 compare theoretical predictions for DSW edges against numerical data; Figure 6 compares the AA model directly to the KdV reduction.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the definition of the Adlam-Allen model and its physical context, followed by the derivation of traveling wave solutions and modulation equations, then moves to the analysis of the dispersionless limit and the DSW-fitting method, and concludes with a comparison between the full model and its KdV reduction via numerical validation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the present work, we revisit the Adlam-Allen (AA) model in order to investigate its numerically observed rarefaction and dispersive shock waves that arise in numerical simulations of the Riemann problem associated with the model. On the one hand, we perform a direct analysis of the rarefaction and dispersive shock waves of the AA model via examining its corresponding dispersionless system and leveraging the DSW-fitting method to obtain theoretical predictions on various edge features of the dispersive shock waves. On the other hand, we review the KdV reduction of the AA model and utilize the KdV dispersive shock wave to approximate that of the AA model. Relevant numerical comparisons demonstrate the good performance of not only the direct analysis on the AA dispersive shock wave, but also of the approximation via the KdV DSW. These methodologies provide a systematic toolbox for analyzing the outcome of Riemann problems in not only this fundamental setting of cold plasmas but also potentially in related plasma-physics problems.

</details>

### [Quasiparticle GW for Superconductors: Toward a Unified Treatment of Electron-Phonon and Electron-Plasmon Couplings](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21700v1)

**Authors:** Catalin D. Spataru, Christopher Renskers, Elena R. Margine  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.21700v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3. a) Superconducting properties of doped monolayer graphene calculated at T = 3 K within s-GW, as a function of the energy cutoff along the Matsubara imaginary-frequency axis. b) Anoma- lous (pairing) self-energy and spectral function (solid red line) for an electron with energy EQP nk ≈ 0.1 eV above the Fermi level in doped mono- layer graphene calculated within the s-GW at T = 3 K. </details>

</details>

**Summary.** The authors present a new theoretical framework, s-qpGW, that allows for a unified treatment of electron-phonon and electron-plasmon couplings in superconductors. This method fixes the issue of 'spurious' superconductivity predictions in 2D materials found in previous GW approaches. It provides a parameter-free way to study how dynamical screening by plasmons influences the superconducting state.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in accurately disentangling phonon-mediated and plasmon-mediated pairing mechanisms in 2D superconductors, and the failure of standard GW methods to avoid spurious superconductivity predictions.

**Main result.** The development of the s-qpGW framework, which correctly predicts the absence of superconductivity in doped monolayer graphene and provides a reliable way to capture dynamical Coulomb screening effects.

**Method.** An extension of the quasiparticle self-consistent GW (s-qpGW) method coupled with Eliashberg theory, using a QP-projection to suppress spurious pairing from high-energy plasmons.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on 2D materials (specifically doped monolayer and few-layer graphene, graphene superlattes, and rhombohedral trilayer graphene) and 3D bulk metals (Niobium).

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature (Tc), superconducting gap (Delta), anomalous self-energy (phi), and mass renormalization function (Z).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electron-phonon coupling strength (lambda), plasmon energy (omega_pl), Fermi energy (Ef), and the Coulomb pseudopotential (mu*).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Uses the Migdal approximation for electron-phonon coupling, the GW approximation for self-energy, and neglects vertex corrections and the incoherent part of the spectral weight.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the convergence of the superconducting gap in Nb with respect to Matsubara frequency cutoffs and the calculated superconducting gap in a 2D model system with enhanced density of states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific problem of pairing mechanisms in 2D, presents the new s-qpGW theoretical framework, benchmarks the method against bulk Nb and graphene, demonstrates the capture of dynamical screening, and discusses computational implementation via a plasmon-pole model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconducting two-dimensional materials, and in particular few-layer graphene, offer an exciting platform for low-power electronics, yet the origin of their unconventional superconductivity remains an open question. Prevailing theories, primarily rooted in the Bardeen-Cooper-Schrieffer (BCS) framework that assumes electron-phonon interactions are the main mechanism of superconductivity, struggle to account quantitatively for the observed phenomena. Recent studies point to a plasmonic pairing mechanism in graphene systems; however, disentangling the relative contributions of phonon- and plasmon-mediated pairing remains challenging due to the lack of a satisfactory first-principles framework capable of accurately capturing dynamical screening effects in the electronic channel. Here, we present a new theoretical framework that extends the quasiparticle self-consistent GW method to the superconducting phase by coupling it with the Eliashberg treatment of both phonon- and plasmon-mediated interactions. Our approach, termed "s-qpGW", is on par with the state-of-the-art Eliashberg theory of superconductivity when applied to bulk metals, and correctly predicts the absence of superconductivity in doped monolayer graphene. To differentiate s-qpGW from conventional Eliashberg approaches, we study a simple model system, graphene with an artificially enhanced density of states, and demonstrate that s-qpGW captures dynamical Coulomb screening effects in ways that standard BCS theory cannot.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Silicate cosmic dust grain collisions in the interstellar medium: A molecular dynamics study](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22225v1)

**Authors:** C. J. Esmerian, S. R. Hashemi, W. M. C. Sameera, W. Vlemmings, S. Andersson, T. J. L. C. Bakx, K. K. Knudsen, S. Aalto, G. Nyman

**Summary.** This paper uses molecular dynamics to show that interstellar silicate dust grains are more resistant to shattering during collisions than previously thought. By correcting an algebraic error in long-standing models, the authors provide updated velocity thresholds that suggest dust populations are more robust in the interstellar medium.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the physical outcomes (shattering, vaporization, or coagulation) of high-velocity collisions between nanoscale silicate dust grains in the interstellar medium and correcting errors in existing analytical threshold models.

**Main result.** The study finds that silicate grains are more resilient to shattering than previously assumed, with updated thresholds around 6 km/s compared to the canonical 2.7 km/s, and identifies that previous models fail to accurately predict product size distributions.

**Method.** The authors use atomistic Molecular Dynamics (MD) simulations with ReaxFF and GFN1-xTB force fields, alongside a re-derivation of shock jump conditions to correct algebraic errors in classical analytical frameworks.

**Model / system.** The system consists of collisions between nanoscale silicate grains (amorphous SiO2 and 'astrodust' Mg-Fe-Si-O) with radii between 5 and 50 Angstroms at velocities of 0.1 to 20 km/s.

**Key observables.** Shattering and vaporization threshold velocities, mass fractions of shattered/vaporized material, and grain size distributions of the resulting products.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Collision velocity (0.1-20 km/s), grain radii (5-50 A), material composition (SiO2 and ADSil), and impact parameter (set to head-on).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The simulations focus on the lowest decade of ISM grain sizes and neglect nuclear quantum effects and nonadiabatic excited-state processes due to computational costs.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the initial grain structures, mass fractions of disrupted/vaporized grains as a function of velocity, the evolution of grain shape and radius, and the cumulative size distributions of shattered products compared to power-law models.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of grain processing, describes the MD simulation setup and grain construction, presents results on mass fractions and size distributions, provides a mathematical correction to previous analytical shock models in an appendix, and concludes with the implications for ISM evolution.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

(abridged) We aim to predict the most important parameters for grain-grain collision outcomes for models of interstellar grain population evolution on astrophysical scales: the threshold velocity above which colliding grains shatter, the threshold for vaporization, and resulting distributions of grain sizes. We use molecular dynamics simulations which evolve the dynamics of each atom in a dust grain to explore the outcomes of collisions between silicate grains of radii $a \in [5,50]~Å$ at velocities $0.1-20$ km/s. We run simulations of grains with two materials: amorphous SiO$_2$ and an amorphous silicate of composition suggested by Draine \& Hensley (2021). With these simulations, we quantify the collision velocity dependence of shattered and vaporized mass fractions, and the resulting size distributions of shattering products. We find grain shattering thresholds are $\sim$6 km/s for both amorphous SiO$_2$ and astrodust material, which is a factor of $\sim$2 higher than the canonical value for silicates of 2.7 km/s from Jones et al. (1996). This discrepancy is mostly alleviated by correcting an error in the expression for these velocity thresholds derived in Tielens et al. (1994). We find that the size distributions of shattered products are generally not consistent with the power law distributions predicted by this previous model. We also find that their expression fails to predict the fraction of shattered or vaporized material observed in our numerical simulations. The model of Hirashita \& Kobayashi (2013) for the same quantities similarly fails to match the simulations. We provide updated shattering velocity thresholds for standard candidate grain materials to the astrophysics community. Broadly, our updated threshold velocities that astrophysical dust grains may be more robust to shattering in the interstellar medium than previously assumed.

</details>

### [Spintronic Neuromorphic Hardware Using Domain Wall Based Neurons and Quantized Synapses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22625v1)

**Authors:** Sakshi Kiran Bandekar, Arnab Ganguly, Debanjan Polley, Debasis Das

Inset shows the plot of the actual ReLU activation function for both positive as well as negative input values; (c) Two-dimensional color plot for mz on the x-y plane of the FL for Jc = 0.15 × 1012 and 0.40 × 1012 A/m2; 3D schematic of the proposed synapse device using symmetric (d) notch-type corrugation and (e) Plot of normalized conductance vs. the applied current density for the synapse using different...</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to build neuromorphic hardware using the controlled movement of magnetic domain walls in spintronic devices. By using physical notches to pin domain walls, the authors created quantized synaptic weights that can perform image recognition tasks. This approach offers a path toward low-power, high-density AI hardware that mimics biological neural plasticity.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing energy-efficient spintronic hardware to overcome the von Neumann bottleneck by implementing artificial neurons and quantized synapses using domain wall dynamics.

**Main result.** The researchers successfully emulated ReLU activation and quantized synaptic weights using notch-induced domain wall pinning, achieving high accuracy on MNIST and Fashion MNIST datasets after fine-tuning.

**Method.** Micromagnetic simulations were performed using OOMMF and UBERMAG, while the neural network architecture was implemented and trained using PyTorch.

**Model / system.** The system consists of heavy metal/ferromagnet (HM/FM) heterostructure nanotubes where domain wall motion is driven by spin-orbit torque (SOT). Neurons use rectangular tracks for ReLU-like activation, and synapses use tracks with semicircular notches to create discrete pinning sites.

**Key observables.** Electrical conductance, domain wall position, magnetization profiles (mz), and neural network inference accuracy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Current pulse durations of 3 ns (neuron) and 10 ns (synapse); FM layer thickness of 2 nm; DMI constant of 1-2 mJ/m2; current densities ranging from 0.1 to 0.81 x 10^12 A/m2.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The accuracy degradation is dependent on dataset complexity; the fine-tuning approach assumes that weight distributions in real hardware will fluctuate.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a 3D schematic of the neuron and conductance profiles; Figures 4 and 5 display the ANN architecture, weight distributions, and confusion matrices; Figure 6 shows misclassified images from Fashion MNIST.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the spintronic hardware problem, describes the physical model of the neuron and synapse, details the micromagnetic and neural network simulation methods, presents results for both FP32 and quantized weights, and concludes with a discussion on scalability and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we simulate the functionality of artificial neuron and synapse using spin-orbit torque-based spintronic devices and implemented a fully connected artificial neural netwrok (ANN). These neuro-synaptic devices are emulated using transverse domain wall dynamics in a rectangular magnetic nanotrack comprised of heavy metal/ferromagnet (HM/FM) heterostructures. The ReLU activation function of the neuron has been mimicked using the domain wall motion induced by a 3 ns current pulse. The synapse has been modelled using current-induced domain wall (DW) dynamics through a corrugated HM/FM nanotrack under the influence of a 10 ns current pulse with varying current density. The semicircular corrugations are in the form of notches, which are symmetrically located on both sides of the nanotrack. By applying 10 ns current pulses of varying densities, we achieve controlled DW pinning, revealing a step-like motion caused by temporary pauses at each pinning center. The electrical conductance of the pinned DW across various pinning points, act as stable synaptic weights for our ANN. Furthermore, we observe a threshold-dependent delay effect where each depinning event is influenced by previous ones, successfully mimicking synaptic memory and adaptability in neuromorphic systems. The fully connected ANN has been modeled using the conventional float32 synaptic weights for the MNIST and Fashion MNIST datasets with an accuracy of ~97 % and ~86 %, respectively, which serves as a test bed of our neuromorphic simulations. With the aim of implementing a sparse and low memory footprint ANN, we quantize the trained synaptic weights into discrete quantized level and tested the network, which demonstrate an accuracy of ~95% and ~62%, for the MNIST and Fashion-MNIST dataset, respectively.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22278"></a>
### [Topological semimetals: surface transport and spin effects](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22278v1)

**Authors:** E. V. Deviatov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22278v1>

<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic representation of the inversion of the bulk spectrum that occurs in some materials, such as Bi2Te3. In materials with band inversion, the hole band lies higher (in energy) than the electron one, precisely the opposite of the usual picture of the direct energy spectrum in a solid. Gen- erally speaking, there should be a line of intersection of the bands in momentum space (a nodal line), so the spectrum of the inverse system could be expected to be gapless (left dia- gram in the ﬁgure). However, the states on the nodal line are doubly degenerate in energy, and as it is usual in perturbation theory, any weak perturbation lifts the degeneracy and leads to repulsion of the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22278_figures/2605.22278_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Edge state symmetry. Chiral edge states of the in- teger quantum Hall eﬀect (QHE) [7] (left) and helical edge states of a two-dimensional topological insulator [11] (right). Each edge state of a topological insulator is a pair of QHE states propagating along the same edge of the sample in oppo- site directions [10] and, at the same time, spin-split due to the spin-momentum locking, as indicated by the diﬀerent colors of the arrows. From the point of view of the edge state symme- try, a topological Weyl semimetal (WSM) is a generalization of the Chern insulator (the quantum Hall eﬀect regime) to the three-dimensional case and zero external magnetic ﬁeld [2].</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22278_figures/2605.22278_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Formation of the bulk spectrum of a Weyl semimetal. Unlike a topological insulator (see Fig. 1), in the case of a topological semimetal, symmetry constraints imposed by the crystal lattice require the preservation of degeneracy at cer- tain points on the nodal line (marked in red), which are then called Dirac points. In a small neighborhood of the Dirac points, the spectrum is linear and spin-degenerate, i.e., a Dirac semimetal (for example, Cd3As2, NiTi2) is a three- dimensional analogue of graphene. For the Weyl spectrum, due to the violation of inversion symmetry P (e.g., in WTe2) or time reversal symmetry T (e.g., in Co3Sn2S2) in the crys- tal, each Dirac point, initially...</sub>

</details>

<sub>FIG. 5. Schematic diagram of Fermi arcs in the surface Brilliant zone and spin-polarized spin pockets in the bulk spectrum for the nonmagnetic Weyl semimetal WTe2. In this material, due to the tilt of the Weyl cones (type II semimetal), bulk electrons and holes are present, similar to classical semimetals, along with counter-directed Fermi arcs on the surface connecting the projections of the Weyl nodes. The splitting line of the Weyl nodes coincides with the b-axis of the crystal, so the preferred direction of carrier propagation in counter-directed chiral arcs coincides with the a-direction in highly anisotropic WTe2. Arrows indicate spin projections, which are deﬁned by the Weyl surface...</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.22278_figures/2605.22278_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Representation of a three-dimensional Weyl semimetal as a stack of two-dimensional planes in momen- tum space [2]. For planes corresponding to kx values between the Weyl nodes, the Berry curvature is non-zero (the eﬀective magnetic ﬁeld Fn = ∇k×An is directed from one Weyl node to another). They are characterized by a non-zero Chern num- ber C ̸= 0. For each of these topologically non-trivial planes, one (C = 1) or more (for C &gt; 1) chiral one-dimensional edge states should be introduced, as is done for the usual regime of the integer quantum Hall eﬀect in two-dimensional sys- tems. The sum of all one-dimensional chiral states forms a two-dimensional chiral surface state of the Fermi...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This review examines how the unique topological features of Weyl and Dirac semimetals, such as Fermi arcs and Berry curvature, manifest in electronic transport. It highlights how advanced experimental probes like the Josephson effect and nonlinear Hall measurements can isolate surface-state physics from bulk contributions. The findings suggest potential applications in high-frequency electronics and spintronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central challenge is distinguishing topologically protected surface state contributions from the gapless bulk spectrum in transport measurements and understanding the origin of chiral surface states in Weyl semimetals.

**Main result.** The review demonstrates that superconducting proximity effects, spin-polarized transport, and the nonlinear anomalous Hall effect serve as powerful diagnostic tools to identify topological surface states in materials like WTe2, Co3Sn2S2, and GeTe.

**Method.** The paper reviews experimental techniques including ARPES for visualizing Fermi arcs, SNS junction measurements for Josephson current, and second-harmonic voltage measurements to isolate the nonlinear Hall effect.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on topological semimetals, specifically Weyl semimetals (WSMs), Dirac semimetals, and nodal-line semimetals, including specific materials like WTe2, Co3Sn2S2, and Fe3GeTe2.

**Key observables.** Hall resistance (linear and nonlinear), magnetoresistance, critical current (Ic), voltage-current (V-I) characteristics, and magnetization hysteresis loops.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ranges (30 mK to room temperature), magnetic field dependence, spin-momentum locking, and Berry curvature.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The difficulty in distinguishing surface transport from trivial bulk effects due to the gapless bulk spectrum is a primary experimental limitation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate band inversion mechanisms, comparisons of chiral vs. helical edge states, the formation of Fermi arcs, the fabrication of contact patterns, and experimental V-I and Hall voltage characteristics.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining topological semimetals and their electronic structures to discussing specific transport phenomena including superconducting proximity effects, spin-dependent transport (spin valves), and the nonlinear anomalous Hall effect.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

For the solid state physics, recent interest to topological systems is mostly connected with topological semimetals, in particular, to Weyl ones as the most representative semimetal type. Like other topological materials, e.g. topological and Chern insulators, topological semimetals acquire topologically protected surface states with linear dispersion. In contrast to helical surface states in topological insulators, the surface states are chiral for Weyl semimetals, similarly to Chern insulators, which allows to consider Weyl semimetals as the three-dimensional analog of the quantum Hall effect regime. Weyl semimetals are also interesting for spin-dependent effects, due to the spin-momentum locking in the topological surface states. For topological semimetals, the main problem of transport investigations is to reveal the surface states contribution in the material with gapless bulk spectrum. Here, we present review of experimental results on charge and spin transport in topological semimetals: charge transport in different superconducting proximity devices; spin-dependent transport; magnetic response of the topological surface states; non-linear anomalous Hall effect as the direct manifestation of the non-zero Berry curvature in topological semimetals. Possible applications are also considered for this new class of topological materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Topologically Driven Giant Effective Spin Mixing Conductance in Antiferromagnetic FeSn/Py Heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22180v1)

**Authors:** Kacho Imtiyaz Ali Khan, Nidhi Kandwal, Pankhuri Gupta, Deeksha Khandelwal, Akash Kumar, Johan Åkerman, Pranaba Kishor Muduli

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the kagome-derived topological surface states of the antiferromagnet FeSn enable a giant spin mixing conductance and highly efficient spin-to-charge conversion. This performance significantly outperforms conventional platinum-based spintronic interfaces. These findings suggest that topological semimetals are promising candidates for next-generation, energy-efficient spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether the topologically active [001]-kagome surface states of the antiferromagnetic semimetal FeSn can significantly enhance spin transport and spin-to-charge conversion compared to standard heavy-metal/ferromagnet interfaces.

**Main result.** The researchers report a giant effective spin mixing conductance of (116 +/- 7) nm^-2 in FeSn/Py heterostructures, which is nearly an order of magnitude higher than standard Pt/Py systems, attributed to the topological kagome surface.

**Method.** The study uses magnetron sputtering for heterostructure growth, Ferromagnetic Resonance (FMR) to extract spin dynamics, and Inverse Spin Hall Effect (ISHE) measurements to quantify spin-to-charge conversion.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of epitaxial FeSn/Py (Ni80Fe20) heterostructures grown on Al2O3(0001) substrates, where FeSn is a kagome-lattice antiferromagnet featuring 3D Dirac fermions and 2D flat bands.

**Key observables.** Effective spin mixing conductance (g_eff_up_down), Gilbert damping (alpha), Inverse Spin Hall Effect voltage (V_ISHE), and spin-to-charge conversion efficiency (I_C).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fe:Sn stoichiometry (52:48 at.%), Py thickness (5-15 nm), Al spacer thickness (0.5-5 nm), and Neel temperature (~368 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the enhancement is interfacial, which is verified by the reduction in damping upon inserting an Al spacer layer.

**Figures summary.** Figure S1 shows the dependence of resonance frequency and linewidth on the resonance field and Py layer thickness for both FeSn/Py and reference Py stacks.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the topological properties of FeSn, describes the experimental growth and structural characterization (XRD, RBS, XRR), presents FMR and ISHE results to demonstrate giant spin mixing conductance, uses an Al spacer to prove the interfacial origin, and concludes with the implications for spintronics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The topological semimetal FeSn antiferromagnet, characterized by its kagome lattice, two-dimensional flat bands, and Dirac-like surface states, holds immense promise for spintronic applications. In this work, for the first time, we investigate the spin pumping behavior in epitaxial-FeSn/Py (Ni$_{80}$Fe$_{20}$) heterostructures. We report a giant effective spin mixing conductance (g$^{\uparrow \downarrow}_{\mathrm{eff}}$) of $(116\pm 7)$~nm$^{-2}$, which is nearly one order of magnitude higher than that of standard Pt/Py heterostructures. The insertion of a 3 nm Al spacer layer results in a two-fold reduction in the effective damping, confirming the interfacial origin of the large g$^{\uparrow\downarrow}_{\mathrm{eff}}$. Consistently, we observe an order-of-magnitude higher inverse spin Hall effect voltage in the FeSn/Py system compared to a reference Pt/Py film stack. We attribute the giant g$^{\uparrow\downarrow}_{\mathrm{eff}}$ to the direct interfacing of the Py layer with the topologically active [001]-kagome surface of epitaxial-FeSn. These findings establish the critical role of topologically active interfaces for advanced quantum-material-based spintronic devices.

</details>
